📘 Deep dive

1 Valid Anagram LC 242 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given two strings, decide whether t is a rearrangement of s (identical letter multisets).

💡 Key insight. Anagrams have identical character multisets. Tally s with +1 and t with -1 in a single 26-slot array; if every slot returns to zero, the multisets matched.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Length mismatch is an instant false.
  2. In one pass over both strings, do count[s[i]-'a']++ and count[t[i]-'a']--.
  3. If any slot is non-zero at the end, the letters didn't cancel — return false; otherwise true.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass over each string 💾 Space O(1) — fixed 26-element counter (lowercase only)

⚠️ Gotchas. Empty vs empty is a valid anagram. The 26-slot trick assumes lowercase ASCII — for full Unicode use a HashMap<Character,Integer> instead. Forgetting the length check lets unequal lengths slip through if you only test "every slot non-negative".
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force would sort both strings and compare in O(n log n). But the alphabet is fixed, so I can just count: increment for s, decrement for t in one shared array. If every count nets to zero the strings are anagrams — O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 ValidAnagram.java

2 Valid Parentheses LC 20 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a string of ()[]{}, decide whether every bracket is closed by the correct type in the correct order.

💡 Key insight. On every opener, push the closer we expect to see next; on every closer, it must match the most recent expectation (LIFO). Pushing the expected closer (rather than the opener) makes the match a single equality check.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For (/[/{, push )/]/} respectively.
  2. For a closer, if the stack is empty or its top ≠ this char, return false.
  3. After the scan, the stack must be empty — leftover openers mean it's unbalanced.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each char pushed/popped at most once 💾 Space O(n) — stack holds up to n/2 pending closers

⚠️ Gotchas. A closer with an empty stack ("nothing to close") is the easy-to-miss case, e.g. ")(". The end-of-string emptiness check catches the opposite, unclosed openers like "(". Use ArrayDeque, not the legacy synchronized Stack.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brackets nest, so the last opener must close first — that's a stack. I push the closer I expect, and each closing char just has to equal the top. Empty stack on a closer, or a non-empty stack at the end, both mean invalid. Linear time, linear space."
📄 ValidParentheses.java

3 Longest Common Prefix LC 14 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Find the longest string that is a prefix of every string in the array (empty if none).

💡 Key insight. Vertical scanning: compare character column 0 across all strings, then column 1, and so on. The moment a string is too short or a character disagrees, the prefix is exactly what we've matched so far. The first string bounds the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Guard the null/empty array → return "".
  2. Walk col over strs[0]; for each column, check every other string at that index.
  3. If a string ends at col or its char differs, return strs[0].substring(0, col).
  4. If we exhaust the first string, it is itself the common prefix.

⏱️ Time O(S) — S = total characters, worst case we touch every one 💾 Space O(1) — only indices; prefix sliced from the first string

⚠️ Gotchas. The bounds check col == strs[row].length() must come before charAt(col) or you'll throw on a shorter string. A single-element array returns that element. Disjoint first chars (e.g. "dog","car") correctly yield "" at column 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I scan column by column — the answer can't be longer than the shortest string, and the first string is a fine reference. As soon as any string disagrees or runs out at a column, I stop and slice. That's O(total characters) and constant extra space."
📄 LongestCommonPrefix.java

4 Roman to Integer LC 13 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Convert a Roman numeral string to its integer value, honoring subtractive forms like IV, IX, CM.

💡 Key insight. Add each numeral's value, EXCEPT when a smaller numeral immediately precedes a larger one — that pairing means subtraction. Scanning right-to-left, each numeral simply compares to its already-processed right neighbour: smaller ⇒ subtract, otherwise add.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Map each char to its value via a switch.
  2. Iterate from the last char to the first, tracking prev = the value just to the right.
  3. If val < prev, subtract it; else add it. Update prev = val.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass over the string 💾 Space O(1) — fixed lookup, a couple of scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. Right-to-left avoids look-ahead; a left-to-right version must peek at i+1. Only six subtractive pairs are legal, but the comparison rule handles them uniformly without enumerating them. Equal-adjacent (e.g. III) correctly adds, since val is not strictly less than prev.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Roman numerals are additive except for the subtractive pairs. If I walk from the right, each symbol knows what's to its right, so I just check: is this symbol smaller than the one I just saw? If so it subtracts, otherwise it adds. One pass, constant space."
📄 RomanToInteger.java

5 Reverse Integer LC 7 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Reverse the digits of a signed 32-bit integer; return 0 if the reversed value overflows the int range.

💡 Key insight. Pop the last digit with x % 10 and push it onto the result via result*10 + digit. The trap is overflow: check BEFORE multiplying whether result has already passed Integer.MAX_VALUE/10 (or the boundary-digit case), so you never actually overflow a 32-bit int.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Loop while x != 0: take digit = x % 10 (sign-preserving in Java), then x /= 10.
  2. Before appending, test the positive overflow boundary (> MAX/10, or == MAX/10 and digit > 7) → return 0.
  3. Symmetric test for the negative boundary (< MIN/10, or == MIN/10 and digit < -8) → return 0.
  4. Otherwise result = result*10 + digit.

⏱️ Time O(log|x|) — one iteration per decimal digit 💾 Space O(1) — a single accumulator

⚠️ Gotchas. Java's % keeps the operand's sign (-123 % 10 == -3), so negatives need no special casing. The boundary digits 7 and -8 come from the last digit of 2147483647 / -2147483648. Don't reverse into a long and cast — the problem assumes a strict 32-bit environment.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I peel digits off the right and rebuild left-to-right. The only subtlety is 32-bit overflow: I check the bound before I multiply, comparing against MAX/10 and the special last-digit case, so the int never actually wraps. Digit-count time, constant space."
📄 ReverseInteger.java

6 Zigzag Conversion LC 6 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Write the string in a zigzag across numRows rows, then read it off row by row.

💡 Key insight. Simulate writing the string row by row. A direction toggles: move down the rows until you hit the bottom, then bounce up to the top, and repeat. Concatenating the per-row buffers top-to-bottom yields the zigzag reading order — no index/geometry formula needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Early return s when numRows == 1 or numRows ≥ s.length() (no zigzag).
  2. Keep a row index and step (+1 down, -1 up); append each char to rows[row].
  3. Flip step to +1 at the top row, -1 at the bottom row, then row += step.
  4. Concatenate all row buffers into the result.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each char placed once, then concatenated once 💾 Space O(n) — the per-row buffers hold every character

⚠️ Gotchas. The numRows == 1 guard prevents a divide/bounce degenerate case where you'd never toggle. Flip direction after appending, checking edges before the move. The formula-based solution is faster to write incorrectly — the simulation is the safe interview answer.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rather than derive the index arithmetic, I literally simulate laying letters into rows, bouncing direction at the top and bottom edges. Then I read the rows out in order. It's linear time and space, and far less error-prone than the closed-form indexing."
📄 ZigzagConversion.java

7 Group Anagrams LC 49 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Group the input words so that all anagrams of each other land in the same list.

💡 Key insight. Words are anagrams iff they share the same letter-count signature, so build a canonical key from each word's 26 counts and bucket by it. Using the count vector (not a sorted string) keys in O(word) instead of O(word log word) — optimal when the alphabet is fixed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each word, count its 26 letters.
  2. Encode counts into a stable string key, appending '#' after each count to avoid collisions like "1,11" vs "11,1".
  3. computeIfAbsent(key, …).add(word) to grow the bucket.
  4. Return the map's values.

⏱️ Time O(n·k) — n words, k = max length, to build each key 💾 Space O(n·k) — keys plus the grouped words

⚠️ Gotchas. The '#' delimiter is essential — without it, counts run together and collide. Sorting each word (O(k log k) key) also works and is shorter to write, but the count-vector key is asymptotically better and worth stating. [""] groups to [[""]], not empty output.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Anagrams share a letter histogram, so I fingerprint each word by its 26-count vector and use that as a hash-map key. I could sort each word instead, but counting is O(k) versus O(k log k). The delimiter in the key stops multi-digit counts from colliding."
📄 GroupAnagrams.java

8 Encode and Decode Strings LC 271 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Serialize a list of arbitrary strings to one string and deserialize it back exactly, with no assumptions about the content.

💡 Key insight. Length-prefix framing: emit each string as "<len>#<payload>". The decoder reads digits up to the '#', learns exactly how many chars follow, and slices that many verbatim — so any delimiter inside the payload (even '#' itself, or an empty string) is irrelevant.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Encode: for each string append length + "#" + string.
  2. Decode: from cursor i, find the next '#'; parse the length header before it.
  3. Take exactly len chars starting after the '#' (content-agnostic), add to the result.
  4. Advance the cursor past that slice and repeat.

⏱️ Time O(N) — encode and decode each touch every character once 💾 Space O(N) — the encoded string / decoded list

⚠️ Gotchas. A naive single-char separator (e.g. comma) breaks the moment a payload contains it — the length prefix is precisely what makes it robust. Empty strings encode as "0#" and round-trip cleanly. The header delimiter must be the first '#' at or after the cursor, found via indexOf('#', i).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The hard part is content that contains your delimiter. I solve that with length prefixing: each string becomes its length, a marker, then the bytes. On decode I read the count, then blindly copy that many characters, so the payload can be anything. Linear in total length."
📄 EncodeAndDecodeStrings.java

9 Longest Palindromic Substring LC 5 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return a longest contiguous substring of s that reads the same forwards and backwards.

💡 Key insight. Every palindrome has a center, and there are 2n-1 of them (n single chars for odd lengths, n-1 gaps for even). Expand outward from each center while the two ends match, keeping the longest span. No DP table needed — O(1) space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each index i, expand an odd center (i, i) and an even center (i, i+1).
  2. expand grows left--/right++ while in-bounds and chars match, returning right - left - 1.
  3. If the larger of the two beats the best, recover bounds: start = i - (len-1)/2, end = i + len/2.
  4. Slice s[start..end].

⏱️ Time O(n²) — 2n-1 centers, each expansion O(n) worst case 💾 Space O(1) — only index bookkeeping

⚠️ Gotchas. Must try both odd and even centers — even-length palindromes ("bb") have no single-char center. The bound recovery (len-1)/2 vs len/2 is what makes one formula serve both parities. Manacher's algorithm gets O(n) but is overkill for an interview unless explicitly asked.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A palindrome is symmetric about a center, so I try all 2n-1 centers — single chars and gaps — and expand outward while the ends match. I track the longest. That's O(n²) time but O(1) space, and I'd mention Manacher's O(n) as the optimal if pressed."
📄 LongestPalindromicSubstring.java

10 Decode String LC 394 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Decode strings like 3[a2[c]] where k[...] means the bracketed substring repeated k times, supporting nesting.

💡 Key insight. Two stacks track the nested k[...] context. On '[' push the repeat count and the string built so far, then reset the builder; on ']' pop both, repeat the just-finished inner string k times, and splice it back onto the saved prefix.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Digits accumulate into count via count*10 + (c-'0') (multi-digit safe).
  2. On '[': push count and the current builder, then reset both.
  3. On ']': pop k and the saved prefix, append current.repeat(k) onto the prefix, make that the new current.
  4. Letters extend current. Return current at the end.

⏱️ Time O(output) — each decoded char emitted once 💾 Space O(output) — stacks plus the result buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. Multi-digit counts (10[a]) require accumulating the number, not reading a single digit. You need two stacks — one for counts, one for the prefix string — kept in lockstep. Resetting count and current on '[' is what isolates the inner segment.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Nesting screams stack. When I hit an opening bracket I stash the multiplier and whatever I've built so far, then start fresh inside. On the closing bracket I expand the inner string by its multiplier and glue it back onto the stashed prefix. Output-linear time."
📄 DecodeString.java

11 Reorganize String LC 767 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Rearrange s so no two adjacent characters are equal, or return "" if impossible.

💡 Key insight. Greedy with a max-heap by frequency: repeatedly pull the two most frequent remaining characters and append both — they differ, so no two equal chars land adjacent. It's feasible iff no character exceeds (n+1)/2; otherwise some copy is forced to touch itself.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count frequencies; if any exceeds (n+1)/2, return "" immediately.
  2. Push all positive counts into a max-heap keyed by frequency.
  3. While ≥ 2 entries remain, poll the top two, append both chars, decrement, and re-offer any still positive.
  4. If one entry remains (guaranteed count 1 by feasibility), append it.

⏱️ Time O(n log k) — n placements, heap ops over k ≤ 26 distinct chars 💾 Space O(k) — counts plus the heap

⚠️ Gotchas. Polling two-at-a-time is what guarantees adjacency is broken; placing one and "holding the previous" is the equivalent alternative. The feasibility bound is (n+1)/2, not n/2 — odd lengths allow one extra. Decrement and re-offer only if the count stays positive, or you'll push zeros.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I always want to place the most frequent leftover char, but not twice in a row — so I pop the top two from a frequency max-heap and place both, since they're distinct. If the heaviest char exceeds (n+1)/2 it's impossible. That's O(n log 26), effectively O(n)."
📄 ReorganizeString.java

12 Find And Replace in String LC 833 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Apply a batch of replacements — each "at index i, if source matches, swap to target" — where all indices refer to the original string and matches are non-overlapping.

💡 Key insight. All replacement indices refer to the ORIGINAL string and matches are non-overlapping, so sort operations by their source index and stitch the answer left-to-right: copy the untouched gap up to the next index, then if the source actually matches emit the target and jump past it, else emit the original. One forward cursor walks both string and op list.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build an index permutation and sort it by indices[idx] ascending.
  2. Keep a cursor = next untouched position in s.
  3. For each op: append the gap s[cursor..at]; if s.startsWith(src, at), append the target and set cursor = at + src.length(); otherwise leave cursor = at.
  4. Append the trailing remainder after the last op.

⏱️ Time O(m log m + n + Σtarget) — m = #ops, n = |s| 💾 Space O(m) — index permutation plus the output builder

⚠️ Gotchas. Why sort? Applying ops in input order would shift later indices; anchoring to the original string and processing left-to-right avoids index drift entirely. startsWith(src, at) matches against the original s, never the partial output. On a non-match, set cursor = at (not at+1) so the next gap/tail copies s[at].
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The catch is that all indices point at the original string, so I must not let earlier replacements shift later ones. I sort the ops by index and rebuild the string with a single cursor: copy the gap, test the source against the original, then either swap-and-skip or fall through. Sorting dominates at O(m log m)."
📄 FindAndReplaceInString.java

13 Number of Matching Subsequences LC 792 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given s and a list of words, count how many words are subsequences of s.

💡 Key insight. Instead of scanning s once per word (O(|words|·|s|)), scan s ONCE and let its characters pull every word forward together. Bucket each word by the char it currently waits for; as you read c from s, every word parked in bucket[c] has its next char satisfied — advance it and re-file under its new waiting char, or count it matched if it ran out.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Create 26 buckets of (wordIndex, matchedSoFar); park each word in the bucket of its first char.
  2. For each char of s, snapshot bucket[c]'s size and drain exactly that many (only words waiting before this char).
  3. For each drained word, advance its pointer; if it reaches the word's length, increment matched, else re-file under the next char's bucket.
  4. Return matched.

⏱️ Time O(|s| + Σ|word|) — each char of each word causes at most one re-bucket 💾 Space O(Σ|word|) — pointers held across the 26 buckets

⚠️ Gotchas. Snapshot the bucket size before the inner loop — otherwise a re-filed word could be re-processed within the same character of s (an infinite/over-count bug on repeated letters). The naive per-word scan is the brute force to contrast against. Duplicate words are counted independently, which is correct.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force checks each word against s independently — quadratic. I invert it: I park every word on the character it needs next, then sweep s once. Each character wakes the words waiting on it and pushes them forward. Total work is |s| plus the total length of all words."
📄 NumberOfMatchingSubsequences.java

14 Sentence Screen Fitting LC 418 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a sentence (array of words) and a rows × cols screen, count how many times the whole sentence fits, words kept intact and separated by single spaces.

💡 Key insight. Lay the sentence out as one cyclic stream "word1 word2 … wordk " (note the trailing space). For a row of width cols, fitting is just: advance a cursor by cols, then if the landing char is a space skip it, else back up to the previous space. Sentences shown = cursor / streamLength. Memoize per starting word so repeated row shapes are O(1).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Precompute, for each possible starting word i: how many full sentences a single row completes and which word the next row starts on.
  2. Greedily pack words while used + len(word) ≤ cols, adding len + 1 per placed word; wrap past the last word increments the sentence count.
  3. Store nextStart[i] and sentencesAdded[i].
  4. Walk rows rows, summing sentencesAdded[word] and jumping via nextStart[word] — O(1) per row.

⏱️ Time O(rows + k·L) — each start word built once, k = #words, L = max word length 💾 Space O(k) — two memo arrays keyed by starting word index

⚠️ Gotchas. Any word longer than cols means zero fits — the packing loop naturally completes nothing. The trailing space in the conceptual stream is what makes the word-end accounting clean. Without the per-start memo, the row simulation is O(rows · cols), which TLEs on large screens — the cache caps distinct starts at k.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A row is fully determined by which word it starts on, so there are at most k distinct row shapes. I precompute, for each start word, how many sentences that row finishes and where the next row begins, then just hop row to row. That turns an O(rows·cols) simulation into O(rows + k·L)."
📄 SentenceScreenFitting.java

15 Read N Characters Given Read4 II LC 158 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Implement read(buf, n) using only a read4 primitive (reads up to 4 chars), where read may be called multiple times on the same stream.

💡 Key insight. read4 only hands back chars in chunks of 4, so a single read(n) may grab more than it needs and must REMEMBER the unconsumed tail for the next call — that internal carry buffer is the whole trick of the "call multiple times" variant.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep instance state: a 4-char buf4, its valid size, and a read position.
  2. In read, loop until n chars are written or the source is drained.
  3. If buf4 is empty, refill via read4; a result of 0 means EOF → break.
  4. Copy from buf4 into buf only as many as still needed; leftover chars stay parked for the next read.

⏱️ Time O(n) — amortized; each source char is moved into buf once 💾 Space O(1) — a fixed 4-char carry buffer regardless of n

⚠️ Gotchas. The distinguishing feature vs LC 157 (single call) is persisting buf4 state between calls — a local buffer fails the multi-call tests. A read4 returning fewer than 4 signals EOF; don't loop forever expecting a full chunk. The two inner conditions (written < n AND buf4Pos < buf4Size) jointly stop you from over-copying or over-reading.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because read4 only comes in groups of four, a read of, say, one character pulls four and must keep the other three. So I hold a 4-char buffer as instance state across calls: each read first drains leftovers, then fetches fresh chunks until satisfied or EOF. Amortized linear, constant extra space."
📄 ReadNCharactersGivenRead4II.java

16 Text Justification LC 68 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Format words into lines of exactly maxWidth, fully justified (extra spaces spread evenly, leftmost gaps favored); the last line and any single-word line are left-justified.

💡 Key insight. Greedy packing: fit as many words on a line as possible (each gap needs ≥ 1 space). Once a line is fixed, distribute the surplus spaces as evenly as possible — when they don't divide evenly, the leftmost gaps get one extra. Single-word lines and the last line are left-justified instead.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Greedily extend a run [i, j) while lettersLen + words[j].length() + (j-i) ≤ maxWidth (the j-i term reserves one space per gap).
  2. If it's the last line or a single word, join with single spaces and right-pad to maxWidth.
  3. Else compute base = totalSpaces / gaps and extra = totalSpaces % gaps; give the first extra gaps one more space.
  4. Emit the line, set i = j, repeat.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every character is emitted once across all lines 💾 Space O(n) — the output strings (O(maxWidth) scratch aside)

⚠️ Gotchas. The packing test lettersLen + len + (j-i) ≤ maxWidth accounts for the minimum one-space gaps; off-by-one here is the classic bug. Two special cases break the even-distribution rule: the final line and any line with a single word (zero gaps) are left-justified and right-padded. The extra leftover spaces go to the leftmost gaps, not the rightmost.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I greedily pack each line, reserving a minimum space per gap. For a full line I split the leftover spaces across the gaps, handing the remainder to the leftmost ones. The last line and any single-word line are left-justified and padded right. It's a careful simulation — linear time, the difficulty is purely in the edge cases."
📄 TextJustification.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work top-to-bottom: the easy four (242, 20, 14, 13) cement the count-vector and stack idioms in minutes — re-derive each from a blank file until they're muscle memory. Then drill the core mediums (49, 271, 5, 394) since they recombine those idioms with hashing, framing, and center-expansion. Save the 🟦 signature quartet (833, 792, 418, 158) and the two hard favorites (767, 68) for focused sessions — each has one non-obvious trick (cursor-against-original, invert-the-loop, per-start memo, carry buffer, frequency heap, space distribution) that interviewers probe. Rehearse these Google-favorite follow-ups out loud: in Decode String, handle malformed input; in Text Justification, justify right instead; in Number of Matching Subsequences, return which words matched, not just the count.

  • ✅ I can explain why the count-vector key beats sorting for anagram grouping, and why the '#' delimiter is mandatory.
  • ✅ I can state when a stack is the right tool (balanced brackets, nested k[...]) and push the expected closer for a one-line match.
  • ✅ I can expand around both odd and even centers and recover the bounds with one parity-agnostic formula.
  • ✅ I can derive the (n+1)/2 feasibility bound for Reorganize String and justify the two-at-a-time heap pop.
  • ✅ I can articulate why Find-and-Replace sorts by index and matches against the original string to avoid index drift.
  • ✅ I can invert the subsequence-matching loop (scan s once, bucket words by waiting char) and explain the snapshot-size pitfall.
  • ✅ I can reduce Sentence Screen Fitting to ≤ k distinct row shapes and memoize per starting word.
  • ✅ I can articulate why Read4 II needs persistent buffer state across calls, unlike the single-call variant.
  • ✅ I can implement Text Justification's space distribution (base + extra to leftmost gaps) and name the two left-justified special cases.
  • ✅ I can guard 32-bit overflow in Reverse Integer before multiplying, citing the 7 / -8 boundary digits.

🥞 Stack & Parsing

Stacks turn "I need to remember earlier context until a later event resolves it" into linear-time passes — that's the engine behind monotonic-stack range queries and recursive-descent expression evaluation. For Google L6 these problems are high-ROI because they reward clean state management and precise reasoning about operator precedence, parenthesis scoping, and amortized O(n) bounds under pressure.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 5 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Next greater / next warmer / next smaller element" or "span until a larger value" → monotonic stack of indices.
  • "Largest rectangle / maximal area bounded by the limiting height" → monotonic increasing stack; width is known at pop time.
  • Expression strings with + - * / and/or parentheses to evaluate → stack-based or recursive-descent parsing.
  • Nested structure with multipliers that compound outward (chemical formulas, encoded strings like 3[a2[c]]) → stack of partial results / count-maps, one frame per nesting level.
  • Precedence matters (*// bind tighter than +/-) → defer addition by pushing terms; apply *// against the stack top immediately.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two idioms dominate. (A) Monotonic stack: keep indices in sorted (increasing or decreasing) height/temperature order; when the incoming element breaks the order, popped elements have both boundaries known — the incoming index on one side and the new stack top on the other — so their answer is computed exactly once. (B) Expression parsing: maintain a current number and a pending operator; resolve *// immediately against the top of an operand stack (so precedence is baked in) and sum the stack at the end; handle parentheses by pushing context (iterative) or recursing on a shared cursor. Here is the precedence-aware evaluator skeleton:

// Operand stack with precedence: defer +/- by pushing, apply */ to the top now.
Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
int num = 0;
char op = '+';                      // operator pending in front of `num`
for (each char c, plus a virtual terminator at the end) {
    if (isDigit(c)) num = num * 10 + (c - '0');
    // on an operator OR ')' OR end-of-string, flush the pending op:
    if (isOperatorOrBoundary(c)) {
        switch (op) {
            case '+' -> stack.push(num);
            case '-' -> stack.push(-num);
            case '*' -> stack.push(stack.pop() * num);   // binds tighter than +/-
            case '/' -> stack.push(stack.pop() / num);
        }
        op = c; num = 0;
    }
}
int total = 0;
for (int v : stack) total += v;     // all */ resolved → just sum additive terms

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace

2Basic Calculator LC 224 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav Sign + result stack for parenthesesO(n)O(d)
3Largest Rectangle in Histogram LC 84 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature Monotonic increasing stack + sentinelO(n)O(n)
4Number of Atoms LC 726 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature Stack of count-maps per scopeO(n log k)O(n)

📘 Deep dive

1 Daily Temperatures LC 739 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. For each day, how many days until a strictly warmer temperature? Return 0 where none exists.

💡 Key insight. Keep a stack of indices whose temperatures strictly decrease from bottom to top. Today's temperature resolves every colder day still waiting on top of the stack — each such day's "next warmer day" is exactly today, so pop and record the gap. Each index is pushed and popped at most once → linear, not O(n²).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Init answer[] to 0 (the default "no warmer day").
  2. Scan left to right. While the stack is non-empty and temps[i] > temps[stack.peek()], pop prev and set answer[prev] = i − prev.
  3. Push i.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every index pushed and popped once 💾 Space O(n) — stack holds a strictly decreasing run in the worst case

⚠️ Gotchas. Store indices, not temperatures — you need the index to compute the day gap and to look up the temperature. The comparison is strict (>): equal temperatures do not count as warmer, so they stay on the stack. Days that never get popped keep their default 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force compares each day against all later days, O(n²). But I notice that when a warm day arrives it answers every colder day still pending, so I keep a decreasing stack of indices: each incoming day pops all the cooler days it resolves and records the gap. Every index is pushed and popped exactly once, so it's O(n) time and O(n) space."
📄 DailyTemperatures.java

2 Basic Calculator LC 224 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Evaluate a string expression of non-negative integers with +, -, parentheses, and spaces (no * or /).

💡 Key insight. A single left-to-right scan with a running result and a current sign (±1) handles +/-. Parentheses need no recursion: on ( push the current result and the sign applying to the whole group, then reset to a fresh sub-expression; on ) fold the group back in by multiplying by the pushed sign and adding the pushed result.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Accumulate multi-digit numbers with number = number*10 + (c−'0').
  2. On +/-: commit result += sign*number, reset number, set the new sign.
  3. On (: push result then sign; reset result = 0, sign = 1.
  4. On ): commit the last inner number, then result = result*stack.pop() + stack.pop() (sign first, then outer result).
  5. Return result + sign*number to fold in a trailing number.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each character processed once 💾 Space O(d) — stack depth equals parenthesis nesting depth

⚠️ Gotchas. Order of the two pops matters: you pushed result then sign, so on ) you pop sign first (multiply) and result second (add). Don't forget the trailing result + sign*number after the loop — the last number isn't committed by any operator. A leading unary minus like -(3-(2+1)) works because sign starts able to flip and applies to the group.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since there's no multiplication, the only state I need is a running total and the sign in front of the next number. Parentheses just save and restore that context: when I hit an open paren I push the result-so-far and the sign that multiplies the whole group, evaluate the inside from zero, and on the close paren I scale the inner value by that saved sign and add back the saved result. Every character is touched once, O(n), and the stack only grows with nesting depth."
📄 BasicCalculator.java

3 Largest Rectangle in Histogram LC 84 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given bar heights, find the area of the largest rectangle that fits entirely within the histogram.

💡 Key insight. For each bar as the limiting height, its widest rectangle stretches from the first shorter bar on its left to the first shorter bar on its right. A stack of indices with strictly increasing heights finds both boundaries in one pass: when a shorter bar arrives, every taller bar popped is "closed off" on the right by i and on the left by the new stack top, so its maximal width — and thus area — is known exactly at pop time.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Iterate i from 0 to n inclusive; treat h = 0 at i == n as a sentinel that flushes the stack.
  2. While the stack's top bar is ≥ h, pop its height; left boundary is the new top (-1 if empty), so width = i − leftExclusive − 1; update best.
  3. Push i.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every index pushed and popped at most once 💾 Space O(n) — the monotonic stack

⚠️ Gotchas. The trailing zero-height sentinel (loop to i == n) is what guarantees every remaining bar gets popped and measured — without it, an increasing histogram never flushes. Width is i − stack.peek() − 1 after popping (the boundary is exclusive), and -1 when the stack empties means the rectangle extends to the far left. Use >= on the pop condition so equal-height bars merge correctly.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The largest rectangle is bounded by some bar's height, extended left and right until it hits something shorter. I keep an increasing stack of indices; when a shorter bar comes in, each taller bar I pop now knows both its right boundary, the current index, and its left boundary, the element below it on the stack, so I can compute its exact width and area in O(1). A virtual zero bar at the end flushes everything. Each bar is pushed and popped once, so O(n)."
📄 LargestRectangleInHistogram.java

4 Number of Atoms LC 726 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a chemical formula (elements, optional counts, nested parentheses with multipliers like K4(ON(SO3)2)2), return the atom counts as a string sorted by element with counts > 1 appended.

💡 Key insight. Parentheses define nested scopes whose multipliers compound outward, so keep a stack of count-maps — one per open group. An element adds its (optionally suffixed) count to the top map; ( pushes a fresh scope; ) pops the scope, reads the trailing multiplier, scales every count in the popped map, and merges it into the now-current scope. A TreeMap keeps names sorted for free.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Push an initial empty TreeMap as the outermost scope.
  2. On an element: read an uppercase letter + following lowercase letters as the name, then an optional count (default 1); merge into the top map.
  3. On (: push a new TreeMap.
  4. On ): read the multiplier, pop the group, multiply each entry by it, and merge into the parent.
  5. Build the result from the final top map, appending counts only when > 1.

⏱️ Time O(n log k) — n = formula length, k = distinct elements (TreeMap merges) 💾 Space O(n) — the scope stack plus accumulated counts

⚠️ Gotchas. An absent count defaults to 1, both for an element and after a ) — handle "no digits" cleanly in the number reader. Element names are uppercase-then-lowercase (Mg, not MG); read greedily. Multipliers apply to the entire popped group, so scale before merging upward. Counts can exceed what a naive parse expects (nested multipliers multiply), but they stay within int for the constraints. Append the count only when it's > 1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each parenthesis level has its own multiplier that scales everything inside, and those multipliers nest, so I use a stack of maps — one per open group. I add atoms to the current top map; an open paren starts a fresh map; a close paren takes the multiplier after it, scales the whole popped map, and folds it into the map below. I store names in a TreeMap so the output is already sorted, and I append a count only when it's greater than one. It's linear in the formula length up to the log factor from the sorted-map merges."
📄 NumberOfAtoms.java

5 Basic Calculator III LC 772 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Evaluate an expression with + - * /, parentheses, and non-negative integers, respecting operator precedence and integer (truncating) division.

💡 Key insight. Handle precedence by deferring addition: push numbers onto a stack, but apply *// immediately against the stack top, so by the time you sum the stack the high-precedence operators are already resolved. The pending operator (the one before the current number) decides the action. Parentheses are handled by recursion on a shared cursor: ( spawns a sub-evaluation that returns at the matching ), and its scalar result becomes an ordinary operand.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a shared pos index so recursion resumes where the sub-expression stopped.
  2. In eval: build num; on ( set num = eval(s) (recurse).
  3. On an operator, ), or end-of-string, apply the pending op: +/- push (±num), *// rewrite the top in place; then store the new op and break on ).
  4. Return the sum of the stack — all *// already folded in.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each character consumed once across all recursion levels 💾 Space O(n) — operand stack plus recursion depth bounded by nesting

⚠️ Gotchas. The shared cursor (pos as a field) is essential — a local index per call would re-scan the inner expression. The "apply pending op" trigger must also fire at end-of-string (atEnd), or the final number is dropped. Operate on the operator before the current number, not after. Integer division truncates toward zero in Java for non-negative operands, matching the spec. Skip spaces without resetting the pending op.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I combine two ideas. For precedence, I push additive terms onto a stack but resolve times and divide immediately against the top, so the final answer is just the sum of the stack. For parentheses, I recurse using a shared cursor — an open paren kicks off a sub-evaluation that returns the value at the matching close paren, which I then treat as a plain number. The key is that the operator I act on is the one in front of the current operand, and I must also flush at the very end of the string. Every character is read exactly once, so O(n)."
📄 BasicCalculatorIII.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the two tracks separately, then together. Monotonic-stack track: derive Daily Temperatures (739) cold, then Largest Rectangle (84) — the sentinel and the exclusive-boundary width are the bits people forget. Parsing track: build Basic Calculator (224) first (sign + context stack, no precedence), then layer precedence in Basic Calculator III (772), then generalize the nesting idea to Number of Atoms (726). From a blank file, re-derive: the decreasing-vs-increasing stack invariant and why each index is touched twice; the "defer +/- , apply */ now, sum at end" precedence trick; and the recursive-cursor pattern for parentheses. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Basic Calculator II (227) (precedence without parens — the warm-up for 772), Decode String (394) (the exact stack-of-frames nesting pattern as Number of Atoms), Trapping Rain Water (42) and Sum of Subarray Minimums (907) as monotonic-stack cousins of 84, and Maximal Rectangle (85) which literally calls 84 per row.

  • ✅ I can explain why a monotonic stack gives O(n): each index is pushed and popped at most once.
  • ✅ I can state, at pop time, what the left and right boundaries of a popped bar are and why the width is i − newTop − 1.
  • ✅ I can justify the zero-height sentinel in Largest Rectangle and why the loop runs to i == n.
  • ✅ I can show how pushing result then sign on ( and popping in the right order restores context on ).
  • ✅ I can explain how deferring addition while applying *// immediately encodes operator precedence with no second pass.
  • ✅ I can describe why a shared cursor (not a local index) is required for the recursive parenthesis evaluation.
  • ✅ I can explain why a stack of count-maps captures compounding nested multipliers in Number of Atoms.

🔎 Binary Search

Binary search is far more than "look up a value in a sorted array": it is a way to halve any monotonic search space — over indices, over rotation pivots, or over the answer itself. For Google L6 it's high-ROI because the hard variants (binary-search-on-answer, partition-the-median, rotated arrays) test whether you can spot a hidden monotone predicate and turn an O(n) or O(n log n) scan into O(log) — exactly the kind of insight interviewers probe for.

Source: Prep Tracker tab · 8 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The input is sorted, or sorted-then-rotated, or otherwise has a monotone structure you can exploit.
  • You want a boundary: first/last occurrence, insertion point, first index satisfying a predicate.
  • The answer is a number in a known range and "is value v feasible?" is monotone (true past some threshold, false before) — that's binary search on the answer.
  • A brute-force solution is O(n) or O(n·range) and the constraints (n or value up to 109) scream for O(log).
  • Phrases like "minimize the maximum", "maximize the minimum", "smallest capacity/speed/cap that works".
  • Two sorted arrays and a k-th / median query — partition by binary search.

🧠 Core idea & template

The reliable mental model is "find the boundary of a monotone predicate". Define a boolean feasible(x) that is false, false, …, true, true over the range; binary search collapses [lo, hi] to the first true (or last false). Use the half-open while (lo < hi) form to converge to a single survivor without off-by-one pain — never index mid+1 unless the loop guarantees it's in bounds. For binary search on the answer, the array values aren't what you search; you search the candidate answers and use a greedy/counting feasible check.

// Boundary form: smallest x in [lo, hi] with feasible(x) == true.
int lo = LOW, hi = HIGH;            // hi inclusive here
while (lo < hi) {                   // converge to ONE index
    int mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2;  // overflow-safe midpoint, biased low
    if (feasible(mid)) hi = mid;   // mid works -> keep it, shrink right
    else               lo = mid + 1; // mid fails -> answer is strictly right
}
return lo;                          // == hi: the boundary

// Binary search ON THE ANSWER (Koko / Split Array template):
//   lo = minimal plausible answer, hi = maximal plausible answer
//   feasible(x): can we meet the constraint if we allow value x?
//                (monotone: bigger x is never harder)
//   answer = smallest x with feasible(x) == true

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Sqrt(x) LC 69 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged Search last k with k²≤xO(log x)O(1)
2Find Peak Element LC 162 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged Follow the ascending slopeO(log n)O(1)
3Find First and Last Position of Element in Sorted Array LC 34 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged Two lower-bound searchesO(log n)O(1)
4Search in Rotated Sorted Array LC 33 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core Identify sorted half, range-checkO(log n)O(1)
5Find Minimum in Rotated Sorted Array LC 153 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core Compare mid to right endO(log n)O(1)
6Koko Eating Bananas LC 875 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core Binary search on answer (speed)O(n log P)O(1)
7Split Array Largest Sum LC 410 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged Binary search on answer (cap)O(n log S)O(1)
8Median of Two Sorted Arrays LC 4 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core Partition the smaller arrayO(log min(m,n))O(1)

📘 Deep dive

1 Sqrt(x) LC 69 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return floor(√x) for a non-negative integer x, using integer arithmetic only.

💡 Key insight. floor(√x) is the largest k with k·k ≤ x. Since k·k is monotincreasing, the valid k form a prefix [0..ans] — binary search the last k satisfying the predicate.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Search lo=0, hi=x (since √x ≤ x for all x ≥ 0).
  2. At each mid, test (long) mid*mid <= x. If true, record ans = mid and move right (lo = mid+1); else move left (hi = mid-1).
  3. Return the last recorded ans.

⏱️ Time O(log x) — halve the candidate range each step 💾 Space O(1) — a few scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. mid*mid overflows int near x = 231−1 (mid ≈ 46340) — cast to long before squaring. This is the classic "last index where predicate holds" form, so it uses the inclusive lo <= hi loop with an explicit ans, not the converge-to-one form. Don't start hi = x/2 as a "shortcut" without handling x = 0 and x = 1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I'm looking for the greatest k whose square doesn't exceed x. Because squaring is monotone, feasibility flips once, so I binary search the boundary: when mid² ≤ x I save mid and search higher, otherwise lower. I square as a long to dodge overflow, giving O(log x)."
📄 SqrtX.java

2 Find Peak Element LC 162 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return the index of any peak (element strictly greater than both neighbors); assume nums[-1] = nums[n] = -∞ and no equal adjacent elements.

💡 Key insight. The local slope points toward a peak. If nums[mid] < nums[mid+1] the array is still rising, so a peak must exist to the right; otherwise you're at or past a peak, so one exists at mid or to its left. Walking uphill always lands on some peak.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Use lo=0, hi=n-1 with the converge form while (lo < hi).
  2. If nums[mid] < nums[mid+1] (ascending), discard the left: lo = mid+1.
  3. Else (descending or peak at mid), keep mid as a candidate: hi = mid. Return lo when the range collapses.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — halve the range each step 💾 Space O(1) — two indices

⚠️ Gotchas. The lo < hi guard is what keeps mid+1 in bounds — with lo <= hi you'd read past the end. This works even on unsorted input, which surprises people: you're not searching a value, you're following a gradient. Any valid peak is accepted, so multiple answers are fine; don't try to find "the" maximum.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Even though the array isn't sorted, the boundaries act like −∞, so from any rising slope a peak must lie ahead. I binary search by comparing mid to its right neighbor: if I'm ascending I go right, otherwise I keep the left side including mid. The range shrinks to a single guaranteed peak in O(log n)."
📄 FindPeakElement.java

3 Find First and Last Position of Element in Sorted Array LC 34 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. In a sorted array, return the first and last indices of a target, or [-1, -1] if absent — in O(log n).

💡 Key insight. A target occupies a contiguous block, so its bounds are two lowerBound searches. lowerBound(target) gives the first index with value ≥ target; lowerBound(target+1) - 1 gives the last index of the block (one before the first value > target).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Compute first = lowerBound(nums, target). If it's out of range or nums[first] != target, the target is absent — return [-1,-1].
  2. Else compute last = lowerBound(nums, target+1) - 1.
  3. lowerBound is the half-open [lo, hi) search returning the smallest i with nums[i] >= key (or n if none).

⏱️ Time O(log n) — two binary searches 💾 Space O(1) — index arithmetic only

⚠️ Gotchas. You must verify nums[first] == target after the first search — lowerBound returns an insertion point, which may hold a larger value or sit at n. Using target+1 elegantly finds the upper bound without a separate "find last" variant, but mind potential overflow if target is Integer.MAX_VALUE. The half-open hi = n (exclusive) convention is what makes the predecessor trick land exactly on the last occurrence.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Equal elements are contiguous, so I find the block's edges with two lower-bound searches: the first index ≥ target is the start, and the first index ≥ target+1 minus one is the end. I confirm the start actually equals target to handle absence. Two O(log n) searches, O(1) space."
📄 FindFirstAndLastPosition.java

4 Search in Rotated Sorted Array LC 33 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. A sorted array of distinct values was rotated at an unknown pivot; find the index of target, or −1, in O(log n).

💡 Key insight. At any mid, at least one half is fully sorted. If nums[lo] <= nums[mid] the left half is sorted; otherwise the right half is. Test whether target falls inside the sorted half (a clean range check) and shrink toward it, discarding the other half.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Inclusive loop lo <= hi. If nums[mid] == target, return mid.
  2. If left is sorted (nums[lo] <= nums[mid]): if nums[lo] <= target < nums[mid] go left (hi = mid-1), else go right.
  3. Else right is sorted: if nums[mid] < target <= nums[hi] go right (lo = mid+1), else go left.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — halve the search space every iteration 💾 Space O(1) — iterative, a few indices

⚠️ Gotchas. The boundary comparisons must use the right strict/non-strict mix: nums[lo] <= target < nums[mid] on the left, nums[mid] < target <= nums[hi] on the right — getting an inequality wrong drops valid hits. Use nums[lo] <= nums[mid] (not <) so a single-element or already-aligned half counts as sorted. With duplicates (LC 81) the nums[lo]==nums[mid] tie breaks this and forces an O(n) worst case.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rotation leaves one sorted half around any midpoint. I detect which half is sorted by comparing the low end to mid, then check whether the target lies within that sorted half's range — if so I recurse into it, otherwise the other half. Each step halves the array, so O(log n)."
📄 SearchInRotatedSortedArray.java

5 Find Minimum in Rotated Sorted Array LC 153 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the minimum element of a rotated sorted array of distinct values in O(log n).

💡 Key insight. The minimum is the single "drop" point of the rotation. Comparing mid to the right end localizes the drop: if nums[mid] > nums[hi] the dip is strictly right of mid, so search (mid, hi]; otherwise mid could be the min, so search [lo, mid]. Comparing to hi (not lo) sidesteps the already-sorted ambiguity.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Converge form while (lo < hi) with lo=0, hi=n-1.
  2. If nums[mid] > nums[hi], set lo = mid+1 (drop is to the right).
  3. Else set hi = mid (keep mid as a min candidate). Return nums[lo] at convergence.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — halve the range each step 💾 Space O(1) — two indices

⚠️ Gotchas. Compare against nums[hi], not nums[lo] — a non-rotated array ([1,2,3,4]) has nums[mid] > nums[lo] everywhere, which would wrongly push you right. The hi = mid (not mid-1) keeps mid in play since it might be the answer. With duplicates (LC 154) the nums[mid] == nums[hi] tie forces an hi-- fallback and an O(n) worst case.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The min sits at the rotation's drop. I compare mid to the right endpoint: if mid is larger, the array dips further right, so I move past mid; otherwise the drop is at mid or to its left, so I keep mid. Comparing to the right end handles the no-rotation case cleanly. It converges in O(log n)."
📄 FindMinimumInRotatedSortedArray.java

6 Koko Eating Bananas LC 875 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given banana piles and h hours, find the minimum integer eating speed k (bananas/hour, one pile at a time) so Koko finishes all piles within h hours.

💡 Key insight — binary search on the answer. Hours-needed is monotone in speed: a faster Koko never needs more hours, so feasible speeds form a suffix [answer..maxPile]. You don't search the piles — you search the candidate speeds and ask "does speed k finish in ≤ h hours?"
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Set lo = 1, hi = max(piles) (no point eating faster than the biggest pile).
  2. Converge: for mid, compute hoursNeeded = Σ ceil(p / mid). If ≤ h, it's feasible, so try slower (hi = mid); else need faster (lo = mid+1).
  3. ceil(p / k) is computed as (p + k - 1) / k to avoid floating point. Return lo.

⏱️ Time O(n·log P) — P = max pile; feasibility scans n piles per search step 💾 Space O(1) — running counters

⚠️ Gotchas. This is the canonical binary-search-on-answer template — the array is the input to feasible, not the thing you bisect. Accumulate hours in a long: a 109 pile at speed 1 alone overflows int. Use the integer-ceil trick (p+k-1)/k, never Math.ceil((double)p/k) which loses precision at scale. lo starts at 1, not 0 (speed 0 is meaningless).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The total hours decrease monotonically as speed rises, so I binary search the smallest speed that still finishes within h. My feasibility check sums the ceiling of each pile divided by the speed; if it fits in h I lower the speed, otherwise raise it. The range is 1 to the largest pile, giving O(n log maxPile)."
📄 KokoEatingBananas.java

7 Split Array Largest Sum LC 410 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Split the array into at most k non-empty contiguous subarrays so that the largest subarray sum is minimized; return that minimized largest sum.

💡 Key insight — binary search on the answer (the cap). "Minimize the maximum" is monotone in the cap you allow: a bigger cap never needs more pieces. So binary search the smallest cap achievable with ≤ k subarrays, where feasibility is a greedy left-to-right sweep that opens a new piece whenever the running sum would exceed the cap.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Search range [max(nums), sum(nums)]: the cap must fit the biggest single element, and one piece (the whole array) is always feasible.
  2. For candidate cap, count piecesNeeded greedily: extend the current piece until adding the next element exceeds cap, then start a new piece.
  3. If piecesNeeded ≤ k, the cap works — tighten (right = cap); else loosen (left = cap+1). Return left.

⏱️ Time O(n·log S) — S = total sum; greedy check scans n elements per step 💾 Space O(1) — running counters

⚠️ Gotchas. Same template as Koko — the answer space is [max element, total sum], and the lower bound must be the max element (a cap below it can never hold that element in any piece). Use long for hi/cap/running sum since the sum can exceed int. The greedy piece-count is provably optimal here (no DP needed), which is the elegance interviewers look for over the O(n²k) DP. piecesNeeded starts at 1, not 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "There's a DP, but the cleaner solution binary searches the answer. The largest-sum cap is monotone: a higher cap needs no more pieces, so I bisect the smallest cap that a greedy split achieves in at most k pieces. The range runs from the max element to the total sum, and each feasibility scan is O(n), giving O(n log sum)."
📄 SplitArrayLargestSum.java

8 Median of Two Sorted Arrays LC 4 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the median of two sorted arrays in O(log(min(m, n))).

💡 Key insight. The median splits the merged array into equal-size left/right halves where every left element ≤ every right element. You only need to decide how many left-half slots come from array A (the rest come from B), so binary search that count on the smaller array. A partition is valid when maxLeftA ≤ minRightB and maxLeftB ≤ minRightA.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Ensure A is the shorter array (swap if not). Let half = (m+n+1)/2 = size of the combined left half.
  2. Binary search i = count taken from A on the left over [0, m]; then j = half - i from B. Use ±∞ sentinels when i or j hits an array edge.
  3. If valid: odd total → max(maxLeftA, maxLeftB); even → average that with min(minRightA, minRightB). If maxLeftA > minRightB, move i left; else move it right.

⏱️ Time O(log min(m, n)) — bisect the partition on the shorter array 💾 Space O(1) — constant scalars

⚠️ Gotchas. Always binary search the smaller array so the partition index range is minimized and j = half - i stays valid. half = (m+n+1)/2 with the +1 makes the formula work uniformly for odd and even totals (odd → median is the left half's max). The ±∞ sentinels (Integer.MIN/MAX_VALUE) handle empty-side partitions; forgetting them is the #1 bug. The loop uses inclusive lo <= hi because a valid partition can sit at either endpoint.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of merging, I find a partition: take i elements from the smaller array and the rest of the left half from the other, so that everything left is ≤ everything right. I binary search i; when the cross-boundary inequalities hold I read off the median from the boundary elements, using ±∞ sentinels at the edges. Bisecting the shorter array gives O(log min(m,n))."
📄 MedianOfTwoSortedArrays.java

🔁 Practice plan

Build from the boundary template outward. Re-derive from a blank file in this order: Sqrt(x) (last-true predicate + overflow cast) → Find First/Last (lowerBound and the target+1 trick) → Find Peak (gradient, converge form) → Find Minimum in Rotated then Search in Rotated (compare-to-right-end, then sorted-half detection) → Koko and Split Array back-to-back to cement the binary-search-on-answer template (define the range, write a monotone feasible) → Median of Two Sorted Arrays (partition + sentinels). For Koko and Split Array especially, practice articulating the three pieces out loud: the answer range, why feasibility is monotone, and what the greedy/counting check is. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Capacity to Ship Packages in D Days (LC 1011, same answer-search template), Search in Rotated Sorted Array II (LC 81, duplicates → O(n) worst case), and Kth Smallest Element in a Sorted Matrix (LC 378, binary search on value + count).

  • ✅ I can write the converge-to-one (lo < hi) and last-true (lo <= hi + ans) templates from memory and say when to use each.
  • ✅ I can recognize "minimize the maximum / maximize the minimum" as a binary-search-on-answer signal and name the range + feasibility check.
  • ✅ I can explain why Koko's and Split Array's feasibility predicates are monotone, and write their greedy checks with long safety.
  • ✅ I can detect the sorted half in a rotated array and pick the correct strict/non-strict range inequalities.
  • ✅ I can justify comparing mid to the right end in Find Minimum and why that handles no-rotation.
  • ✅ I can derive Median of Two Sorted Arrays' partition condition and explain the ±∞ sentinels and the (m+n+1)/2 half.
  • ✅ I can name where integer overflow bites (mid·mid, summed hours, total sum) and how the casts prevent it.

🔗 Linked Lists

Linked-list problems are pure pointer surgery: you re-wire next (and sometimes prev/random) links in place instead of copying data. A small toolkit — dummy heads, fast/slow two-pointers, in-place reversal, and the heap/hash-map pairings — solves nearly all of them, which makes this family extremely high-ROI: Google L6 interviewers lean on it to test whether you can mutate a structure cleanly under pressure without losing a node or leaking a cycle.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 10 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The input is a singly/doubly linked list and you're asked to mutate structure (reverse, reorder, splice, merge) rather than just read values.
  • "In O(1) extra space" or "without using an array" — screams in-place pointer rewiring, not a copy-to-list-and-back.
  • "Nth from the end", "middle of the list", or "is there a cycle" — the fast/slow two-pointer idiom.
  • You need the node before a target to splice it out, or removal of the head is a possible edge case → reach for a dummy/sentinel head.
  • "k sorted lists" or "always take the smallest front" → a min-heap over the current heads.
  • O(1) get/put with eviction by recency → hash map + doubly linked list (LRU).

🧠 Core idea & template

Three idioms cover this whole chapter. (1) A dummy head gives every node a predecessor, so appending and deleting need no special case for the first node. (2) Fast/slow pointers find the middle or a cycle in one pass: advance one by 1 and one by 2. (3) In-place reversal walks the list flipping each next backward, with prev accumulating the reversed prefix. Master these and most problems become a composition of two or three of them.

// (1) Dummy head — build/splice with no first-node special case
ListNode dummy = new ListNode(0);
ListNode tail = dummy;
// ... tail.next = node; tail = tail.next; ...
return dummy.next;

// (2) Fast/slow — middle (and cycle) detection in one pass
ListNode slow = head, fast = head;
while (fast != null && fast.next != null) {
    slow = slow.next;
    fast = fast.next.next;
    // if (slow == fast) -> cycle
}
// slow is now at/near the middle

// (3) In-place reversal — flip each next backward
ListNode prev = null, cur = head;
while (cur != null) {
    ListNode next = cur.next; // stash before overwriting
    cur.next = prev;
    prev = cur;
    cur = next;
}
return prev; // new head

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace

📘 Deep dive

1 Reverse Linked List LC 206 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Reverse a singly linked list and return the new head.

💡 Key insight. Walk once, flipping each node's next to point back at the node you just left. prev accumulates the reversed prefix and is the new head when cur runs off the end. The only trap is overwriting cur.next before you've saved it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Hold prev = null, cur = head.
  2. Each step: stash next = cur.next, set cur.next = prev, then slide prev = cur, cur = next.
  3. Return prev. (Recursive variant: recurse to the tail as the new head, then head.next.next = head; head.next = null on the way up.)

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass over the list 💾 Space O(1) iterative — three pointers (O(n) for the recursive stack)

⚠️ Gotchas. Save cur.next before rewriting it or you lose the rest of the list. Handle empty/single-node lists (loop simply doesn't run). In recursion, forgetting head.next = null leaves a 2-node cycle at the old head.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a prev pointer and march through the list flipping each link backward, stashing the next node first so I don't lose it. prev ends up at the old tail, which is the new head. One pass, O(1) space. I can also do it recursively, but that costs O(n) stack."
📄 ReverseLinkedList.java

2 Merge Two Sorted Lists LC 21 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Merge two sorted linked lists into one sorted list by splicing nodes.

💡 Key insight. A classic two-finger merge: at each step append the smaller front node. A dummy head removes the "is this the first node?" special case, and when one list empties you splice the entire remaining tail in a single link.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Create dummy; let tail = dummy.
  2. While both lists are non-empty, attach the smaller head (l1.val <= l2.val keeps it stable) and advance that list and tail.
  3. After the loop, tail.next = (l1 != null) ? l1 : l2 to attach the leftover.
  4. Return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(m+n) — each node is linked exactly once 💾 Space O(1) — re-links existing nodes, no copies

⚠️ Gotchas. Use <= not < to keep the merge stable and avoid dropping equal values. Don't forget the leftover-tail splice — without it you truncate the longer list. Both-empty inputs must return an empty list (dummy handles it).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I use a dummy head and a two-finger walk: compare the two front nodes, splice the smaller one on, advance. When one list runs out I attach the other in one move. It's O(m+n) time and O(1) extra space because I reuse the existing nodes."
📄 MergeTwoSortedLists.java

3 Linked List Cycle LC 141 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Return whether the linked list contains a cycle.

💡 Key insight. Floyd's tortoise and hare: move one pointer 1 step and another 2 steps per iteration. Inside a cycle the fast pointer gains one node per step on the slow one, so it must eventually lap and coincide; with no cycle, fast simply walks off the end.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Start slow = fast = head.
  2. While fast != null && fast.next != null: advance slow by 1 and fast by 2.
  3. If slow == fast, return true; if the loop exits, return false.

⏱️ Time O(n) — fast traverses at most ~2n nodes before meeting or exiting 💾 Space O(1) — two pointers, no auxiliary set

⚠️ Gotchas. Guard both fast and fast.next before the double hop or you NPE on the last node. A hash-set of visited nodes also works but costs O(n) space — interviewers want the O(1) Floyd version. Follow-up LC 142 asks for the cycle's entry node (reset one pointer to head, advance both by 1).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I run two pointers, one twice as fast as the other. If there's a loop the fast one closes the gap by one node each step and they collide; if there's no loop the fast one falls off the end. Constant space, linear time — strictly better than tracking visited nodes in a set."
📄 DetectCycle.java

4 Palindrome Linked List LC 234 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return whether a singly linked list reads the same forward and backward, ideally in O(1) space.

💡 Key insight. A list is a palindrome iff its reversed second half equals its first half. Find the middle with slow/fast, reverse the second half in place, then walk inward from both ends comparing values — no array copy, so O(1) space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Slow/fast to the middle; for even length slow lands on the first node of the second half.
  2. Reverse from slow to the end, giving prev as the reversed-half head.
  3. Walk left = head and right = prev in lockstep, bounded by the shorter reversed half; any mismatch returns false.

⏱️ Time O(n) — find-mid, reverse, and compare are each linear 💾 Space O(1) — in-place reversal, only pointers

⚠️ Gotchas. Loop on the reversed (second) half since it's the shorter side for odd lengths — the unmatched middle node is harmless. Empty/single-node lists are palindromes. Purists restore the list afterward (re-reverse the half); mention it even if you don't code it.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The simple version copies values into an array and checks two pointers — O(n) space. To hit O(1), I find the midpoint with fast/slow, reverse the back half in place, then compare it node-by-node against the front half. Three linear passes, constant space. If asked, I'd re-reverse to leave the list intact."
📄 PalindromeLinkedList.java

5 Remove Nth Node From End of List LC 19 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Remove the n-th node counting from the end and return the list head, in one pass.

💡 Key insight. Open a gap of exactly n nodes between a lead and a trail pointer, then advance both until the lead hits the last node — the trail now sits on the node just before the target. Anchoring trail at a dummy head makes removing the real head need no special case.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Make dummy.next = head; set lead = trail = dummy.
  2. Advance lead by n steps to open the gap.
  3. While lead.next != null, advance both — trail stops on the predecessor.
  4. Splice: trail.next = trail.next.next; return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(L) — one pass; the lead pointer crosses the list once 💾 Space O(1) — two pointers

⚠️ Gotchas. The dummy head is what lets you delete the first node uniformly — without it, removing head is a special case. Loop on lead.next != null (stop on the last node), not lead != null, so trail lands on the predecessor. Assumes n is valid (1 ≤ n ≤ length).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I put a dummy in front, then space two pointers n apart. When the lead reaches the end, the trailing pointer is exactly one before the node to delete, so I splice it out. The dummy means deleting the head is no different from deleting anything else. Single pass, O(1) space."
📄 RemoveNthNodeFromEnd.java

6 Add Two Numbers LC 2 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Two numbers are given as linked lists of digits in reverse order; return their sum as a linked list.

💡 Key insight. Because the least-significant digit is at the head, you add column by column straight from the heads, propagating a carry forward — exactly grade-school addition. Treating a missing node as 0 lets unequal lengths and a trailing carry fall out of the same loop.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Dummy head, carry = 0.
  2. Loop while l1 != null || l2 != null || carry != 0: sum = carry + (l1?.val) + (l2?.val).
  3. Append new ListNode(sum % 10); update carry = sum / 10; advance whichever lists exist.
  4. Return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(max(m,n)) — one column per iteration 💾 Space O(max(m,n)) — the result list (+ O(1) working state)

⚠️ Gotchas. The carry != 0 term in the loop condition is what handles a final carry (e.g. 5+5 → an extra node). Don't forget to advance only the lists that still have nodes. The trickiest variant is LC 445 (digits in forward order) — there you reverse both lists first or use stacks.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since the lists store digits least-significant-first, the heads line up like columns in long addition. I walk both at once, sum each column plus the carry, emit sum % 10, carry sum / 10. Keeping the carry in the loop condition handles different lengths and a leftover carry in one shot."
📄 AddTwoNumbers.java

7 Reorder List LC 143 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Reorder L0→L1→…→Ln into L0→Ln→L1→Ln-1→… in place.

💡 Key insight. The target order interleaves the list with its own reverse. So split at the middle, reverse the second half in place, then zip the two halves together one node at a time — no copying, no extra storage. It's a composition of all three core idioms.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Find the middle (here fast.next/fast.next.next so slow ends at the first half's tail).
  2. Sever at slow, then reverse the second half into prev.
  3. Zip: alternately splice nodes from first and the reversed second, saving each next before relinking.

⏱️ Time O(n) — find-mid, reverse, and merge are each linear passes 💾 Space O(1) — pointer surgery only

⚠️ Gotchas. Sever the first half (slow.next = null) before reversing, or you create a cycle. The split must leave first no longer than second so the zip loop terminates cleanly on second. Cache both fNext and sNext each step. Handle length ≤ 2 up front.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The result is the list interleaved with its reverse. So I find the midpoint, cut the list, reverse the back half, then weave the two halves together node by node. It's just find-middle plus reverse plus merge — all three of the standard linked-list moves — done in O(n) time and O(1) space."
📄 ReorderList.java

8 Copy List with Random Pointer LC 138 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Deep-copy a linked list where each node has an extra random pointer to any node (or null).

💡 Key insight. Avoid the O(n) hash map by weaving each clone directly after its original (A→A'→B→B'→…). Then a clone's random is just its original's-random's next, resolvable in place. A final pass unzips the two interleaved lists, restoring the original and extracting the copy.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Pass 1 — interleave: for each cur, insert clone between cur and cur.next.
  2. Pass 2 — wire randoms: cur.next.random = cur.random.next (guarding null random).
  3. Pass 3 — unzip: restore each cur.next and chain the clones onto a dummy copy list.

⏱️ Time O(n) — three linear passes 💾 Space O(1) — no map; only the cloned nodes themselves (output excluded)

⚠️ Gotchas. Step into cur.next.next when iterating the interleaved list, not cur.next. Null-check cur.random before dereferencing in pass 2. The unzip must restore the original links — leaving the lists interleaved is a common silent bug. The simpler-to-derive O(n)-space version maps original→clone in one pass, then fills next/random in a second; lead with that, then offer the O(1) weave.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The naive approach maps each original node to its clone in a hash map, then a second pass wires next and random — O(n) time and space. The slick trick is to interleave each clone right after its original so I can read a clone's random as its original-random's next, no map needed. Three passes, O(1) extra space, and I unzip at the end to restore the input."
📄 CopyListWithRandomPointer.java

9 Merge k Sorted Lists LC 23 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Merge k sorted linked lists into one sorted list.

💡 Key insight. The global minimum across all lists is always one of the k current heads. A min-heap keyed on node value surfaces that minimum in O(log k): pop it, append it, push its successor. The heap never holds more than k nodes.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed a min-heap (PriorityQueue comparing val) with every non-empty head.
  2. Pop the smallest, append to the result tail, and if it has a successor, push that.
  3. Repeat until the heap empties; return dummy.next.

⏱️ Time O(N log k) — N total nodes, each pushed/popped once at O(log k) 💾 Space O(k) — heap holds at most one node per list

⚠️ Gotchas. Only seed non-empty heads or you'll NPE. Empty input array and arrays of all-null lists must return an empty list. The alternative — divide-and-conquer pairwise merge — is also O(N log k) time but O(1) extra space; mention it as the trade-off. Naively merging one-by-one is O(Nk).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The next node in the output is always the smallest among the k list fronts, so I keep those fronts in a min-heap. Each pop is the global minimum; I append it and push its successor. That's O(N log k) since every node enters and leaves the heap once. Alternatively I can pairwise-merge the lists in a tournament for the same time bound with O(1) space."
📄 MergeKSortedLists.java

10 LRU Cache LC 146 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Design a fixed-capacity cache with O(1) get and put that evicts the least-recently-used key on overflow.

💡 Key insight. A hash map gives O(1) lookup by key; a doubly linked list gives O(1) reordering by recency. Pair them — the map value is the list node — so both get and put touch only a constant number of links. Sentinel head/tail nodes erase every null-edge case.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Doubly linked list with sentinel head (MRU side) and tail (LRU side); Map<key, Node>.
  2. get: miss → -1; hit → moveToFront(node) and return its value.
  3. put: existing key → update value + move to front; new key → if at capacity, unlink tail.prev and remove it from the map, then add the fresh node at the front.

⏱️ Time O(1) — each op is a map lookup plus a fixed number of relinks 💾 Space O(capacity) — at most capacity entries across map and list

⚠️ Gotchas. Store the key inside the node so eviction can map.remove(lru.key) — a list-only node can't find its map entry. Sentinels mean addToFront/unlink never touch null. Update an existing key's value and bump recency. LinkedHashMap with removeEldestEntry is the library shortcut — know the hand-rolled version cold, since interviewers usually ban it.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I need O(1) lookup and O(1) recency updates, so I combine a hash map keyed by the cache key with a doubly linked list ordered by recency, where the map points straight at the list node. On access I unlink the node and move it to the front; on insert past capacity I drop the tail and evict its key. Sentinel head and tail nodes keep the relinking branch-free."
📄 LRUCache.java

🔁 Practice plan

Drill in the table's order — the three Easies (206, 21, 141) install the dummy-head, two-finger-merge, and fast/slow idioms you'll reuse everywhere. Then 234/19/143 layer them together; 138, 23, and 146 are the design-flavored payoffs. From a blank file, re-derive the iterative reversal and the fast/slow middle without looking — they're the backbone of half this chapter. Rehearse these Google-favorite follow-ups out loud: LC 142 (cycle entry node), LC 445 (add two numbers, forward order), and LRU → LFU (LC 460).

  • ✅ I can explain why a dummy head removes the head-deletion special case.
  • ✅ I can prove the fast/slow pointers must meet inside a cycle, and find the cycle's entry.
  • ✅ I can reverse a list both iteratively (O(1)) and recursively (O(n) stack) and state the trade-off.
  • ✅ I can compose find-mid + reverse + merge for Reorder List and Palindrome without losing a node.
  • ✅ I can copy the random-pointer list both with a hash map and with the O(1) interleave-and-unzip trick.
  • ✅ I can build LRU from a hash map + doubly linked list and justify O(1) per operation, including why the node stores its key.

🌳 Trees

Binary-tree problems are about choosing the right traversal and then carrying exactly the right state across it: a BFS queue when you reason by level, a post-order DFS when a node's answer depends on its children, and bounds threaded top-down when validity is a global property. This family is high-ROI for Google L6 because it rewards a clean recursive contract ("what does this call return, and what does it leave behind?") over memorized code — the exact judgment interviewers probe.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 9 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Per level", "right side", "top view", "zigzag", "depth" → BFS with a size snapshot per level.
  • A node's answer is built from its children's answers (height, max path, LCA, flatten) → post-order DFS returning a value up the stack.
  • "Is this a valid BST / does ordering hold globally?" → thread a (low, high) bound interval downward, not a local parent comparison.
  • "Distance / directions / common ancestor of two nodes" → root-to-target paths or a split-point post-order search.
  • "Reconstruct / encode the tree" → pre-order with explicit null markers makes the shape self-delimiting.
  • Repeatedly stripping leaves / grouping by height → one post-order pass bucketing by height-from-bottom beats O(n²) re-pruning.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two skeletons cover almost everything here. (1) Level-order BFS: a queue holds exactly the current frontier, so freezing queue.size() before draining gives that level's node count without storing depth per node. (2) Post-order DFS with a return contract: each call returns a summary of its subtree (a height, a best downward path, a "did I find a target?" flag), and the parent combines the two child summaries. Pin down what the recursion returns versus what it records globally and most tree problems collapse to a few lines.

// (1) Level-order BFS: size snapshot groups nodes by depth
Queue<TreeNode> q = new LinkedList<>();
if (root != null) q.offer(root);
while (!q.isEmpty()) {
    int size = q.size();                 // freeze the frontier count
    for (int i = 0; i < size; i++) {
        TreeNode node = q.poll();
        // ... use node for this level ...
        if (node.left  != null) q.offer(node.left);
        if (node.right != null) q.offer(node.right);
    }
}

// (2) Post-order DFS: return a subtree summary, fold a global answer
int dfs(TreeNode node) {
    if (node == null) return BASE;       // e.g. 0 or -1
    int left  = dfs(node.left);
    int right = dfs(node.right);
    global = combine(global, node, left, right);   // answer that "turns" at node
    return contribution(node, left, right);        // what a parent may extend
}
