📘 Deep dive

1 Binary Tree Level Order Traversal LC 102 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the node values grouped level by level, top to bottom, left to right.

💡 Key insight. At any moment a BFS queue holds exactly the nodes of one frontier. Snapshotting queue.size() before you start draining tells you how many nodes belong to the current level, so you can group output without ever storing a depth on each node.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Push the root (if non-null) and loop while the queue is non-empty.
  2. Capture size = queue.size() and start a fresh level list of that capacity.
  3. Poll exactly size nodes; append each value to the level list and enqueue its non-null children.
  4. Add the completed level list to the result and repeat.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node is enqueued and dequeued exactly once 💾 Space O(n) — the queue holds up to one full level (~n/2 leaves at peak)

⚠️ Gotchas. Guard the empty tree (return an empty list, not a list containing an empty list). You must read size into a variable before the inner loop — calling queue.size() in the loop condition is wrong because the queue grows as you enqueue children of the current level.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "BFS naturally visits in level order, but a plain queue loses the level boundaries. The trick is that the queue, right after finishing a level, contains precisely the next level — so I freeze its size, drain exactly that many nodes into one group, and enqueue their children for the following round. That's O(n) time and O(n) space for the widest level."
📄 BinaryTreeLevelOrderTraversal.java

2 Binary Tree Right Side View LC 199 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Standing to the right of the tree, return the values you can see from top to bottom — the rightmost node at each depth.

💡 Key insight. Do a DFS that recurses right before left. Then the first time the recursion arrives at any new depth, that node is exactly the rightmost one at that depth. Tracking "is this a new depth?" is just depth == result.size().
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Recurse from the root carrying a depth counter and the shared result list.
  2. If depth == view.size(), this is the first node reaching this depth — append its value.
  3. Recurse into node.right first (so the right side wins each depth), then node.left.
  4. Left-side nodes only fill in depths the right side never reached (deeper-left subtrees).

⏱️ Time O(n) — every node is visited once 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The visible node at the deepest level can come from the left subtree when the right subtree is shorter — the right-first ordering handles this automatically, but it trips people who assume "rightmost child always". A BFS-last-of-level solution also works and is O(n)/O(n); the right-first DFS is more elegant at O(h) space.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I want the first node seen at each depth from the right. If I DFS visiting the right child first, the very first arrival at a depth is guaranteed rightmost. I record a value only when the current depth equals the result size, which means I haven't filled that depth yet. Left subtrees then back-fill any depths the right side couldn't reach. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 BinaryTreeRightSideView.java

3 Validate Binary Search Tree LC 98 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Decide whether a binary tree satisfies the BST property: every node's value is greater than all values in its left subtree and less than all in its right subtree.

💡 Key insight. BST validity is global, not local. A node must be greater than every ancestor it sits to the right of and less than every ancestor it sits to the left of. Thread an open interval (low, high) down the recursion: going left tightens high to the parent's value, going right tightens low.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Call validate(root, null, null) — null bounds mean unbounded, which also dodges Integer overflow at the extremes.
  2. At each node, fail if node.val <= low or node.val >= high (open interval, so equal values are rejected).
  3. Recurse left with (low, node.val) and right with (node.val, high).
  4. A null node is vacuously valid.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one comparison per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The classic bug is checking only node.left.val < node.val < node.right.val locally — a deep right-descendant smaller than the root passes that check but violates the BST. Use Integer (nullable) for bounds rather than Integer.MIN_VALUE/MAX_VALUE sentinels so a node holding exactly Integer.MIN_VALUE is handled. An in-order traversal that checks strict increase is an equally valid alternative.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The naive parent-child check is insufficient because the constraint is inherited from the entire root path, not just the immediate parent. So I carry a valid open range down: every left turn lowers the ceiling, every right turn raises the floor, and each node must fall strictly inside its inherited window. Null bounds avoid overflow. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 ValidateBinarySearchTree.java

4 Lowest Common Ancestor of a Binary Tree LC 236 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given two nodes p and q in a binary tree (not a BST), return their lowest common ancestor — the deepest node that has both as descendants.

💡 Key insight. The LCA is the deepest node from which p and q descend into different subtrees, or a node that is itself one target with the other below it. A post-order search returns "I found p, q, or the LCA in my subtree"; the node where both the left and right searches come back non-null is exactly that split point.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Base case: if the node is null or equals p or q, return it (you've hit a target or bottomed out).
  2. Recurse into the left and right subtrees.
  3. If both sides return non-null, p and q split here — this node is the LCA.
  4. Otherwise return whichever side is non-null (both targets, or neither, lie on one side).

⏱️ Time O(n) — each node visited once 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. A node can be its own ancestor: if p is an ancestor of q, the early return at root == p correctly yields p. This assumes both nodes are guaranteed present; if existence isn't guaranteed you'd need an extra found-count check before trusting the result. The comparison is by node identity here, not by value.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I post-order DFS and have each call report whether it saw either target below it. If a node hears 'yes' from both its left and right, the two targets first meet at that node, so it's the LCA. If only one side reports a hit, the answer is somewhere on that side, so I bubble it up. The early return when a node is a target handles the ancestor-of-itself case. O(n) time, O(h) stack."
📄 LowestCommonAncestor.java

5 Flatten Binary Tree to Linked List LC 114 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Flatten the tree in place into a "linked list" that follows pre-order: every left child becomes null and the right pointer chains the pre-order sequence.

💡 Key insight. In pre-order a node is immediately followed by its entire left subtree, then by whatever used to follow it (its right subtree). So for each node with a left child, find that left subtree's rightmost node (its in-order tail), graft the current right subtree onto it, then slide the left subtree over to the right. This Morris-style rewiring needs only O(1) extra space.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Walk a curr pointer down the tree.
  2. If curr.left exists, descend the left subtree's right spine to its rightmost node.
  3. Attach curr.right to that rightmost node, move curr.left onto curr.right, and null out curr.left.
  4. Advance curr = curr.right down the now-linear chain and repeat.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each edge is traversed a constant number of times 💾 Space O(1) — pure pointer rewiring, no recursion or explicit stack

⚠️ Gotchas. You must reattach the old right subtree to the rightmost node of the left subtree, not just the left child, or you'll drop nodes. The simpler recursive / reverse-pre-order (visit right, left, prepend) solution is correct too but uses O(h) stack — the interviewer's follow-up is usually "can you do it in O(1) space?", which is this Morris approach.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Pre-order says left subtree comes right after the node, then the right subtree. For each node with a left child I splice the right subtree onto the tail of the left subtree — its rightmost node — then promote the left subtree to be the new right and clear the left pointer. Marching down the resulting right spine flattens everything in O(n) time and O(1) space, no stack needed."
📄 FlattenBinaryTreeToLinkedList.java

6 Find Leaves of Binary Tree LC 366 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Repeatedly collect and remove all current leaves until the tree is empty; return the groups in removal order.

💡 Key insight. The round in which a node gets stripped equals its height from the bottom: leaves are height 0, and any node leaves on round h = 1 + max(child heights). Compute that height bottom-up in one post-order pass and bucket each node by it directly — no repeated re-pruning.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Post-order recurse; a null child returns height -1 so a true leaf computes height 0.
  2. Compute h = 1 + max(height(left), height(right)).
  3. If h == result.size(), this is the first node seen at that height — append a new bucket.
  4. Add the node's value to result.get(h) and return h upward.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single post-order visit per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack (output excluded), h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The naive "find leaves, delete, repeat" is O(n²) on a degenerate tree — the height-bucketing reframing is the whole point and the reason this is a signature question. Returning -1 for null (not 0) is what makes leaf height come out as 0; mixing this up shifts every bucket. The result index is the height, so buckets are naturally ordered.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of literally peeling leaves layer by layer, I notice a node's removal round is just its height above the deepest leaf. One post-order pass gives every node its height, and I drop each node into the bucket indexed by that height — creating the bucket the first time I reach a new height. That turns an O(n²) simulation into a single O(n) traversal."
📄 FindLeavesOfBinaryTree.java

7 Step-By-Step Directions From a Binary Tree Node to Another LC 2096 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given a tree with unique values, return the shortest path of moves ('L', 'R', 'U') from the node valued startValue to the node valued destValue.

💡 Key insight. The shortest route climbs up to the lowest common ancestor, then descends. Build both root-to-target direction strings; their shared prefix is precisely the path down to the LCA. Strip that common prefix: the leftover of the start-path becomes all 'U' (climb), and the leftover of the dest-path is appended verbatim (descend) — no explicit LCA node needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. DFS once to record the root→start path and once for root→dest, appending 'L'/'R' and backtracking on dead ends.
  2. Compute the length of the longest common prefix of the two strings.
  3. Emit 'U' repeated (startLen - common) times to walk up from start to the LCA.
  4. Append the dest-path's suffix from common onward to descend to the target.

⏱️ Time O(n) — two root-to-target DFS passes 💾 Space O(h) — path buffers and recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The path-builder reuses one StringBuilder slot: it sets 'L', and on left-failure overwrites that same character with 'R' before trying right, then deletes on full backtrack — get the slot bookkeeping wrong and paths corrupt. All start-side steps become 'U' regardless of whether they were 'L' or 'R' originally, since climbing is direction-agnostic. Values are unique, which is what lets a value-targeted DFS work.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Any node-to-node path in a tree goes up to the LCA then down. I find the root paths to both endpoints as strings of L/R. Their common prefix is the descent into the LCA, so I discard it. Whatever remains of the start path I replace with that many 'U' moves to climb to the LCA, then I tack on the remaining dest path to go down. Two DFS passes, O(n) total."
📄 StepByStepDirections.java

8 Binary Tree Maximum Path Sum LC 124 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the maximum sum over all non-empty paths, where a path is any sequence of connected nodes and need not pass through the root.

💡 Key insight. Every maximal path has a single highest node where it "turns". At that apex the path sum is node.val + bestLeftDownward + bestRightDownward. A post-order pass returns the best straight downward path a parent can extend (one side only), while separately folding the turning sum into a global max. Clamping each child's contribution at 0 means "skip a branch that only hurts".
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Define gain(node) = the largest sum of a downward path starting at node.
  2. Compute left = max(gain(node.left), 0) and right = max(gain(node.right), 0) (drop negatives).
  3. Update the global best with node.val + left + right — the path that turns at this node.
  4. Return node.val + max(left, right), since a parent can only extend through one child.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one visit per node 💾 Space O(h) — recursion stack, h the tree height

⚠️ Gotchas. The value you return (single-branch extension) differs from the value you record (two-branch turn) — conflating them is the canonical mistake. Initialize the global best to Integer.MIN_VALUE, not 0, so all-negative trees (e.g. a single -3) return the least-negative node rather than 0. Clamping at 0 is what lets the algorithm ignore harmful subtrees.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I think of each path as turning at one topmost node. At that node the best path is its value plus the best downward gain from each side, where I clamp negative gains to zero to skip them. That turning value updates a global maximum. But to my parent I can only hand back a straight path through one child, so I return value plus the larger side. Single O(n) post-order pass, O(h) stack, and I seed the answer with negative infinity for all-negative inputs."
📄 BinaryTreeMaximumPathSum.java

9 Serialize and Deserialize Binary Tree LC 297 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Design serialize and deserialize so any binary tree can be encoded to a string and rebuilt identically.

💡 Key insight. A pre-order walk that emits an explicit marker (#) for every null pins down the shape uniquely. The first token is always the root, and each subtree is a self-delimiting prefix — so deserialize can rebuild by consuming tokens in the same root→left→right order, with no separate in-order traversal required.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Serialize: pre-order recurse; for null append "#,", otherwise append the value plus separator, then recurse left and right.
  2. Deserialize: split on the separator into a token iterator.
  3. Read one token: if it's # return null; else create the node and recurse to build left then right from the next tokens.
  4. The shared pre-order ordering makes serialize and deserialize mirror images.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one append / one token consumed per node and null slot 💾 Space O(n) — the output string (or token list) plus recursion stack

⚠️ Gotchas. Without null markers the shape is ambiguous (pre-order alone can't reconstruct a general binary tree). Negative values and multi-digit numbers work because tokens are split on the separator, not by character — don't try to read fixed-width digits. An empty tree must round-trip (serialize to just "#," and back to null). Level-order (BFS) encoding is an equally accepted alternative.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I serialize with a pre-order traversal, writing a sentinel for every null so the structure is unambiguous. Because pre-order lists the root first and each subtree forms a contiguous self-delimiting block, deserialize just walks the tokens in the same order: a sentinel means an empty child, otherwise build the node and recursively fill its left then right from the remaining tokens. Both directions are O(n)."
📄 SerializeAndDeserializeBinaryTree.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the list in the order above — it climbs from the two foundational traversals (BFS level-order, DFS-by-depth) to the post-order "return-a-summary" family, then the two Hards. From a blank file, re-derive: the BFS size-snapshot loop (102), the (low, high) bound recursion (98), the post-order split-point for LCA (236), and the two-value return-vs-record split in max-path-sum (124) — that last one is the single best discriminator of recursion fluency. Rehearse these Google-favourite follow-ups out loud: "flatten in O(1) space" (114, Morris), "serialize with level-order instead" (297), and "what changes if the tree is a BST?" for LCA (236, you can walk down by value comparison in O(h)).

  • ✅ I can explain why a queue's size at the top of the loop equals the current level's node count.
  • ✅ I can explain why local parent-child checks fail for BST validity and how bounds fix it.
  • ✅ I can explain why the LCA is the post-order node whose left and right searches both return non-null.
  • ✅ I can articulate the difference between what max-path-sum returns versus what it records globally.
  • ✅ I can re-derive Find Leaves as height-from-bottom bucketing and say why it beats O(n²).
  • ✅ I can reconstruct a tree from a pre-order-with-null-markers string and explain why the markers are essential.

🌲 Tries

A trie (prefix tree) stores a set of strings as shared root-to-node paths, turning prefix queries into a single walk down character edges. It is high-ROI for Google L6 because the pattern shows up whenever a problem hinges on shared prefixes — autocomplete, dictionary matching, multi-word grid search — and because pairing a trie with a grid DFS is a clean, interview-favorite way to collapse repeated work. The same "store a future gate in a map" discipline also powers the streaming rate-limiter design that frequently rides alongside this topic.

Source: Prep Tracker tab · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The problem talks about prefixes, startsWith, autocomplete, or "does any word begin with…" — the defining trie use case.
  • You must match many words at once against some text or grid; searching each independently re-walks shared prefixes.
  • A 2-D board where you collect all dictionary words formable by adjacent cells → trie + backtracking DFS with prefix pruning.
  • Fixed small alphabet (e.g. 26 lowercase letters) makes a TrieNode[26] array of children natural and cache-friendly.
  • A streaming/timestamped "allow this only once per window" design — a hash-map gate is the trie-chapter cousin of these structural problems.

🧠 Core idea & template

A trie node owns one child slot per alphabet symbol plus a flag (or stored word) marking where an inserted word ends. Insert walks the string, creating missing children, and marks the terminal node. Search/startsWith walk the same path; reaching the end means the prefix exists, and the terminal flag distinguishes a real word from a path that's merely a prefix. The big win in grid problems: the trie's edges tell you exactly which next characters are still worth exploring, so a DFS branch dies the instant it stops being a prefix of any word.

class TrieNode {
    TrieNode[] children = new TrieNode[26];
    boolean isWord;            // (or: String word; at the terminal node)
}

void insert(TrieNode root, String w) {
    TrieNode node = root;
    for (int i = 0; i < w.length(); i++) {
        int c = w.charAt(i) - 'a';
        if (node.children[c] == null) node.children[c] = new TrieNode();
        node = node.children[c];
    }
    node.isWord = true;       // mark the terminal node
}

// Follow the path for s; null if it breaks. search() also checks isWord.
TrieNode walk(TrieNode root, String s) {
    TrieNode node = root;
    for (int i = 0; i < s.length(); i++) {
        int c = s.charAt(i) - 'a';
        if (node.children[c] == null) return null;
        node = node.children[c];
    }
    return node;
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

📘 Deep dive

1 Implement Trie (Prefix Tree) LC 208 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Implement a trie supporting insert, search (exact word), and startsWith (any word with this prefix).

💡 Key insight. A prefix tree where each node owns 26 child slots, one per lowercase letter, so a word is a root-to-node path. An isWord flag marks where a complete inserted word ends — that flag is exactly what separates a real word from a path that's merely a prefix.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. insert: walk the word, allocating any missing child, then set isWord = true on the terminal node.
  2. search: walk the word; return node != null && node.isWord.
  3. startsWith: walk the prefix; the path simply existing (walk != null) is enough.
  4. A shared private walk helper follows a string and returns the final node or null.

⏱️ Time O(L) per op — one step per character of length L 💾 Space O(total characters inserted) — up to 26 children per node

⚠️ Gotchas. The whole point of isWord: after inserting "apple", search("app") must be false while startsWith("app") is true. Don't conflate the two. Convert chars with ch - 'a' and assume the stated alphabet. For arbitrary/Unicode keys, swap the array for a Map<Character, Node>.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I model each node as 26 child links plus a boolean for whether a word ends here. Insert walks the string creating nodes as needed and flags the last one; search walks and checks that flag; startsWith just checks the path exists. Every operation is linear in the key length, independent of how many words I've stored."
📄 ImplementTrie.java

2 Logger Rate Limiter LC 359 ↗ Easy ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Print each unique message at most once every 10 seconds; shouldPrintMessage(timestamp, message) returns whether it's allowed now.

💡 Key insight. A message is allowed iff at least 10 seconds have passed since it last printed. Instead of storing the last-printed time, store the earliest timestamp it may print again (lastPrinted + 10); then the check is one comparison and the update is one map write. Timestamps arrive non-decreasing, so the stored gate is always a valid future bound.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep Map<String, Integer> nextAllowed.
  2. Look up the message; if present and timestamp < allowedAt, return false (inside cooldown).
  3. Otherwise set nextAllowed[message] = timestamp + 10 and return true.

⏱️ Time O(1) per call — one hash lookup, at most one insert 💾 Space O(D) — one entry per distinct message ever printed

⚠️ Gotchas. The boundary is "exactly 10s later is allowed": store +10 and reject only when timestamp < allowedAt (strict <), so a message gated until 11 prints at 11, not 12. Storing the next-allowed time (vs. last-seen) saves an addition per call and reads more clearly. Space grows unboundedly with distinct messages — for an infinite stream you'd want a sliding window / eviction, a great follow-up to raise.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "For each message I remember the next timestamp it's allowed to print — last time plus ten. A call is allowed if its timestamp has reached that gate, in which case I push the gate forward. It's O(1) per call. The catch is the boundary at exactly ten seconds, and the fact that memory grows with the number of distinct messages, which I'd bound with eviction if the stream were unbounded."
📄 LoggerRateLimiter.java

3 Word Search II LC 212 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a 2-D board of letters and a dictionary, return every word formable by a path of adjacent (up/down/left/right) cells, no cell reused within a word.

💡 Key insight. Searching each word independently re-walks the same grid prefixes over and over. Instead, build ONE trie of all words and DFS the grid a single time: the trie's edges say which characters are still worth pursuing, so a path dies the moment it stops being a prefix of any word. Storing the full word at its terminal node makes collection O(1), and nulling that word after capture deduplicates words reachable by multiple paths.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build a trie from words, storing the full word string at each terminal node.
  2. Start a DFS from every cell, descending the trie by the cell's letter; return immediately if there's no matching child (prefix prune).
  3. When a node carries a word, add it to the result and null it out (dedupe).
  4. Mark the cell '#' before recursing into the 4 neighbours; restore it afterward (backtrack).

⏱️ Time O(M·N·4·3^(L-1)) — each cell seeds a DFS bounded by trie depth L, branching to 3 unused neighbours after the first step 💾 Space O(total characters in words) for the trie, plus O(L) recursion depth

⚠️ Gotchas. The trie prune is what makes this tractable — without it you'd DFS blindly. Mark visited in place ('#') and always restore on the way out, or sibling paths break. Null the captured word so the same word found via two paths isn't reported twice. Storing the whole word at the terminal node avoids reconstructing it from the path. Optional optimization: prune dead trie leaves after capture to speed later cells.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Running Word Search I per word repeats the same prefix walks, so I put all the words in one trie and walk the grid once. At each cell I descend the trie by that letter; if there's no edge, the prefix can't extend any word, so I stop. When I hit a node that ends a word I record it and clear it to avoid duplicates. I mark cells visited in place and restore them on backtrack. The trie turns a pile of independent searches into one pruned DFS."
📄 WordSearchII.java

🔁 Practice plan

Build LC 208 from a blank file first — the insert/walk helper and the isWord-vs-prefix distinction are the foundation everything else reuses. Then do LC 359 as a palate-cleanser on the "store a future gate" idea, being precise about the boundary. Finish with LC 212, re-deriving the trie-backed DFS and articulating exactly where the prefix prune fires. Rehearse these Google-favorite follow-ups out loud: LC 211 (Add and Search Word, with . wildcards forcing a branching DFS through the trie), LC 648 (Replace Words — shortest-root replacement), and the streaming-eviction variant of the rate limiter.

  • ✅ I can explain why search needs the isWord flag but startsWith does not.
  • ✅ I can state each trie operation's cost as O(L) and why it's independent of the number of stored words.
  • ✅ I can justify storing the next-allowed timestamp in the rate limiter and nail the exactly-10s boundary.
  • ✅ I can describe how a trie prunes the Word Search II DFS and where a branch is abandoned.
  • ✅ I can explain the in-place visited marking and why the captured word is nulled to dedupe.
  • ✅ I can adapt the array-of-26 children to a hash-map of children for arbitrary alphabets.

⛰️ Heap / Priority Queue

Heaps shine whenever you need the smallest or largest element of a changing set without fully sorting it — "top-k", "k closest", "running median", "sliding extreme." The Google L6 signal is knowing when a bounded heap (size k) beats a full sort, when twin heaps beat one, and when a monotonic deque or ordered multiset is the sharper tool than a literal PriorityQueue.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 5 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Top k", "k closest", "k largest/smallest" — a bounded heap of size k gives O(n log k) instead of O(n log n).
  • "Median of a stream" or "balance two halves" — twin heaps (max-heap of lows, min-heap of highs).
  • "Merge k sorted lists/streams" — seed a heap with one element per source and pop in order.
  • A query repeatedly needs the current min or max as elements come and go — a heap or TreeMap multiset.
  • "Maximum/minimum over every sliding window" — a monotonic deque (often beats a heap to O(n)).
  • "Drop the k smallest and k largest" / "trimmed mean" — ordered multisets partitioned by rank.

🧠 Core idea & template

A heap gives you O(log n) insert and O(log n) extract-extreme while keeping only what you need in memory. The most reused idiom is the bounded heap: to find the k best of n, keep a heap of size k of the opposite polarity (a max-heap for the k smallest), and evict the worst whenever it overflows. What survives is exactly the answer, computed in O(n log k). Reach for twin heaps when you need the middle, and a monotonic deque when "extreme over a window" can drop the log factor.

// k smallest of n via a bounded MAX-heap (top = worst of the current best k)
PriorityQueue<T> heap =
    new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> compareDesc(a, b)); // max-heap
for (T x : items) {
    heap.offer(x);
    if (heap.size() > k) heap.poll();   // drop the worst; it can't be in the best k
}
// heap now holds the k smallest, in O(n log k) time and O(k) space

// twin heaps for a running median
PriorityQueue<Integer> low  = new PriorityQueue<>(Collections.reverseOrder()); // smaller half
PriorityQueue<Integer> high = new PriorityQueue<>();                          // larger half
// invariant: low.size() == high.size() || low.size() == high.size() + 1

📘 Deep dive

1 Top K Frequent Elements LC 347 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the k most frequent values in an array.

💡 Key insight. A frequency can never exceed n, so index buckets by frequency: bucket[f] lists every value seen exactly f times. Walking buckets from the highest frequency down and collecting until you have k yields the answer in linear time, sidestepping the O(n log k) heap route entirely.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count frequencies into a HashMap with merge(num, 1, sum).
  2. Allocate buckets[n+1]; for each value, append it to buckets[freq].
  3. Scan f from n down to 1, collecting values until you have k, then stop.

⏱️ Time O(n) — count, scatter, then one descending pass over n+1 buckets 💾 Space O(n) — frequency map plus bucket array

⚠️ Gotchas. Bucket array needs size n+1 (a value can appear all n times). Skip null buckets on the descending scan. The heap alternative — a size-k min-heap of (value, freq) evicting the least frequent — is easier to reason about but a log-factor slower; mention it, then justify buckets as the linear-time win. The problem guarantees a unique answer set, so ties at the k-boundary aren't ambiguous here.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I count frequencies, then bucket values by their count. Since no frequency exceeds n, a single high-to-low pass collects the top k in O(n) — better than the O(n log k) heap. I'd open with the heap as the obvious approach, then upgrade to bucket sort for linear time."
📄 TopKFrequentElements.java

2 K Closest Points to Origin LC 973 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return the k points nearest the origin from a list of 2-D points.

💡 Key insight. Keep a max-heap of the k closest points seen so far, keyed on squared distance — no sqrt needed since it's monotonic, so comparisons stay exact and integer-cheap. For each new point, if it's nearer than the current farthest (the heap top), swap it in. The heap stays at size k, so you never sort all n points.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build a max-heap comparing on dist² = x² + y² (use long to avoid overflow).
  2. Offer each point; whenever heap.size() > k, poll() to evict the farthest — it can't be in the closest k.
  3. Drain the heap into the result array (order within the k may vary).

⏱️ Time O(n log k) — n points, each an O(log k) heap op 💾 Space O(k) — the heap holds at most k points

⚠️ Gotchas. Compute squared distance in long; x² + y² can overflow int at coordinate extremes. A max-heap (not min-heap) is what lets you discard the worst on overflow — a min-heap would force you to insert all n first. The Quickselect alternative is O(n) average but mutates the array and degrades to O(n²) on bad pivots; the bounded heap gives a stable O(n log k) and is the safer thing to code under pressure.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep a max-heap of size k keyed on squared distance — no sqrt because it's order-preserving. Each point either gets dropped immediately or bumps out the current farthest, so I touch every point once in O(log k): O(n log k) overall, O(k) space. If they push for O(n), I'd reach for Quickselect but flag its worst-case and array mutation."
📄 KClosestPointsToOrigin.java

3 Sliding Window Maximum LC 239 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given an array and window size k, return the maximum of every contiguous window as it slides left to right.

💡 Key insight. Maintain a deque of indices whose values are strictly decreasing front-to-back; the front is always the current window's maximum. A new element evicts every smaller value from the back (they can never be the max while this larger, newer element is in window), and indices that fall off the left edge are dropped from the front. Each index enters and leaves the deque at most once, so the whole sweep is linear — better than a heap's O(n log k).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Iterate i over the array, holding a deque of indices with strictly decreasing values.
  2. If the front index has slid past the window (peekFirst() <= i - k), pollFirst().
  3. While the back's value < nums[i], pollLast() — those are dominated. Then offerLast(i).
  4. Once i >= k - 1, record nums[peekFirst()] as the current window's max.

⏱️ Time O(n) — every index is pushed and popped at most once 💾 Space O(k) — the deque holds at most one window of indices

⚠️ Gotchas. Store indices, not values, so you can tell when the front has aged out of the window. Use a strict < when evicting the back (equal values may both be valid maxima for different windows; either convention works but be consistent). Drop stale fronts before reading the max. A heap also works but lazily-deleted stale entries push it to O(n log k) — the deque is the signature O(n) answer Google looks for.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The naive scan is O(nk). I keep a monotonic decreasing deque of indices so the front is always the window max. New larger values evict smaller ones from the back; the front is popped when it slides out of range. Each index is added and removed once, giving O(n) time and O(k) space — strictly better than the heap approach."
📄 SlidingWindowMaximum.java

4 Find Median from Data Stream LC 295 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Support addNum on a stream and findMedian returning the running median at any point.

💡 Key insight. Split the stream into two halves by value: a max-heap holds the smaller half (its top is the largest of the lows) and a min-heap holds the larger half (its top is the smallest of the highs). With the two tops adjacent in sorted order, the median is either the larger heap's top (odd count) or the average of both tops (even count). A size invariant makes the median an O(1) read after each O(log n) insert.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. low = max-heap (smaller half), high = min-heap (larger half).
  2. addNum: push into low, then move low.poll() into high (so high holds genuinely larger values).
  3. Rebalance: if high.size() > low.size(), move high.poll() back to low — low may be at most one larger.
  4. findMedian: if low is bigger, return low.peek(); else return (low.peek() + high.peek()) / 2.0.

⏱️ Time addNum O(log n) — one or two heap ops; findMedian O(1) 💾 Space O(n) — every element lives in exactly one heap

⚠️ Gotchas. The push-to-low-then-spill-to-high dance guarantees correct partitioning without comparing against tops manually. Keep the invariant "low.size() equals high.size() or is exactly one greater" so the median branch is unambiguous. Average as a double (/ 2.0) to avoid integer truncation. For a follow-up where values fit a small range, a bucket/count array gives O(1) updates.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep the lower half in a max-heap and the upper half in a min-heap, balanced so their sizes differ by at most one. Each insert is one or two O(log n) heap moves; the median is then just the top of the bigger heap, or the average of the two tops — an O(1) read. Re-sorting the stream would be O(n log n) per query, so the twin-heap is the win."
📄 FindMedianFromDataStream.java

5 Finding MK Average LC 1825 ↗ Hard 🆕 Recent 2025-26

Problem. Stream of integers; calculateMKAverage looks at the last m elements, drops the k smallest and k largest, and returns the integer mean of the rest (or −1 before m elements exist).

💡 Key insight. Keep the window's m values partitioned into three ordered multisets by rank: bottom (the k smallest), mid (the middle m−2k), and top (the k largest). Each multiset caches its element count and running sum, so the answer is simply mid.sum / mid.count in O(1). The only real work is keeping the partition correct as values stream in and age out — a bounded rebalance shuffles a few boundary elements across the cuts. A TreeMap<value,count> backs each multiset for O(log n) min/max.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. addElement: append to a window deque and provisionally add the value to mid. If the window exceeds m, evict the oldest value from whichever tier holds it, then rebalance().
  2. rebalance Phase 1 (sizes): pull from mid until bottom and top each hold exactly k (or push surplus back to mid).
  3. rebalance Phase 2 (order): while bottom.max() > mid.min(), swap them; symmetrically across the mid|top boundary, so bottom ≤ mid ≤ top holds.
  4. calculateMKAverage: return -1 if fewer than m elements, else (int)(mid.sum / mid.count).

⏱️ Time addElement O(log m) — a few TreeMap ops; query O(1) — cached sum 💾 Space O(m) — the window plus three multisets over those m values

⚠️ Gotchas. When evicting an aged-out value, route it by comparing against bottom.max() / top.min() — it may live in any tier. Caching sum and count per multiset is what makes the query O(1); recomputing would be O(m). The two-phase rebalance (fix sizes, then fix order across both cut lines) keeps the partition invariant after every insert and delete. Multisets, not sets, since duplicate values are common in the stream.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I maintain three ordered multisets — bottom k, middle, top k — each caching its sum and size, so the trimmed mean is an O(1) read of the middle band. Every insert or eviction touches one multiset and then a bounded rebalance restores the rank split across the two cut lines, all in O(log m). It's essentially a streaming version of a trimmed mean built on TreeMaps."
📄 FindingMKAverage.java

🔁 Practice plan

Go in order: the first two cement the bounded-heap reflex (and the "bucket sort beats the heap" upgrade), then deliberately break away from PriorityQueue — Sliding Window Maximum (monotonic deque) and MK Average (ordered multisets) reward the candidate who knows a heap isn't always the answer. From a blank file, re-derive: the size-k max-heap eviction rule, the twin-heap balance invariant, the monotonic-deque sweep, and MK Average's two-phase rebalance. Median and MK Average are the high-signal pieces — type them cold.

  • ✅ I can explain why a size-k max-heap finds the k closest in O(n log k) and why squared distance avoids sqrt.
  • ✅ I can justify bucket sort over a heap for Top K Frequent and state both complexities.
  • ✅ I can implement Sliding Window Maximum's monotonic deque and argue it's O(n), beating the heap.
  • ✅ I can maintain the twin-heap median invariant and read the median in O(1) for odd and even counts.
  • ✅ I can reconstruct MK Average's three-multiset partition and its two-phase rebalance from scratch.
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: Kth Largest Element in an Array (LC 215) and in a Stream (LC 703), Merge k Sorted Lists (LC 23), and Sliding Window Median (LC 480) as the median + window mashup.

📅 Intervals & Line Sweep

Interval problems are about reasoning over ranges on a number line — meetings, bookings, building footprints, free slots. The two universal weapons are sort-then-sweep (sort by start, then make one linear pass where overlap can only happen between neighbours) and the ordered/heap structure (a min-heap of end times or a TreeMap of disjoint ranges) when you need to track concurrency or mutate the timeline online. This family is high-ROI for Google L6 because the same five-line idiom unlocks an entire ladder of questions, from a one-liner overlap check up to the Skyline sweep — and interviewers love to start easy and escalate.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 10 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • Input is a list of [start, end] pairs — meetings, bookings, ranges, segments.
  • Question asks "do any overlap?", "merge overlaps", "how many rooms/resources at once?", or "find the gaps".
  • You need the maximum number of simultaneously-active intervals → think min-heap of end times (or a +1/−1 sweep).
  • Online API: book / addRange / removeRange / query arriving one at a time → ordered map (TreeMap) keyed by start, navigate with floorKey/ceilingKey.
  • "Critical points where something changes" along an axis (skyline, max concurrency, +1/−1 events) → line sweep over sorted edges.
  • Times on a 24-hour clock, angles, or any wrap-around domain → sort + check the wraparound pair too.

🧠 Core idea & template

Almost every interval problem reduces to: impose an order, then exploit locality. Once intervals are sorted by start, anything that overlaps a given interval is contiguous with it, so a single left-to-right sweep that compares each interval against a running frontier (the furthest end seen so far) handles merging, gap-finding, and overlap detection. When you instead need how many overlap at once or an online timeline, swap the running scalar for a min-heap of end times or a TreeMap of disjoint ranges.

// Sort-then-sweep: the reusable skeleton behind merge / free-time / overlap.
Arrays.sort(intervals, (a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[0], b[0]));
int end = intervals[0][1];                 // running frontier (furthest end so far)
for (int i = 1; i < intervals.length; i++) {
    if (intervals[i][0] > end) {           // strict gap: no overlap with the frontier
        // ... emit a gap, or start a fresh merged run ...
        end = intervals[i][1];
    } else {
        end = Math.max(end, intervals[i][1]); // overlap/contained: extend the frontier
    }
}

// Max-concurrency variant: min-heap of end times; peak size = rooms needed.
PriorityQueue<Integer> ends = new PriorityQueue<>();
for (int[] m : sortedByStart) {
    if (!ends.isEmpty() && ends.peek() <= m[0]) ends.poll(); // a resource freed
    ends.add(m[1]);
}
int roomsNeeded = ends.size();

// Online timeline: TreeMap start->end of disjoint ranges, navigated by floorKey/ceilingKey.

📘 Deep dive

1 Meeting Rooms LC 252 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. Given meeting intervals, decide whether one person can attend all of them — i.e. whether no two overlap.

💡 Key insight. A person can attend everything iff no two meetings overlap. After sorting by start time, an overlap can only occur between adjacent meetings, so a single pass comparing each start against the previous end decides it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort the intervals by start time.
  2. Walk from index 1: if intervals[i][0] < intervals[i-1][1] (this meeting starts before its predecessor ends) return false.
  3. If the loop finishes with no conflict, return true. Touching endpoints (start == prevEnd) are allowed.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — the sort dominates; the scan is O(n) 💾 Space O(1) — in-place, ignoring sort scratch

⚠️ Gotchas. Use strict <, not <= — a meeting ending exactly when the next begins (e.g. [1,5] then [5,10]) does not conflict. An empty list is vacuously true.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force compares all pairs in O(n²). The insight is that after sorting by start, any conflict must be between neighbours, so I sort and do one linear pass checking each start against the previous end — O(n log n), dominated by the sort, O(1) extra space."
📄 MeetingRooms.java

2 Merge Intervals LC 56 ↗ Medium 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Merge all overlapping intervals and return the resulting non-overlapping set.

💡 Key insight. Once sorted by start, overlapping intervals are contiguous. The incoming interval extends the current run iff its start is <= the run's end; if so, stretch the run's end to max(end, incoming end), otherwise start a new run.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort intervals by start.
  2. Seed the output with the first interval as the current run.
  3. For each subsequent interval: if its start <= the last run's end, absorb it via last[1] = Math.max(last[1], cur[1]); else append it as a new run.
  4. Return the built list (trimmed to its true size).

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort dominates; merge sweep is O(n) 💾 Space O(n) — the output list

⚠️ Gotchas. Use <= so touching intervals ([1,4],[4,5] → [1,5]) merge. Always take the max of ends — a fully contained interval like [2,3] inside [1,4] must not shrink the run. Handle the empty input up front.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I sort by start so overlaps become adjacent. Then I sweep, keeping a current merged run; if the next interval starts at or before the run's end I extend the end to the larger of the two, otherwise I close the run and open a new one. Sort is O(n log n), the sweep O(n), output O(n)."
📄 MergeIntervals.java

3 Insert Interval LC 57 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a sorted, non-overlapping interval list and a new interval, insert it and merge as needed.

💡 Key insight. The input is already sorted and disjoint, so the answer splits into three contiguous runs and needs no sort: intervals entirely before the new one (copied), intervals that touch it (collapsed by widening the new interval's bounds), and intervals entirely after (copied).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Phase 1: while intervals[i].end < newStart, copy as-is.
  2. Phase 2: while intervals[i].start <= newEnd, absorb — set start = min(start, cur.start), end = max(end, cur.end); then append the merged interval once.
  3. Phase 3: copy every remaining interval as-is.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one linear scan, no sort 💾 Space O(n) — the output list

⚠️ Gotchas. The phase-1 boundary is strict (end < newStart) while phase-2 is inclusive (start <= newEnd) so touching intervals merge. Don't forget to emit the merged interval exactly once between phases. Empty input or a new interval beyond all others must still work.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because the list is already sorted and disjoint, I don't need a sort — just three passes: copy everything that ends before the new interval, then swallow everything that overlaps by expanding the new interval's min start and max end, then copy the rest. That's a single O(n) sweep."
📄 InsertInterval.java

4 Meeting Rooms II LC 253 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Find the minimum number of conference rooms required so that no two simultaneous meetings share a room.

💡 Key insight. Process meetings in start order, keeping a min-heap of the end times of meetings currently in progress. Before a meeting starts, free every room whose meeting has already ended (heap.peek() <= start). The heap's peak size over the sweep equals the maximum concurrency — the rooms needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort meetings by start time.
  2. For each meeting: if the heap is non-empty and its smallest end time is <= this start, poll() (reuse a freed room).
  3. Always add this meeting's end time to the heap.
  4. The final heap size = the concurrent peak = answer.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort plus n heap operations 💾 Space O(n) — heap of end times

⚠️ Gotchas. Use <= when freeing so a meeting ending exactly at another's start can hand off the room. You poll at most once per meeting (not in a loop) — that's what keeps the heap size equal to the true concurrent maximum rather than collapsing it.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I sort by start, then walk through meetings keeping a min-heap of end times for in-progress rooms. For each meeting I first reclaim one room if its earliest-ending meeting is already done, then add the new end time. The heap never shrinks below the maximum number of overlapping meetings, so its final size is the answer — O(n log n)."
📄 MeetingRoomsII.java

5 Minimum Time Difference LC 539 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a list of "HH:MM" time points, return the smallest minute-difference between any two, on a 24-hour clock.

💡 Key insight. Convert each time to minutes-since-midnight (0..1439) and sort. The closest pair is either two adjacent sorted values or the wraparound pair (last and first), whose circular distance is 1440 − gap. With more than 1440 entries a collision is forced by pigeonhole, giving answer 0 — and the sort handles that automatically.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Parse each "HH:MM" into h*60 + m minutes.
  2. Sort the minute values.
  3. Scan adjacent pairs tracking the smallest gap.
  4. Also compute the wraparound gap 1440 - last + first and take the overall minimum.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort dominates the parse and scan 💾 Space O(n) — the minutes array

⚠️ Gotchas. The wraparound pair is the classic miss — "23:59" and "00:00" are 1 minute apart, not 1439. Duplicate times give a 0 difference (and they sort adjacently). You could early-return 0 when n > 1440 by pigeonhole, but it isn't required for correctness.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I normalize every time to minutes-after-midnight and sort. The minimum difference is between adjacent sorted times, except I must also consider the clock wrapping from the latest time back to the earliest, which is 1440 minus their direct gap. Sorting makes it O(n log n)."
📄 MinimumTimeDifference.java

6 My Calendar I LC 729 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Implement book(start, end) for half-open events [start, end), returning true and storing the event only if it doesn't double-book.

💡 Key insight. Keep events in a TreeMap keyed by start time. A new [start, end) conflicts iff it overlaps the neighbour immediately before it (floorKey's end > start) or the neighbour immediately after it (ceilingKey's start < end). Only those two neighbours can collide, and each is reachable in O(log n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. prev = floorKey(start): if it exists and calendar.get(prev) > start, reject.
  2. next = ceilingKey(start): if it exists and next < end, reject.
  3. Otherwise put(start, end) and return true.

⏱️ Time O(log n) per book — two TreeMap navigations 💾 Space O(n) — one entry per event

⚠️ Gotchas. Intervals are half-open, so touching at a boundary is allowed — compare with strict > / <, not >= / <=. Checking only the floor and ceiling neighbours is sufficient because existing events are already non-overlapping; you don't need to scan the whole map.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I store bookings in a TreeMap by start time. To test a new booking I look at just two neighbours — the one starting at or before it and the one starting at or after it — and check for overlap with each. Both lookups are logarithmic, so each booking is O(log n)."
📄 MyCalendarI.java

7 Meeting Rooms III LC 2402 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. With n rooms, assign each meeting (in start order) the lowest-indexed free room, delaying a meeting if all rooms are busy; return the index of the room that hosted the most meetings (lowest index on ties).

💡 Key insight. Two heaps drive the greedy: available holds idle room indices (lowest wins), and busy holds (freeTime, index) ordered by when each room frees. For each meeting, first release every room with freeTime <= start; if a room is free take the lowest index and keep the meeting's real length, otherwise wait for the earliest-freeing room and shift the meeting later so it still runs its full duration.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort meetings by start; seed available with all room indices; keep a count[] per room.
  2. For each meeting, pop from busy into available every room whose freeTime <= start.
  3. If available is non-empty: take the lowest index, count[room]++, push (end, room) to busy.
  4. Else pop the earliest-freeing room, count[room]++, and push (earliestFree + duration, room) — the delayed meeting keeps its full length.
  5. Return the room with the max count, lowest index breaking ties.

⏱️ Time O(n log n + n log rooms) — sort plus heap ops 💾 Space O(n + rooms) — heaps and counts

⚠️ Gotchas. Use long for times — delayed meetings can push free-times past int range. The delayed meeting's new end is roomFreeTime + (end − start), not the original end. The available heap must order by index (not insertion) so the lowest-index tie-break holds; the busy heap breaks ties on index too. Final tie-break also keeps the lower index.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I greedily assign meetings in start order. Two heaps: free rooms by index, and busy rooms by free-time. Before each meeting I reclaim every room that's done. If one's free I take the lowest index; if not, I delay the meeting until the soonest room frees, preserving its duration. I tally per room and return the busiest, lowest index on ties. Heap ops make it O(n log n)."
📄 MeetingRoomsIII.java

8 Employee Free Time LC 759 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given each employee's list of busy intervals, return the finite intervals during which all employees are simultaneously free.

💡 Key insight. Common free time is exactly the gaps left after merging everyone's busy intervals into one timeline. Flatten all intervals into a single list, sort by start, then sweep tracking the furthest busy end so far; whenever the next interval starts after that frontier, the span between them is a gap nobody is busy in.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Flatten every employee's intervals into one list and sort by start.
  2. Initialize end to the first interval's end (the busy frontier).
  3. For each subsequent interval: if cur.start > end, emit the gap [end, cur.start) and reset end = cur.end; else extend end = max(end, cur.end) to swallow overlaps and nested intervals.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort over all n intervals 💾 Space O(n) — the flattened list

⚠️ Gotchas. Use strict > to detect a gap — touching intervals leave no free time. The max on end is essential for intervals fully contained in an earlier one (e.g. [2,3] inside [1,10]), which must not create a phantom gap. The result is the gaps between busy spans; the unbounded time before the first / after the last is excluded.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I ignore which employee owns which interval — free-for-all means I merge every busy interval into one timeline. I flatten and sort by start, then sweep keeping the furthest end reached; each time the next interval starts beyond that frontier I've found a shared free gap. It's the merge-intervals sweep, emitting gaps instead of runs — O(n log n)."
📄 EmployeeFreeTime.java

9 Range Module LC 715 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Support online addRange, removeRange, and queryRange over half-open intervals, keeping the tracked set as disjoint ranges.

💡 Key insight. Maintain disjoint half-open intervals in a TreeMap of start → end, and pivot every operation on floorKey — the interval that could contain or abut a boundary. addRange swallows all touching/overlapping entries into one widened interval; removeRange clips the straddling intervals at the cut boundaries and deletes everything strictly inside; queryRange is covered iff a single interval starting at or before left already reaches >= right.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. addRange(l,r): set start = floorKey(l) if its end reaches l, else l; extend r by floorEntry(r)'s end if larger; clear subMap(start, r); put(start, r).
  2. removeRange(l,r): if the interval straddling r extends past it, re-insert its tail [r, end); if the interval straddling l extends past it, trim it to [start, l); clear subMap(l, r).
  3. queryRange(l,r): start = floorKey(l); return start != null && get(start) >= r.

⏱️ Time O(log n) amortized per op — each interval is inserted/removed O(1) times overall 💾 Space O(n) — one entry per disjoint interval

⚠️ Gotchas. Half-open semantics mean abutting ranges merge in addRange (use floorEntry(right) to catch a range starting exactly at right's left edge). In removeRange, handle the right cut before the left so re-inserting the tail doesn't get clobbered, and use lowerEntry (strictly less) for the straddle checks. queryRange needs full coverage by one interval — partial coverage across two disjoint ranges still returns false.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep the tracked time as disjoint half-open intervals in a TreeMap by start. Every operation anchors on floorKey to find the relevant neighbour. Adding merges all overlapping/abutting ranges into one; removing clips the two boundary ranges and erases what's inside; querying checks that one range starting at or before left already covers through right. Each op is amortized O(log n)."
📄 RangeModule.java

10 The Skyline Problem LC 218 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given buildings as [left, right, height], output the skyline as key points [x, height] where the silhouette's height changes.

💡 Key insight. Sweep a vertical line across all critical x-coordinates (building edges), keeping a multiset of currently-active heights. A left edge inserts a height, a right edge removes one, and a key point is emitted whenever the tallest active height changes after processing an x. Encoding left edges as negative heights and sorting by (x, height) ensures that at a shared x, starts process before ends and taller starts first — which kills spurious points.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Expand each building into two edges: {left, -height} (start, negative) and {right, +height} (end).
  2. Sort edges by x, breaking ties by the signed height.
  3. Maintain a TreeMap height→count multiset, pre-seeded with 0 (ground) so the max is always defined.
  4. For each edge: a start merges its height in; an end decrements/removes one copy. After each edge, if active.lastKey() differs from the previous max, append [x, curMax] and update.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — sort plus a log-time multiset op per edge 💾 Space O(n) — edge list and active-height multiset

⚠️ Gotchas. The negative-height encoding for starts is the linchpin: at a shared x it forces all starts before ends, and among starts the tallest first — without it you emit duplicate or out-of-order points. The multiset (height→count) is needed, not a plain set, because several buildings can share a height. Seed the map with ground level 0 so a drop to nothing yields a [x, 0] key point. Adjacent equal-height buildings correctly produce no intermediate point.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I turn each building into a start and end event on the x-axis, sort them, and sweep left to right keeping a multiset of active heights with a TreeMap so the current max is the last key. A start adds a height, an end removes one; whenever the max changes I record a key point. The trick is encoding starts as negative heights so ties at the same x resolve starts-before-ends and tallest-first — O(n log n) overall."
📄 TheSkylineProblem.java

🔁 Practice plan

Climb the ladder in order: start by re-deriving Meeting Rooms and Merge Intervals from a blank file — they encode the sort-then-sweep idiom every later problem reuses. Then do Insert Interval (the no-sort three-phase scan) and Meeting Rooms II (the min-heap concurrency trick). Next master the ordered-map family — My Calendar I → Range Module — practising floorKey/ceilingKey navigation until the neighbour logic is reflexive. Finish with the two true sweeps, Employee Free Time and The Skyline Problem, saying the edge-encoding and tie-break rules out loud. Google-favourite follow-ups worth rehearsing: My Calendar II/III (overlap counting with a difference array / segment tree), Merge Intervals returning total covered length, and Meeting Rooms II via a +1/−1 sweep as an alternative to the heap.

  • ✅ I can explain why sorting by start makes overlaps adjacent, and reproduce the merge sweep blind.
  • ✅ I can argue why a min-heap of end times yields the minimum number of rooms, and code it in under five minutes.
  • ✅ I can navigate a TreeMap with floorKey/ceilingKey/lowerEntry and reason about half-open vs. closed boundary comparisons.
  • ✅ I can derive the Skyline edge encoding (negative starts) and justify each tie-break in the sort.
  • ✅ I remember the wraparound pair in Minimum Time Difference and the long overflow in Meeting Rooms III.

🔙 Backtracking

Backtracking is systematic, DFS-driven exploration of a decision tree: at each step you choose, recurse, then un-choose, pruning whole subtrees the moment they can't lead to a valid answer. It is one of the highest-ROI families for Google L6 because so many onsite problems — combinatorial enumeration, constraint satisfaction, and "generate every valid X" — reduce to the same choose/explore/un-choose skeleton, and interviewers grade you on whether you can prune intelligently and reason about the branching factor.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 9 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The prompt asks for all solutions / subsets / permutations / combinations / valid configurations, not just one or a count you can close-form.
  • Each position has a small, enumerable set of choices, and a solution is a sequence of choices (a root-to-leaf path).
  • There are constraints that can invalidate a partial path early (sum overshoot, attacked square, unbalanced parens) — i.e. pruning is possible.
  • Output size is itself exponential (2n, n!, Catalan) — so an exponential algorithm is expected and acceptable.
  • You need to build a string/board incrementally and undo, rather than recompute state from scratch each call.
  • State can be carried in mutable scratch (a path list, a StringBuilder, used[]/cols[]/diag[] flags) and restored on return.

🧠 Core idea & template

Picture a decision tree. Each node is a partial candidate; each edge is one choice. DFS down: apply a choice (mutate shared state), recurse, then reverse exactly that mutation so siblings start from a clean slate. A complete path (a leaf, or a base case like "remaining == 0") is recorded. Pruning is just an early return the instant the partial candidate can't possibly extend to a valid one. The whole craft is (1) defining the choice set per level, (2) defining "complete", and (3) cutting dead branches as early as possible.

void backtrack(State path, Choices choices, Output out) {
    if (isComplete(path)) {        // leaf / goal -> record a snapshot (deep copy!)
        out.add(copyOf(path));
        return;
    }
    if (isDead(path)) return;      // prune: this partial can never succeed

    for (Choice c : choices(path)) {
        apply(c, path);            // CHOOSE  (mutate shared state)
        backtrack(path, choices, out);   // EXPLORE
        undo(c, path);             // UN-CHOOSE (restore exactly what apply changed)
    }
}

// Two recurring "where does the loop start" idioms:
//   start at i+1  -> each element used at most once  (combinations / subsets-by-loop)
//   start at i    -> element reusable                (CombinationSum)
//   used[] flags  -> order matters, no reuse         (Permutations)

🗺️ Problems at a glance
