📘 Deep dive

1 Subsets LC 78 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given an array of distinct integers, return the powerset — every possible subset, including the empty set.

💡 Key insight. Each element is an independent binary decision: in or out. That makes the powerset a binary decision tree of height n, with exactly 2n leaves — one per subset.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Recurse on index idx. When idx == nums.length every element has been decided, so the current path is one complete subset — snapshot it.
  2. Branch exclude first: recurse to idx+1 without touching path.
  3. Branch include: path.add(nums[idx]), recurse to idx+1, then path.remove(last) to un-choose.

⏱️ Time O(n·2n) — 2n subsets, O(n) to deep-copy each into the result 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + path buffer; output not counted

⚠️ Gotchas. You must add a deep copy (new ArrayList<>(path)); adding the live path stores a reference that later mutations corrupt. The empty subset falls out naturally from the all-exclude path — don't special-case it. This include/exclude tree differs from the common loop+startIndex subset template but produces the same set (different order).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each number is independently in or out, so I model the powerset as a height-n binary tree and DFS it. At a leaf I've made all n decisions, so I record the path. There are 2n leaves and copying each costs O(n), giving O(n·2n) time and O(n) auxiliary space."
📄 Subsets.java

2 Combination Sum LC 39 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given distinct candidates and a target, return all unique combinations summing to target; each candidate may be reused unlimited times.

💡 Key insight. Allow reuse by recursing on the same index i; forbid permuted duplicates by starting the loop at start so you never revisit an earlier candidate. "Move forward or stay, never back."
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Carry remaining (target minus chosen so far). If remaining == 0, snapshot the path — exact hit.
  2. If remaining < 0, this branch overshot — return to prune.
  3. Loop i from start to end: add cand[i], recurse with start = i (not i+1) to permit reuse, then remove to un-choose.

⏱️ Time O(nT/m) — branching n, depth bounded by target/min-candidate 💾 Space O(T/m) — recursion depth + path

⚠️ Gotchas. Recursing on i+1 instead of i silently turns this into the "use each once" problem. The start index is what kills duplicate orderings like [2,3] vs [3,2] — without it you'd emit permutations. Sorting candidates lets you replace the remaining<0 check with a break for an extra constant-factor prune (the code prunes via the <0 guard).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I DFS over candidates carrying the remaining target. To allow reuse I recurse on the same index; to avoid counting [2,3] and [3,2] separately I never start the loop before the current index. I prune the moment remaining goes negative and record when it hits exactly zero."
📄 CombinationSum.java

3 Letter Combinations of a Phone Number LC 17 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given a digit string (2–9), return all letter combinations the number could spell on a phone keypad.

💡 Key insight. Each digit independently contributes exactly one letter, so a combination is one path through the digit→letters tree — a Cartesian product realized by DFS.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Map each digit to its keypad letters in a fixed array. Return empty immediately if input is empty.
  2. At depth idx, look up the letters for digits[idx] and loop over them: append to a StringBuilder, recurse to idx+1, then delete the last char.
  3. When idx == digits.length() the combination is complete — add path.toString().

⏱️ Time O(4n·n) — up to 4 letters per digit, O(n) to materialize each leaf string 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + StringBuilder

⚠️ Gotchas. Empty input must yield [], not [""] — guard it up front. Digits 7 and 9 have four letters, which is where the 4n bound comes from (2/3/4/5/6/8 have three). Using a StringBuilder with append/deleteCharAt is the clean undo; string concatenation also works but allocates per node.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's a Cartesian product over the per-digit letter sets, which I generate with DFS. At each level I pick one letter for the current digit and recurse; at depth equal to the input length I've chosen a letter for every digit, so I emit the string. With at most four choices per digit it's O(4n·n)."
📄 LetterCombinations.java

4 Generate Parentheses LC 22 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given n, generate all combinations of n pairs of well-formed (balanced) parentheses.

💡 Key insight. Enforce validity by construction with two counters: add '(' only while open < n (budget remains), and add ')' only while close < open (there's an unmatched open to pair). Every leaf is then valid — no post-hoc checking.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Track open and close counts. When path.length() == 2n, the string is fully built and balanced — record it.
  2. If open < n: append '(', recurse with open+1, undo.
  3. If close < open: append ')', recurse with close+1, undo.

⏱️ Time O(4n/√n) — the number of valid strings is the n-th Catalan number 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + path buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. The condition for ')' is close < open, NOT close < n — the latter generates invalid strings like ")(". Because the two pruning rules make every produced string valid, the base case needs no validation. The Catalan growth is why you can't do meaningfully better than enumerating the output.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rather than generate all 2^(2n) bracket strings and filter, I prune at construction: I can open while I have budget, and I can close only against an outstanding open. That guarantees balance, so every leaf of length 2n is a valid answer. The count is the n-th Catalan number, ~4n/(n√n)."
📄 GenerateParentheses.java

5 Permutations LC 46 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given an array of distinct integers, return all of its permutations.

💡 Key insight. A permutation fixes one element per position. A used[] boolean array guarantees each element appears exactly once along any root-to-leaf path; flip it on choose and off on un-choose.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. When path.size() == nums.length every slot is filled — snapshot the permutation.
  2. Loop over all indices; continue on any used[i].
  3. Else mark used[i] = true, add nums[i], recurse; then remove and set used[i] = false to restore.

⏱️ Time O(n·n!) — n! leaves, O(n) to copy each 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + path + used[]

⚠️ Gotchas. Unlike combinations there is no start index — every unused element is a candidate at every level, because order matters. Forgetting to reset used[i] = false on the way up is the classic bug; it strands elements as permanently used. For inputs with duplicates (LC 47) you'd sort and add a "skip equal sibling unless its predecessor was used" guard.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I fill positions left to right. At each position I try every element not yet used, mark it, recurse, then unmark on the way back so siblings can reuse it. There are n! complete orderings and copying each is O(n), so O(n·n!) time with O(n) extra space for the used flags and path."
📄 Permutations.java

6 Confusing Number II LC 1088 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Count integers in [1, n] that become a different valid number when the whole thing is rotated 180° (6↔9, 0/1/8 fixed; 2,3,4,5,7 are invalid).

💡 Key insight. Only numbers built solely from {0,1,6,8,9} can be rotated at all, so generate exactly those by DFS and carry the rotated value alongside the built value. Appending digit d makes value = value*10 + d, but its rotation lands at the front: rotated = rot(d)*place + rotated. Count whenever value != rotated.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. DFS over the five rotatable digits, tracking (value, rotated, place) where place is the positional weight of the next digit.
  2. Skip leading zero (value == 0 && digit == 0); prune any branch whose nextValue > n.
  3. At each node, add 1 to the count when value != 0 and value != rotated (a genuine flip).

⏱️ Time O(5L) — L = digits in n; ≤5 choices per position 💾 Space O(L) — recursion depth

⚠️ Gotchas. The rotated digit must be prepended, not appended — rotating reverses positional order, hence multiplying rot(d) by the running place and adding it on top. Use long for value/rotated/place since n can be 109. A "confusing" number must differ after rotation, so 1, 8, 88, 1001 (palindromic under rotation) do not count. The smart pruning is generating only rotatable digits rather than testing all of [1, n].
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force would test every number to 109 — far too slow. Instead I only build numbers from the five rotatable digits and maintain the rotation incrementally: a new digit appends to the value but its rotated form goes to the front of the rotated value. I count a node when value and rotated disagree, pruning as soon as the value exceeds n, giving O(5L)."
📄 ConfusingNumberII.java

7 N-Queens LC 51 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Place n queens on an n×n board so none attack another; return every distinct board configuration.

💡 Key insight. Place exactly one queen per row, so a solution is just a column choice per row. The two diagonal families have constant invariants: a "\" diagonal shares row - col, a "/" diagonal shares row + col. Encoding column + both diagonals as boolean sets makes each safety test three O(1) lookups.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Recurse by row. When row == n a queen sits safely in every row — build and record the board.
  2. For each col, compute d = row - col + (n-1) and a = row + col; skip if cols[col], diag[d], or anti[a] is occupied.
  3. Otherwise mark all three true and store queenCol[row] = col, recurse to row+1, then clear all three to un-choose.

⏱️ Time O(n!) — ≤n columns in row 0, n−1 in row 1, … with diagonal pruning 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth, three sets, placement array

⚠️ Gotchas. The row - col invariant is negative for cells above the main diagonal, so offset by n-1 to index a 0..2n−2 array. Both diagonal arrays have size 2n-1. Reset all three flags on backtrack — a partial reset leaves phantom attacks. Placing one queen per row implicitly handles the row constraint, so you never check rows.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I place one queen per row and only need to choose its column. A column is safe if its file and both diagonals are free; I track those with three boolean arrays keyed by col, row−col, and row+col, so each check is O(1). I DFS row by row, recording a board when all n rows are placed, with worst case O(n!) before diagonal pruning."
📄 NQueens.java

8 Expression Add Operators LC 282 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given a digit string and a target, insert +, -, * (or nothing) between digits so the resulting expression evaluates to target; return all such expressions.

💡 Key insight. The hard part is * precedence without a parser. Carry the value of the last operand (prev): multiplication must undo that operand's prior additive contribution, so cur = cur - prev + prev*operand and the new prev becomes prev*operand. +/- simply fold into cur and reset prev to the signed operand.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. At position idx, grow the next operand digit-by-digit in an inner loop; a multi-digit operand starting with '0' is illegal, so break after the single "0".
  2. The very first operand takes no leading operator. For later operands, try all three: +operand (cur+operand), -operand (cur−operand, prev=−operand), *operand (cur−prev+prev·operand, prev=prev·operand).
  3. At idx == length, if cur == target record the expression string. Truncate the StringBuilder back to its saved length after each branch (un-choose).

⏱️ Time O(4n) — at each of n−1 gaps: split-or-not × three operators 💾 Space O(n) — recursion depth + expression buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. Use long for cur, prev, and operand — intermediate products overflow int on long inputs even when the final target fits. The leading-zero rule means "05" is never a valid operand but "0" alone is. The cur - prev + prev*operand identity is the whole trick — write it out and sanity-check on "2+3*2" = 8 (prev was 3, so we subtract 3, add 3·2). Truncate, don't rebuild, the StringBuilder.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "At each gap I either extend the current operand or close it and pick an operator, so the branching is roughly four. The subtlety is multiplication binding tighter than the running sum: I keep the last operand's value, subtract its old contribution, and add its product. I guard against leading-zero operands and use longs to survive overflow, exploring O(4n) expressions."
📄 ExpressionAddOperators.java

9 Robot Room Cleaner LC 489 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Control a blind robot (only move/turnRight/turnLeft/clean, no coordinates) to clean every reachable cell of an unknown grid.

💡 Key insight. Impose your own coordinate frame: track (r, c) relative to the start and a dir facing. The trick that makes DFS+backtracking work without absolute positioning is the go-back maneuver: turn 180°, move one cell, turn 180° again — this restores BOTH position and original facing, so the loop can cleanly try the next direction.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Mark (r,c) visited (via an encoded long key) and clean() it.
  2. Probe all four directions in a fixed order. For each, compute the neighbor; if unvisited and move() succeeds, recurse into it, then perform goBack() to return to (r,c) facing the same way.
  3. After handling each direction, turnRight() so the next iteration's direction index lines up with the robot's actual facing.

⏱️ Time O(N) — four probes over N free cells, O(4N), each move O(1) 💾 Space O(N) — visited set + recursion depth bounded by free cells

⚠️ Gotchas. The directions array and the per-iteration turnRight() must stay in lockstep: nd = (dir + turn) % 4 assumes the robot has physically turned right turn times. The go-back must end facing the original direction or the next probe targets the wrong cell — it's two right-turns, a move, two more right-turns. Encode coordinates with an offset (here +100000) so negative relative coords stay distinct and non-negative. You can't randomly teleport, so backtracking must be a physical reversal, not a state pop.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Since the robot is blind, I overlay a relative grid: I track an (r,c) offset from the start and a facing direction. I DFS, cleaning each cell and probing four directions in a fixed rotation. The crux is undoing a move physically — turn around, step back, turn around again — which restores position and heading so the next direction is tried from a known state. It's O(N) over the free cells."
📄 RobotRoomCleaner.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the ladder easiest→hardest so the skeleton becomes muscle memory before the twists. From a blank file, re-derive in this order: Subsets (the bare choose/explore/un-choose tree) → Combination Sum (start-index vs reuse) → Permutations (used[] flags, no start index) → Letter Combinations & Generate Parentheses (string-building + prune-by-construction) → N-Queens (constraint sets + diagonal invariants) → Expression Add Operators (the cur−prev+prev·operand identity) → Robot Room Cleaner (relative frame + physical go-back). Drill the three loop idioms until you never confuse them. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Subsets II / Combination Sum II (dedup with sorted skip), Permutations II (duplicates), Word Search (grid DFS backtracking), and Sudoku Solver (N-Queens-style constraint tracking).

  • ✅ I can write the generic choose/explore/un-choose template from memory and explain when to deep-copy the path.
  • ✅ I can state which loop idiom (start=i, start=i+1, or used[]) each problem needs and why.
  • ✅ I can explain Generate Parentheses' two pruning conditions and why close < open (not close < n) is correct.
  • ✅ I can derive N-Queens' two diagonal invariants and justify the + (n-1) offset.
  • ✅ I can reproduce the cur - prev + prev*operand multiplication trick and trace it on "2+3*2".
  • ✅ I can explain Confusing Number II's "rotated digit goes to the front" recurrence and the leading-zero / palindrome edge cases.
  • ✅ I can describe the Robot Room Cleaner go-back maneuver and why backtracking must be physical.

🔲 Matrix

Grid problems split into two flavors: geometric index manipulation — traversing or transforming a 2D array in place by reasoning about coordinate mappings and boundaries — and grids as graphs, where cells are nodes and adjacency drives DP or DFS. They're high-ROI for Google L6 because the in-place ones test whether you can nail off-by-one boundary logic under pressure, and the graph ones test whether you spot that a DAG (strictly increasing values) or a unique-anchor trick removes the need for a visited set entirely.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 7 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Spiral", "layer by layer", "peel the border" → four shrinking boundaries (top/bottom/left/right).
  • "Rotate 90°", "transpose", "mirror in place" → decompose into a coordinate-mapping of transpose + row/column reversal.
  • "Set entire row/column to zero" with O(1) space → reuse row 0 and column 0 as marker storage.
  • "Count shapes / connected components" where shapes have a regular form → count a unique anchor cell (e.g. top-left) per shape, no flood fill.
  • "Longest run / line of equal values in any direction" → directional DP reading already-computed neighbours.
  • "Longest strictly increasing path" → the grid is a DAG; DFS + memoization, no visited set.
  • "Minimum-area axis-aligned rectangle from points" → hash points, treat each pair as a diagonal.

🧠 Core idea & template

For in-place geometry, write down the coordinate map (e.g. a 90° clockwise turn sends (r,c) → (c, n-1-r)) and find a sequence of in-place passes that composes to it — transpose then reverse-rows here. For boundary traversal, keep four inward-moving walls and peel one edge per step, guarding the single-row/column case. For grid-as-graph, decide whether adjacency forms a DAG (strict monotonicity ⇒ yes ⇒ memoize, skip visited tracking) or needs DFS/BFS with a visited set; directional run problems become one DP layer per direction.

// (A) Boundary peel — spiral / layer traversal
int top = 0, bottom = m - 1, left = 0, right = n - 1;
while (top <= bottom && left <= right) {
    for (int c = left; c <= right; c++) visit(top, c);     top++;
    for (int r = top;  r <= bottom; r++) visit(r, right);  right--;
    if (top <= bottom) { for (int c = right; c >= left; c--) visit(bottom, c); bottom--; }
    if (left <= right) { for (int r = bottom; r >= top; r--) visit(r, left);   left++;  }
}

// (B) Grid-as-DAG — longest strictly increasing path, memoized DFS
int dfs(int r, int c) {
    if (memo[r][c] != 0) return memo[r][c];
    int best = 1;
    for (int d = 0; d < 4; d++) {
        int nr = r + DR[d], nc = c + DC[d];
        if (inBounds(nr, nc) && grid[nr][nc] > grid[r][c])   // strict => no cycles
            best = Math.max(best, 1 + dfs(nr, nc));
    }
    return memo[r][c] = best;
}

📘 Deep dive

1 Spiral Matrix LC 54 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return all elements of an m×n matrix in spiral (clockwise, starting top-left) order.

💡 Key insight. Maintain four boundaries — top, bottom, left, right — and peel one edge per step, moving that boundary inward afterward. The loop ends when the boundaries cross. The two guards before the bottom row and left column are what prevent re-emitting cells when only a single row or column remains.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Walk the top row left→right, then top++.
  2. Walk the right column top→bottom, then right--.
  3. If top <= bottom still holds, walk the bottom row right→left, then bottom--.
  4. If left <= right still holds, walk the left column bottom→top, then left++. Repeat while the walls haven't crossed.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell is emitted exactly once 💾 Space O(1) — beyond the required output list

⚠️ Gotchas. Without the if (top <= bottom) and if (left <= right) guards, a non-square matrix that collapses to a lone middle row or column will double-emit those cells. Handle the empty matrix up front. The loop condition must be both top <= bottom and left <= right.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I keep four walls and strip the matrix one border at a time — top row, right column, bottom row, left column — shrinking the corresponding wall after each. The subtlety is the last layer: when only one row or column is left, I have to guard the bottom and left passes so I don't print those cells twice. Every cell is visited once, so O(m·n) time and O(1) extra space."
📄 SpiralMatrix.java

2 Rotate Image LC 48 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Rotate an n×n matrix 90° clockwise in place, without allocating another matrix.

💡 Key insight. A 90° clockwise rotation maps (r, c) → (c, n-1-r). That single mapping factors into two in-place passes: transpose (swap across the main diagonal, (r,c) → (c,r)) followed by reversing each row ((c,r) → (c, n-1-r)). Composing them lands every element at its rotated position using only swaps of existing cells.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Transpose: iterate the upper triangle only (c > r) and swap matrix[r][c] with matrix[c][r].
  2. Restricting to c > r avoids swapping each pair back to where it started.
  3. Reverse each row in place with a two-pointer swap (lo from the left, hi from the right).
  4. The composition equals the clockwise rotation.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — every cell is touched a constant number of times 💾 Space O(1) — all swaps happen in place

⚠️ Gotchas. Transposing over the full grid instead of just the upper triangle swaps every pair twice and yields the original matrix — the c = r + 1 start bound is essential. For counter-clockwise, reverse rows first then transpose (or transpose then reverse columns). This works only for square matrices; a non-square rotation needs a new buffer.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rather than juggling four-way corner cycles, I observe that a clockwise turn is exactly a transpose followed by reversing each row. Transpose mirrors across the main diagonal — and I only walk the upper triangle so I don't undo my own swaps — then reversing each row completes the rotation. Both passes are in place, so O(n²) time and O(1) space."
📄 RotateImage.java

3 Set Matrix Zeroes LC 73 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. If any cell is 0, set its entire row and column to 0 — in place, using O(1) extra space.

💡 Key insight. Instead of O(m+n) side arrays to record which rows/columns to zero, reuse the matrix's own first row and first column as that marker storage: a zero at (r, c) stamps matrix[r][0] and matrix[0][c]. The only conflict is cell (0,0), shared by both markers, so capture whether row 0 and column 0 originally held a zero in two boolean flags before stamping.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Scan row 0 and column 0 once to set firstRowZero / firstColZero flags.
  2. For each interior zero, stamp its row-0 and column-0 markers.
  3. Sweep the interior again, zeroing any cell whose row-marker or column-marker fired.
  4. Finally apply the two saved flags to row 0 and column 0 — done last so their marker values aren't clobbered before being read.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — a constant number of passes over the grid 💾 Space O(1) — markers live inside the matrix; only two boolean flags extra

⚠️ Gotchas. Order matters: if you zero row 0 / column 0 before using them as markers, you destroy the marker information — apply the flags last. The two flags exist precisely because cell (0,0) can't distinguish "row 0 should zero" from "column 0 should zero". Don't zero a row the moment you see a zero, or you'll cascade and wipe the whole matrix.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The naive fix uses two arrays of size m and n. To hit O(1) space I repurpose the first row and first column as those arrays — a zero at (r,c) marks row r and column c there. Because cell (0,0) overlaps both, I first remember in two booleans whether the original first row and first column had any zero, do all the interior marking and zeroing from the markers, and only at the very end zero the first row/column per those booleans. Constant extra space, O(m·n) time."
📄 SetMatrixZeroes.java

4 Battleships in a Board LC 419 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Count the battleships on a board, where each ship is a straight 1×k or k×1 run of 'X' and no two ships are adjacent.

💡 Key insight. Because ships are straight and never touch, each ship has exactly one "top-left" cell: an 'X' whose neighbour directly above and directly to the left are both not 'X' (or off-grid). Count only those cells and you count each ship once — a single pass, no marking of visited cells, board untouched.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Scan every cell; skip anything that isn't 'X'.
  2. If the cell above is 'X', this continues a vertical ship — skip.
  3. If the cell to the left is 'X', this continues a horizontal ship — skip.
  4. Otherwise it's a ship's unique top-left cell — increment the count.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — one scan of the board 💾 Space O(1) — only a counter; the board is not modified

⚠️ Gotchas. This anchor trick relies on the problem's "no two ships are adjacent" guarantee — without it, two touching ships would merge and the count breaks; you'd then need flood-fill / union-find. The bounds checks (r > 0, c > 0) handle ships flush against the top or left edge. The follow-up "do it in one pass without modifying the board" is exactly this solution.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I could flood-fill each ship, but since ships are straight and separated, every ship has a single distinguished cell — its top-left — recognizable because the cells immediately above and to its left aren't part of a ship. I tally only those, which counts each ship exactly once in one pass, O(1) space, and I never touch the board. If ships could be adjacent I'd fall back to connected-components."
📄 Battleships.java

5 Minimum Area Rectangle LC 939 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given points in the plane, find the minimum area of any axis-aligned rectangle formed from four of them, or 0 if none exists.

💡 Key insight. Any two points differing in both x and y can act as a rectangle's diagonal; that fixes the other two corners at (p1.x, p2.y) and (p2.x, p1.y). So hash all points, try every unordered pair as a diagonal, and if both implied corners exist, |dx|·|dy| is a candidate area. Encoding a point as x·40001 + y gives O(1) membership tests.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Insert every point's encoded key into a hash set.
  2. For each unordered pair (i, j), skip if they share an x or a y (that's an edge, not a diagonal).
  3. Check whether both companion corners (x1, y2) and (x2, y1) are present.
  4. If so, update the best with |x1 - x2| · |y1 - y2|; return 0 if no rectangle was ever found.

⏱️ Time O(n²) — every pair of points is examined, each check O(1) 💾 Space O(n) — the hash set of points

⚠️ Gotchas. Only pairs that differ in both coordinates are valid diagonals — skipping same-x or same-y pairs is essential or you'd compute zero-area "rectangles". The radix in the encoding must exceed the coordinate range (coordinates are in [0, 40000], so 40001 is collision-free); a too-small multiplier causes key collisions. An alternative groups points by column and looks for shared y-pairs across columns, also O(n²)-ish.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "An axis-aligned rectangle is pinned down by either diagonal. So I hash every point, then for each pair of points that differ in both x and y — a candidate diagonal — I check if the two complementary corners exist in the set. If they do, I have a rectangle and I track the smallest area. It's O(n²) pairs with O(1) lookups, O(n) space, and I encode points into a single integer to make the set fast."
📄 MinimumAreaRectangle.java

6 Longest Line of Consecutive One in Matrix LC 562 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Find the length of the longest line of consecutive 1s in the matrix, where a line may be horizontal, vertical, or along either diagonal.

💡 Key insight. A maximal line ends at some cell in one of four orientations, and the run ending at (r, c) is just 1 + the run ending at the predecessor in that direction. Four DP layers — each reading one already-computed neighbour (left, up, up-left, up-right) — yield every cell's four run lengths in a single forward sweep; the global max over all of them is the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep four tables: horiz, vert, diag, anti (all 0 where the cell is 0, since a 0 breaks every run).
  2. For a 1, set horiz[r][c] = horiz[r][c-1] + 1 and vert[r][c] = vert[r-1][c] + 1.
  3. Set diag[r][c] = diag[r-1][c-1] + 1 (up-left) and anti[r][c] = anti[r-1][c+1] + 1 (up-right), guarding the borders.
  4. Track the running maximum across all four tables.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — constant work (four transitions) per cell 💾 Space O(m·n) — four DP tables; reducible to O(n) with rolling rows

⚠️ Gotchas. The anti-diagonal reads anti[r-1][c+1], so its column guard is c < n-1, not c > 0 — mixing up which border each diagonal needs is the common bug. A single forward pass suffices only because every transition reads a neighbour already processed earlier in the same scan order. Space drops to O(n) since only the previous row is needed for the vertical and diagonal layers.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "For each of the four directions, the longest run of 1s ending at a cell extends the run ending at the neighbour behind it in that direction by one. So I maintain four DP grids and fill them in one top-to-bottom, left-to-right sweep, each cell reading an already-computed predecessor. The answer is the largest value across all four grids. O(m·n) time, and I can shrink space to one row if asked."
📄 LongestLineOfConsecutiveOne.java

7 Longest Increasing Path in a Matrix LC 329 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Find the length of the longest strictly increasing path in a matrix, moving only up/down/left/right.

💡 Key insight. Because every step must be strictly increasing, values can never repeat along a path, so the reachability graph is a DAG and no visited set is needed. The longest increasing path starting at a cell depends only on that cell, so memoize it: dfs(r,c) = 1 + max over larger neighbours of dfs(neighbour). Each cell is solved once and cached.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate a memo grid (0 = not yet computed) and take the max of dfs over every starting cell.
  2. In dfs(r,c), return the cached value immediately on a hit.
  3. Start longest = 1 (the cell itself); for each of the four neighbours with a strictly greater value, take 1 + dfs(neighbour).
  4. Cache and return the best.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell's value is computed once and reused thereafter 💾 Space O(m·n) — memo table plus recursion depth in the worst case

⚠️ Gotchas. Forgetting the memo turns this into exponential blow-up; with it, each of the m·n cells is expanded once. The strict-increase condition is what guarantees acyclicity — relax it to non-strict and you'd reintroduce cycles and need a visited set. A non-recursive alternative does a topological sort by value (peeling cells with no larger neighbour, like Kahn's algorithm), which avoids deep recursion stacks on huge grids.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I treat the grid as a graph where edges point from smaller to strictly larger neighbours. Strict increase means no cycles — it's a DAG — so I don't even need a visited set. The longest path from a cell only depends on that cell, so I memoize: a cell's answer is one plus the best answer among its larger neighbours. Every cell is computed once, giving O(m·n) time and O(m·n) for the memo. For very large grids I'd mention the topological-peel variant to avoid stack overflow."
📄 LongestIncreasingPath.java

🔁 Practice plan

Start with the three in-place geometry problems (54, 48, 73) to drill boundary and coordinate-mapping precision, then move to the graph/DP trio (419 anchor-counting, 562 directional DP, 329 memoized DAG). From a blank file, re-derive: the spiral's two collapse guards (54), the transpose-then-reverse decomposition and why transpose stays in the upper triangle (48), and the "apply first-row/col flags last" ordering in Set Matrix Zeroes (73). Rehearse these Google-favourite follow-ups out loud: "rotate 90° counter-clockwise instead" (48), "set zeroes in true O(1) space" (73, this marker trick), and "longest increasing path without recursion" (329, topological peel). The single highest-value insight to internalize is that strict monotonicity makes a grid a DAG, killing the visited set.

  • ✅ I can explain why the spiral needs guards before the bottom row and left column.
  • ✅ I can derive a 90° rotation as transpose + row-reverse and say why transpose only swaps the upper triangle.
  • ✅ I can explain why row 0 and column 0 can serve as markers and why their flags are applied last.
  • ✅ I can justify the top-left anchor count and name what breaks it (adjacent ships).
  • ✅ I can set up four-direction DP and state which border each diagonal layer must guard.
  • ✅ I can explain why strict increase makes the grid a DAG so memoized DFS needs no visited set.

🕸️ Graphs — BFS/DFS, Topological Sort, Union-Find & Grids

Graphs are the connective tissue of the L6 interview: islands, course prerequisites, account merges and rolling-ball mazes are all the same four traversals in disguise — DFS/BFS flood fill, Kahn's topological sort, union-find, and weighted shortest paths (Dijkstra/Bellman-Ford). Because almost every "real system" modelling question (dependency resolution, network reachability, grouping) reduces to one of these, fluency here is the single highest-ROI pattern family to over-prepare. The skill the interviewer is really probing is modelling — turning a word problem into nodes and edges, then picking the cheapest traversal that fits the structure.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 18 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • A 2-D grid where you must group, count, or measure distance over 4-connected cells → flood fill (DFS) or multi-source BFS.
  • "How many separate groups / components / islands?" or "are these all connected?" → connected components via DFS or union-find.
  • "Merge things that share an attribute" (accounts sharing an email, friends-of-friends) → union-find on the transitive closure.
  • Prerequisites, ordering constraints, "can this be scheduled", "derive the alphabet" → directed graph + topological sort (Kahn / cycle detection).
  • "Shortest / fewest steps" on an unweighted graph → plain BFS by levels; from many sources at once → multi-source BFS.
  • "Cheapest / minimum cost" with non-negative weights → Dijkstra (min-heap); with a hop/edge limit → Bellman-Ford rounds.
  • "Minimize the maximum value along the path" (bottleneck / rising water) → Dijkstra with max instead of sum.
  • State you can encode as a tuple (position+speed, ball stop-points) and you want the fewest moves → BFS over an implicit state graph.

🧠 Core idea & template

Four reusable engines cover this whole chapter. (1) Grid flood fill: scan, and on each unvisited seed sink its whole blob, mutating the grid as the visited-marker. (2) Union-Find: start with N singletons and merge on each relation; the merge count tells you components, cycles, and "fully connected". (3) Kahn's topological sort: BFS removing indegree-0 nodes; a shortfall means a cycle. (4) BFS / Dijkstra for shortest paths — a queue for unweighted, a priority queue for weighted. Memorise these four skeletons cold and most problems become "which engine + how do I define a node/edge?".

// (1) GRID FLOOD FILL — count/colour 4-connected blobs, grid is its own visited set
void dfs(int[][] g, int r, int c) {
    if (r < 0 || c < 0 || r >= g.length || c >= g[0].length || g[r][c] != TARGET) return;
    g[r][c] = MARK;                       // sink in place so a cell is counted once
    dfs(g, r + 1, c); dfs(g, r - 1, c);
    dfs(g, r, c + 1); dfs(g, r, c - 1);
}

// (2) UNION-FIND — components / cycles / "is it a tree"
int find(int[] p, int x) {                // path compression (halving)
    while (p[x] != x) { p[x] = p[p[x]]; x = p[x]; }
    return x;
}
boolean union(int[] p, int[] rank, int a, int b) {
    int ra = find(p, a), rb = find(p, b);
    if (ra == rb) return false;           // already together -> would form a cycle
    if (rank[ra] < rank[rb]) { int t = ra; ra = rb; rb = t; }
    p[rb] = ra;
    if (rank[ra] == rank[rb]) rank[ra]++;
    return true;                          // a real merge happened
}

// (3) KAHN TOPOLOGICAL SORT — feasibility / ordering of a directed graph
int taken = 0;
Deque<Integer> q = new ArrayDeque<>();
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) if (indeg[i] == 0) q.offer(i);
while (!q.isEmpty()) {
    int u = q.poll(); taken++;
    for (int v : adj.get(u)) if (--indeg[v] == 0) q.offer(v);
}
boolean isDAG = (taken == n);             // shortfall == cycle

// (4) BFS (unweighted) — fewest steps, level by level
int level = 0;
while (!q.isEmpty()) {
    for (int sz = q.size(); sz > 0; sz--) {   // drain exactly one ring
        var node = q.poll();
        // if (node == goal) return level;
        // for each neighbour not visited: visited.add(n); q.offer(n);
    }
    level++;
}
// Dijkstra variant: swap the ArrayDeque for a PriorityQueue keyed by cost-so-far.

📘 Deep dive

1 Flood Fill LC 733 ↗ Easy 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Given an image grid, a start pixel and a new colour, repaint the entire 4-connected region of pixels sharing the start's original colour.

💡 Key insight. This is the atom every grid problem is built from: DFS out from the seed, recolouring any neighbour that still holds the original colour. The one trap is the no-op case — if the new colour equals the original, the "already painted?" test never fails and the recursion loops forever, so guard it up front.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Read original = image[sr][sc].
  2. If original == color, return immediately — repainting is a no-op and would otherwise infinite-loop.
  3. DFS from (sr, sc): bounds-check, bail if the cell != original, else set it to color and recurse into all four neighbours.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell of the target region is visited once 💾 Space O(m·n) — recursion depth when the whole image is one colour

⚠️ Gotchas. Forgetting the original == color guard is the classic infinite-recursion bug. Also remember the region is 4-connected (no diagonals) unless told otherwise, and the comparison must be against the captured original, not image[sr][sc] (which you mutate mid-fill).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The region to repaint is a connected blob of one colour, so I flood-fill it with DFS, recolouring each matching neighbour. The only subtlety is that if the new colour equals the old one, my visited check never trips and I recurse forever — so I return early in that case. Every painted cell is touched once, giving O(m·n) time and up to O(m·n) stack depth."
📄 FloodFill.java

2 Number of Islands LC 200 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Count the number of islands (maximal 4-connected groups of '1') in a grid of land and water.

💡 Key insight. Each island is one flood-fill. Scan the grid; the first land cell you hit that hasn't been sunk is a brand-new island — bump the counter and sink the whole blob to '0' so none of its cells are recounted. The counter increments exactly once per island.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Iterate every cell (r, c).
  2. If grid[r][c] == '1': increment count and call sink (DFS) which flips the entire connected blob to '0'.
  3. sink bounds-checks, returns on water/visited, marks the cell '0', and recurses into 4 neighbours.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell is examined once and sunk at most once 💾 Space O(m·n) — recursion depth on an all-land grid

⚠️ Gotchas. Mutating the grid in place is the simplest "visited" marker but destroys the input — say so out loud, or use a separate boolean grid if the caller needs the input preserved. On very large all-land grids the recursive DFS can overflow the stack; an explicit stack or BFS queue is the safe alternative. Watch the cell type: it's char '1'/'0', not int.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "An island is a connected component of land, so I walk the grid and every time I land on an unvisited '1' I've found a new island — I count it and flood-fill it to water so I never count it again. That's O(m·n) since each cell is sunk at most once. I'm mutating the grid as my visited set; if that's not allowed I'd keep a separate visited matrix."
📄 NumberOfIslands.java

3 Number of Closed Islands LC 1254 ↗ Medium 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Count islands of land (0) that are fully enclosed by water (1) and do not touch the grid border.

💡 Key insight. "Touches the border ⇒ not closed." So eliminate the border-connected land first: flood every land cell reachable from an edge, sinking it to water. Whatever land survives is guaranteed interior, and now a plain island-count gives exactly the closed islands. This "neutralise the border, then count" trick recurs across many grid problems.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Flood (sink to 1) every land cell on the four borders — left/right columns and top/bottom rows.
  2. Scan the interior; each remaining 0 blob is a closed island — increment count and flood it.
  3. The shared flood helper converts land 0 → water 1 as its visited marker.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — border flooding plus the interior scan each touch a cell O(1) times 💾 Space O(m·n) — worst-case DFS recursion depth

⚠️ Gotchas. Note the polarity is inverted from LC 200: here land is 0 and water is 1. The corners belong to two borders — flooding them twice is harmless because the second pass sees water. Don't forget a single border cell can chain inland, sinking a large region; that's intended.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A closed island is land that never reaches the edge, so I first flood-fill away every island that does touch the border. After that, any land left is interior by construction, and I just run the standard island count. Two linear passes over the grid, O(m·n)."
📄 NumberOfClosedIslands.java

4 Clone Graph LC 133 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Deep-copy a connected, possibly cyclic undirected graph given a reference to one node.

💡 Key insight. A cyclic deep copy needs a single source of truth: an original → clone hash map. DFS creates the clone the first time it sees a node and registers it before recursing into neighbours; on re-encountering any node (including via a back edge in a cycle) it returns the stored clone instead of recursing. That both terminates the traversal and preserves the shared structure.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If the input node is null, return null.
  2. If the map already holds this node, return its clone (this breaks cycles).
  3. Otherwise create copy = new Node(val), put (node → copy) into the map first, then for each neighbour recurse and add the cloned neighbour to copy.neighbors.

⏱️ Time O(V + E) — every node is cloned once and every edge wired once 💾 Space O(V) — the map plus recursion stack hold up to V nodes

⚠️ Gotchas. Register the clone in the map before recursing — registering after will re-enter the same node through a cycle and recurse infinitely. Use the node object (or its unique val) as the map key, not the cloned object. Don't forget the null-input case.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I DFS the graph keeping a map from each original node to its copy. The first time I visit a node I make its clone and record it immediately, then wire up its neighbours recursively; if I ever reach a node already in the map I just return the existing clone, which is what lets cycles terminate. Every node and edge is processed once, so O(V+E)."
📄 CloneGraph.java

5 Number of Connected Components LC 323 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given n nodes and an undirected edge list, count the connected components.

💡 Key insight. Start by assuming all n nodes are isolated, so there are n components, and let each edge potentially merge two. An edge only reduces the count when its endpoints were in different sets — union-find tells you that in near-constant time, so decrement on each successful merge and the residual count is exactly the number of components.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Init parent[i] = i, rank[i] = 0, and components = n.
  2. For each edge, call union; if it returns true (the roots differed), decrement components.
  3. find uses path-halving; union uses union-by-rank and reports whether a real merge happened.

⏱️ Time O(n + E·α(n)) — linear init; each edge is a near-constant union 💾 Space O(n) — the parent and rank arrays

⚠️ Gotchas. Only decrement when the union actually merged two distinct sets — counting every edge (including redundant ones inside a cycle) over-counts. The DFS/BFS alternative is also O(V+E) but needs an explicit adjacency list and visited array; union-find is leaner here. Path compression plus union-by-rank together give the α(n) bound — one without the other is weaker.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I treat each node as its own component initially, then process edges with union-find. Every edge that joins two previously-separate components drops the count by one, so I just start at n and subtract a successful merge each time. With path compression and union by rank it's effectively linear."
📄 NumberOfConnectedComponents.java

6 Graph Valid Tree LC 261 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Decide whether n nodes and an undirected edge list form a valid tree (connected and acyclic).

💡 Key insight. A tree on n nodes is exactly a connected acyclic graph, and such a graph always has precisely n−1 edges. So reject anything without n−1 edges first (too few can't connect, too many forces a cycle). Once you know the count is n−1, "acyclic" and "connected" become equivalent, so a single cycle check via union-find decides it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If edges.length != n - 1, return false outright.
  2. Init union-find over n nodes.
  3. For each edge, union its endpoints; if any union finds them already connected, that's a cycle → return false.
  4. If all unions succeed, n−1 edges with no cycle ⇒ fully connected ⇒ return true.

⏱️ Time O(n·α(n)) — near-linear via union by rank and path compression 💾 Space O(n) — the parent and rank arrays

⚠️ Gotchas. The n−1 edge-count short-circuit is what lets you skip a separate connectivity check — don't omit it. A single node with no edges is a valid tree (0 == 1 - 1). Be careful not to double-count an undirected edge given twice; that would look like a cycle.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A tree has exactly n−1 edges, so I check that first — wrong count is an instant no. Given the right count, connected and acyclic are the same condition, so I just union the edges and fail the moment two endpoints are already in the same set. No cycle plus n−1 edges guarantees it's connected, so I return true."
📄 GraphValidTree.java

7 Course Schedule LC 207 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given numCourses and prerequisite pairs, decide whether all courses can be completed (i.e. no circular dependency).

💡 Key insight. A prerequisite [a, b] is a directed edge b → a; the schedule is feasible iff this graph is a DAG. Kahn's algorithm peels off indegree-0 nodes — a course becomes takeable exactly when its last prerequisite is taken. If a real cycle exists, its nodes never reach indegree 0, so the count of processed courses falls short of numCourses.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build adjacency lists (b → a for each pair [a, b]) and an indegree array.
  2. Seed a queue with every course of indegree 0.
  3. Pop a course, increment taken, and for each successor decrement its indegree; if it hits 0, enqueue it.
  4. Return taken == numCourses — a shortfall means a cycle blocked some courses.

⏱️ Time O(V + E) — each node and edge processed once 💾 Space O(V + E) — adjacency lists plus the indegree array and queue

⚠️ Gotchas. Get the edge direction right: [a, b] means "take b before a", so the edge runs b → a and it's a's indegree that increments. Initialise the queue with all indegree-0 nodes, not just node 0. The DFS three-colour (white/gray/black) cycle detection is an equally valid alternative.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I model prerequisites as a directed graph and ask whether it's acyclic. With Kahn's topological sort I repeatedly take any course that has no remaining prerequisites and relax its dependents. If I manage to take all numCourses, it was a DAG; if some get stuck with positive indegree, there's a cycle and it's impossible. Linear in nodes plus edges."
📄 CourseSchedule.java

8 Alien Dictionary LC 269 ↗ Hard 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Given a list of words sorted by an unknown alphabet, recover any valid ordering of the letters (or return "" if none exists).

💡 Key insight. Because the dictionary is sorted, each adjacent pair of words yields exactly one ordering constraint — the first position where they differ says "this char precedes that char". Those constraints form a precedence graph over the distinct letters, and a valid alphabet is any topological order of it. Kahn's BFS produces such an order and signals a contradiction (cycle) when fewer than all letters come out.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed every distinct character with an empty adjacency set and indegree 0 (so isolated letters still appear).
  2. For each adjacent pair, find the first differing index j and add edge a[j] → b[j] (incrementing indegree once per distinct edge).
  3. Invalid-prefix check: if no difference is found but the earlier word is longer (e.g. "abc" before "ab"), return "".
  4. Run Kahn's toposort; if the emitted length equals the number of letters, return it, else return "" (cycle).

⏱️ Time O(C) — C is total characters; edge-building and toposort are linear in C 💾 Space O(1) — at most 26 nodes and 26·26 edges, i.e. constant

⚠️ Gotchas. Two infamous edge cases: the prefix violation ("abc" before "ab" is invalid — must return ""), and only comparing adjacent pairs (non-adjacent words give no direct constraint). Seed all characters before adding edges or isolated letters vanish. Add each edge only once or the indegree bookkeeping breaks. Stop at the first differing char — later chars carry no ordering information.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Sorted order means consecutive words disagree at exactly one informative spot: their first differing character gives me a 'before' edge. I build that precedence graph over the distinct letters and topologically sort it — any valid topo order is a valid alphabet. I have to handle the prefix case where a longer word precedes its own prefix, which is unsatisfiable, and a cycle in the constraints, which I detect when the toposort can't emit every letter."
📄 AlienDictionary.java

9 Pacific Atlantic Water Flow LC 417 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. On a height grid, find all cells from which water can flow to both the Pacific (top/left edges) and the Atlantic (bottom/right edges), flowing only to equal-or-lower neighbours.

💡 Key insight. Asking "which cells reach the ocean?" is awkward forward (water descends), but trivial in reverse: start at the ocean's border cells and climb to equal-or-higher neighbours. Run that reverse flood from the Pacific borders and from the Atlantic borders separately to get two reachability masks; the answer is their intersection.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate two boolean grids, pacific and atlantic.
  2. DFS-climb inward from the top row + left column marking pacific, and from the bottom row + right column marking atlantic; only move to a neighbour with height ≥ the current cell.
  3. Collect every cell where both masks are true.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell is visited at most once per ocean's DFS 💾 Space O(m·n) — two boolean masks plus recursion depth

⚠️ Gotchas. The flow condition inverts when you reverse the search: forward water needs ≤, the reverse climb needs ≥. Border cells touch their ocean by definition (start them marked). Doing a separate BFS/DFS per cell forward would be O((m·n)²) — the two-source reverse flood is what makes it linear.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Instead of testing each cell's drainage forward, I flip it: I flood inward from each ocean's edge, climbing to cells of equal or greater height, since whatever I reach could have drained back to that ocean. I do this once for the Pacific borders and once for the Atlantic, then intersect the two reachable sets. That's two linear passes, O(m·n)."
📄 PacificAtlanticWaterFlow.java

10 Rotting Oranges LC 994 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Each minute every rotten orange (2) rots its 4-adjacent fresh oranges (1); return the minutes until none are fresh, or −1 if some can never rot.

💡 Key insight. Rot spreads to all neighbours simultaneously each minute — that's a textbook multi-source BFS where every initially rotten orange is a level-0 source. The number of BFS layers needed to drain the queue equals the minutes until the last fresh orange rots. Count fresh oranges up front so you can distinguish "all rotted" from "some unreachable".
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Enqueue every rotten cell as a source and count the fresh oranges.
  2. If fresh == 0, return 0 immediately.
  3. BFS layer by layer (drain the whole queue per minute), incrementing minutes each layer; rot each fresh neighbour, decrement fresh, and enqueue it.
  4. Return minutes if fresh == 0 at the end, else −1.

⏱️ Time O(m·n) — each cell is enqueued and processed at most once 💾 Space O(m·n) — the BFS queue when everything starts rotten

⚠️ Gotchas. The fresh == 0 early return matters: a grid with no fresh oranges takes 0 minutes, not the number of layers. You must process a whole layer per minute (snapshot queue.size()) — counting per-orange would over-count time. Mark a cell rotten as you enqueue it to avoid double-processing. Any fresh orange unreachable from all sources forces the −1.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rot spreading to all neighbours each minute is exactly multi-source BFS — I seed the queue with every rotten orange at once and count BFS levels, which equals elapsed minutes. I track the fresh count so that if any remain after the BFS settles, they were unreachable and I return −1. Each cell is visited once, so O(m·n)."
📄 RottingOranges.java

11 The Maze LC 490 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. A ball rolls in a 4-direction maze and only stops when it hits a wall or border; decide whether it can come to rest exactly on the destination.

💡 Key insight. The ball does not stop on every cell, so the graph's nodes are only the stopping positions and an edge is a full roll in one direction until a wall stops it. Generate a neighbour by rolling all the way, then BFS over those stop points; marking stop points visited prevents re-rolling, and reaching the destination as a stop point answers the query.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. BFS from start, marking it visited.
  2. Pop a stop point; if it's the destination, return true.
  3. For each of the 4 directions, roll while the next cell is in-bounds and open, then enqueue the resting cell if not already visited.
  4. Exhausting all reachable stops without hitting the destination returns false.

⏱️ Time O(m·n·(m+n)) — up to m·n stop states, each rolling O(m+n) to the next wall 💾 Space O(m·n) — the visited grid and BFS queue

⚠️ Gotchas. The crucial twist vs. a normal maze: you mark stop points visited, not every cell rolled over, and the destination only counts if the ball actually stops there (LC 505 asks for the shortest such path instead). The roll loop must look one cell ahead so it halts at the wall, not inside it. A single-row maze with no interior walls means the ball rolls straight to the far end.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Because the ball slides until it hits a wall, the only meaningful states are resting positions, so I treat each roll-to-a-wall as one edge and BFS over stop points. From each stop I try all four directions, rolling to the next wall, and enqueue any new resting cell. The destination counts only if the ball can actually stop on it. With up to m·n stops and an O(m+n) roll each, it's O(m·n·(m+n))."
📄 TheMaze.java

12 Accounts Merge LC 721 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Merge a list of accounts (each a name plus emails) so that any two accounts sharing an email belong to the same person, then output each merged account's name followed by its sorted unique emails.

💡 Key insight. "Same person" is the transitive closure of "shares an email" — precisely connected components. Union accounts by index: map each email to the first account that owned it, and whenever an email recurs, union the current account with that owner. Then regroup all emails under their component's root, sort them (a TreeSet keeps them ordered and deduped), and prefix the owner's name.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Union-find over account indices. For each account, for each email: if unseen, record email → accountIndex; if seen, union(currentIndex, owner).
  2. Build root → TreeSet<email> by finding each email's owner's root and adding the email.
  3. For each component, emit [name from the representative account] + sorted emails.

⏱️ Time O(N log N) — N total emails; the per-component sort dominates over near-constant unions 💾 Space O(N) — the union-find arrays plus the email-to-owner and component maps

⚠️ Gotchas. Skip index 0 (the name) when iterating emails. Two accounts with the same name can still be different people — you must merge on shared emails, never on the name. Grouping must key on the component root via find, not the raw account index, or you'll split a merged component. The TreeSet handles both the required sort and de-duplication.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Accounts that share any email are the same person, and that relation is transitive, so it's a connected-components problem. I union account indices whenever an email reappears, then collect each component's emails into a sorted set keyed by the component root and prepend the name. Sorting the emails dominates, so O(N log N)."
📄 AccountsMerge.java

13 Earliest Moment Everyone Friends LC 1101 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given timestamped friendship logs [time, a, b] over n people, return the earliest time at which everyone is connected (directly or transitively), or −1.

💡 Key insight. Friendship is the transitive closure of the introductions, so the group is fully acquainted exactly when its people form a single connected component. Process logs in increasing time order, unioning each pair; the moment the component count first drops to 1 is the earliest such time — earlier intros couldn't have done it, later ones are irrelevant.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Sort the logs ascending by timestamp.
  2. Init union-find with components = n.
  3. For each log, union(a, b); on a successful merge, decrement components and if it hits 1, return that log's timestamp.
  4. If the logs run out with more than one component, return −1.

⏱️ Time O(L log L + L·α(n)) — sorting dominates; each union is near-constant 💾 Space O(n) — the parent and rank arrays

⚠️ Gotchas. Sorting by time is mandatory — the logs aren't given in order, and the answer is the timestamp of the connecting event. Only decrement on a genuine merge (ignore redundant introductions among already-connected people). Return early the instant the count reaches 1; don't keep processing later logs.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Everyone being acquainted means one connected component, so I sort the introductions by time and union people as the events happen. Each time a union actually merges two groups I drop the component count, and the first event that brings it to one is my answer. If I exhaust the logs still fragmented, it never happens, so −1. Sorting dominates at O(L log L)."
📄 EarliestMomentEveryoneFriends.java

14 Word Ladder LC 127 ↗ Hard ⚪ Core

Problem. Transform beginWord into endWord one letter at a time, with every intermediate word in the dictionary; return the length of the shortest such chain (or 0).

💡 Key insight. Words are nodes and an edge joins two words differing in exactly one letter; the shortest ladder is a shortest path, found by unweighted BFS. The trick is generating neighbours cheaply: instead of comparing a word against the whole dictionary (O(N) each), map each word to its L wildcard patterns like "h*t" and bucket words by pattern — two words sharing a pattern are exactly one edit apart, so a lookup costs O(L).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If endWord isn't in the dictionary, return 0.
  2. Precompute a map from each single-wildcard pattern to the list of dictionary words matching it.
  3. BFS from beginWord at level 1; for the current word, form each wildcard pattern and enqueue every unvisited word in that bucket.
  4. Return level when endWord is dequeued; if BFS drains, return 0.

⏱️ Time O(N·L²) — N words, each forming L patterns of length L 💾 Space O(N·L²) — the pattern-to-words index

⚠️ Gotchas. The length counts words/nodes, so the start word is level 1 (not 0) — off-by-one here is the most common bug. Mark words visited as you enqueue, not as you dequeue, to avoid re-queuing within a level. The wildcard-bucket index is what avoids the O(N²·L) naive neighbour scan; mention it explicitly. beginWord need not be in the dictionary, but endWord must be.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each word is a node and one-letter edits are edges, so the shortest transformation is just BFS. The expensive part is finding neighbours; rather than scan the dictionary I bucket words by wildcard patterns like h*t, so all one-edit neighbours are a single map lookup. I BFS by levels and return the level when I dequeue the target, remembering the count is of words so the start is level 1."
📄 WordLadder.java

15 Shortest Distance from All Buildings LC 317 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. On a grid of buildings (1), obstacles (2) and empty land (0), find the empty cell minimizing the total walking distance to every building, or −1 if none reaches all.

💡 Key insight. The best lot minimizes the sum of shortest-path distances to all buildings, and on an unweighted grid each distance is a BFS layer. Rather than BFS from every candidate lot (many), BFS once per building (few) and accumulate into each empty cell both the running distance sum and a count of how many buildings reached it. A valid cell must be reachable by all buildings, so the reach-count filters out blocked lots.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Maintain dist[][] (summed distance) and reach[][] (buildings that reached the cell); count totalBuildings.
  2. From each building, BFS by layers over empty land with a fresh visited grid; for each newly reached empty cell add steps to dist and increment reach.
  3. The answer is the minimum dist among empty cells with reach == totalBuildings, or −1 if none qualify.

⏱️ Time O(B·m·n) — one grid-wide BFS per building B 💾 Space O(m·n) — the distance-sum and reach-count grids plus the BFS queue

⚠️ Gotchas. Use a fresh visited grid per building so each contributes a cell's distance at most once. Only cells reached by all buildings are valid — the reach == totalBuildings filter is essential, otherwise you'd pick a lot blocked from some building. BFS only walks through empty land (0); obstacles and buildings block. A common optimization (not used here) decrements a target marker so unreachable buildings prune early.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I want the empty cell with the smallest total distance to every building, and each distance is a BFS layer. So I flip the work: I BFS from each building, since there are fewer of them, and accumulate into every empty cell the distance and how many buildings reached it. At the end I take the minimum accumulated distance among cells reachable from all buildings. With B buildings it's O(B·m·n)."
📄 ShortestDistanceFromAllBuildings.java

16 Swim in Rising Water LC 778 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Water level rises over time; at time t any cell with elevation ≤ t is swimmable. Return the least time to swim from the top-left to the bottom-right corner.

💡 Key insight. A path is usable at time t iff its single highest cell is ≤ t, so you want the path whose maximum elevation is smallest — a minimax (bottleneck) shortest path. Dijkstra works once you redefine a path's cost as the max elevation along it rather than a sum: always expand the reachable cell with the smallest "max-so-far", and the first pop of the bottom-right corner is the answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Min-heap of {maxElevationSoFar, r, c}, seeded with the start cell's elevation; mark start visited.
  2. Pop the smallest; if it's the corner, return its maxElevationSoFar.
  3. For each unvisited neighbour, push {max(currentMax, neighbourElevation), nr, nc} and mark it visited.

⏱️ Time O(n² log n) — each of the n² cells is settled once via a heap of size O(n²) 💾 Space O(n²) — the visited grid and the priority queue

⚠️ Gotchas. The heap key is max along the path, not a running sum — using sum solves the wrong problem. The first time you pop the corner you're done (any cheaper route would have popped earlier), so return immediately. Marking visited on push (as here) is fine because Dijkstra settles the smallest first. A union-find / binary-search-on-time approach also works but this minimax-Dijkstra is the cleanest single pass.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Time to traverse a path is just the highest elevation on it, since I must wait for the water to cover that peak. So I want the path that minimizes its maximum cell — a bottleneck shortest path — which is Dijkstra with the path cost being max instead of sum. I always expand the cell reachable with the lowest peak so far, and the moment I pop the bottom-right corner that peak is my answer. O(n² log n) over the grid."
📄 SwimInRisingWater.java

17 Cheapest Flights Within K Stops LC 787 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given flights as weighted directed edges, find the cheapest price from src to dst using at most K intermediate stops (or −1).

💡 Key insight. "At most K stops" means a path of at most K+1 edges, and Bellman-Ford's i-th relaxation round establishes the cheapest cost reachable using at most i edges. So exactly K+1 rounds yield the answer under the stop limit. The subtlety: each round must relax off the previous round's costs, or a flight relaxed earlier this round could chain into another and smuggle in extra stops.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Init dist[src] = 0, everything else +∞.
  2. Repeat K+1 times: clone dist into next, and for every flight relax next[to] = min(next[to], dist[from] + price) using the old dist; then swap in next.
  3. Return dist[dst] (or −1 if still +∞).

⏱️ Time O(K·E) — K+1 rounds, each scanning every flight once 💾 Space O(n) — two cost arrays of size n, no adjacency structure needed

⚠️ Gotchas. The cloned-array boundary is the whole trick — relaxing in place lets a path use more than one new edge per round, breaking the stop limit (and silently giving cheaper-but-too-many-hop answers). Rounds are K+1 (edges), not K. Skip relaxing from an unreached node (dist[from] == +∞) to avoid overflow. Plain Dijkstra is wrong here because the cheapest route may exceed the hop budget.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "K stops is K+1 edges, and Bellman-Ford after i rounds gives the cheapest cost using at most i edges — so I run K+1 rounds. The key detail is relaxing each round against the previous round's snapshot, which I get by cloning the distance array; otherwise edges relaxed this round would chain and exceed the stop budget. It's O(K·E), just sweeping all flights each round."
📄 CheapestFlightsWithinKStops.java

18 Race Car LC 818 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Starting at position 0 with speed +1, instruction 'A' moves to (pos+speed, speed*2) and 'R' resets speed to ±1 facing the other way; return the fewest instructions to reach target.

💡 Key insight. The car's full state is (position, speed), and each instruction is one unweighted edge, so the fewest instructions to reach the target is a shortest path — BFS over states. The state space is bounded by only exploring positions within [-target, 2·target]: any optimal solution stays in that band (overshooting past 2·target is never on a shortest path), keeping the search finite.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. BFS from state (0, +1), levels = instruction count; a visited set keys on "pos,speed".
  2. If pos == target, return the current step count.
  3. Accelerate edge: (pos+speed, speed*2), enqueued only if |pos+speed| ≤ 2·target.
  4. Reverse edge: (pos, speed>0 ? -1 : 1), always a candidate (subject to visited).

⏱️ Time O(target log target) — reachable states bounded by the band and the doubling speeds 💾 Space O(target log target) — the visited set and BFS queue over those states

⚠️ Gotchas. Bound the search or it never terminates — the |pos| ≤ 2·target band is what makes the state space finite. Key visited on the full (pos, speed) pair, not position alone, since the same spot at different speeds leads to different futures. Speeds grow as powers of two, which is why the log factor appears. The bottom-up DP alternative gives O(target log target) without BFS but is trickier to derive on the spot.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I model the car as a state machine over (position, speed); each instruction is a single edge, so minimum instructions is a shortest path and I run BFS. The catch is keeping the state space finite — I only keep positions within the band from −target to 2·target, since an optimal path never overshoots that far. I dedupe on the full (pos, speed) state because speed changes the reachable future."
📄 RaceCar.java

🔁 Practice plan

Build the four engines first, then layer on the variations. Day 1 — grid flood fill: re-derive Flood Fill (733) → Number of Islands (200) → Number of Closed Islands (1254) → Pacific Atlantic (417) from a blank file; they're the same DFS with different markers and a border trick. Day 2 — union-find: write the path-compression + union-by-rank skeleton once, then knock out Connected Components (323), Graph Valid Tree (261), Accounts Merge (721) and Earliest Moment (1101) — they only differ in what you union. Day 3 — toposort & BFS: Course Schedule (207) → Alien Dictionary (269) for Kahn's, then Rotting Oranges (994) → Word Ladder (127) → The Maze (490) for unweighted/multi-source BFS. Day 4 — weighted & state-space: Swim in Rising Water (778), Cheapest Flights (787), Shortest Distance from All Buildings (317) and Race Car (818) — the hard ones where the node/edge definition is the whole game.

Rehearse these Google-favourite follow-ups out loud: "now return the actual shortest path, not just its length" (Word Ladder II / LC 126); "do it iteratively to avoid stack overflow on a 10⁶-cell grid" (Number of Islands); "the grid streams in and islands merge online" (Number of Islands II / LC 305 — pure union-find); and "what if edges were weighted?" (when does BFS become Dijkstra become Bellman-Ford).

  • ✅ I can write path-compression + union-by-rank from memory and state why both give the α(n) bound.
  • ✅ I can explain why Kahn's toposort detects a cycle (processed count < node count) and reconstruct the indegree bookkeeping.
  • ✅ I can articulate when to reach for BFS vs. Dijkstra vs. Bellman-Ford (unweighted vs. non-negative weights vs. hop-limited / negative-aware).
  • ✅ I can recognise the "reverse the search from the boundary" trick (Pacific Atlantic, Closed Islands) and the "BFS from the few, not the many" trick (Shortest Distance from All Buildings).
  • ✅ I can reframe a word problem as nodes + edges on the fly — stop points (The Maze), (pos, speed) states (Race Car), shared-email components (Accounts Merge).
  • ✅ I can explain why Dijkstra with max instead of sum solves bottleneck paths (Swim in Rising Water), and why cloning the dist array bounds the hop count (Cheapest Flights).
  • ✅ I never forget the foundational gotchas: the flood-fill no-op guard, BFS level counting words not edges, and mutating-the-grid as a visited set.

🚀 Advanced Graphs (L6 Differentiators)

This is the family that separates a strong L5 from an L6: weighted shortest paths (Dijkstra), bridge/articulation detection (Tarjan), connectivity tallies via union-find, and full-game solving by retrograde DP. Each problem is really a clean classic dressed in a story — the differentiator is recognizing the classic fast and narrating why the algorithm is correct, not just coding it.

Source: Prep Tracker tab · 6 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Time for a signal/fluid/cost to reach every node" with non-negative weights → Dijkstra; the answer is the max over all shortest arrivals.
  • Grid where entry into a cell is gated by a time/value threshold, plus you can "wait" → Dijkstra by arrival time with a parity adjustment.
  • "Edge whose removal disconnects the graph" / "critical connection" / "find all bridges" → Tarjan low-link DFS.
  • "Count paths/pairs where the maximum (or minimum) along the path equals the endpoints" → sort by value + union-find, add nodes in order.
  • Asymmetric trigger relation ("a reaches b but not necessarily b reaches a") → directed graph + reachability from each source.
  • Two-player turn-based game with cycles and possible draws → retrograde analysis (BFS backward from terminal states), not naive forward minimax.

🧠 Core idea & template

Three idioms cover this chapter. (1) Dijkstra: a min-heap keyed on tentative distance always settles the closest unsettled node next; skip stale heap entries with a d > dist[node] guard. (2) Tarjan bridges: one DFS assigns each node a discovery time and a low-link (the smallest discovery time reachable via tree edges plus at most one back edge); edge (u,v) is a bridge iff low[v] > disc[u]. (3) Union-find by value: process nodes in sorted order so each new component contains exactly the nodes reachable through smaller-or-equal values. Here is the Dijkstra skeleton that powers two of these problems:

// Dijkstra: shortest arrival time from a source over non-negative weights.
int[] dist = new int[n];
Arrays.fill(dist, Integer.MAX_VALUE);
dist[src] = 0;
PriorityQueue<int[]> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> Integer.compare(a[1], b[1]));
pq.offer(new int[]{src, 0});               // {node, dist}
while (!pq.isEmpty()) {
    int[] cur = pq.poll();
    int node = cur[0], d = cur[1];
    if (d > dist[node]) continue;          // stale entry — already settled shorter
    for (int[] edge : adj[node]) {          // edge = {neighbor, weight}
        int next = edge[0], nd = d + edge[1];
        if (nd < dist[next]) {
            dist[next] = nd;
            pq.offer(new int[]{next, nd});
        }
    }
}
