📘 Deep dive

1 Network Delay Time LC 743 ↗ Medium 🟪 L6 differentiator

Problem. Given directed edges (u, v, w) with travel time w, a signal starts at node k; return the time for all n nodes to receive it, or -1 if some node never does.

💡 Key insight. A node's arrival time is its shortest-path distance from k under non-negative weights, so Dijkstra computes every node's arrival simultaneously. The whole network is covered exactly when the slowest shortest arrival lands — so the answer is max(dist[i]). Any unreached node (dist == ∞) means the signal never fully propagates → -1.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build 1-indexed adjacency lists of {neighbor, weight}.
  2. Run Dijkstra from k: min-heap keyed on tentative distance, relaxing each edge and skipping stale pops with d > dist[node].
  3. Scan dist[1..n]: if any is Integer.MAX_VALUE return -1, else return the maximum.

⏱️ Time O(E log V) — each edge relaxes at most once; each heap op is O(log V) 💾 Space O(V + E) — adjacency lists, dist array, and heap

⚠️ Gotchas. Nodes are 1-indexed — size arrays at n + 1 and leave index 0 unused. Without the stale-entry guard you waste work re-relaxing settled nodes. Edge weights are non-negative, which is exactly the precondition that makes Dijkstra (not Bellman-Ford) correct.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force would re-run a BFS/relaxation from every node, but I notice the time to reach a node is just its shortest path from the source, so this is single-source shortest path with non-negative weights — textbook Dijkstra. I settle nodes closest-first from a min-heap; the moment the farthest node settles, the network is fully covered, so I return the max distance, or -1 if anything stayed infinite. That's O(E log V) because each edge relaxes once and every heap operation is logarithmic."
📄 NetworkDelayTime.java

2 Detonate the Maximum Bombs LC 2101 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Each bomb has a center and blast radius; detonating one triggers every bomb inside its radius, recursively. Return the maximum number of bombs you can detonate by manually setting off exactly one.

💡 Key insight. Triggering is directed and asymmetric — a big bomb can reach a small one without the reverse. So add edge a → b when b lies inside a's radius; the bombs set off by manually detonating a are exactly the set reachable from a. Try every starting bomb and take the largest reachable count.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. For each ordered pair (i, j), add i → j if dx² + dy² ≤ r_i², computing in long to dodge overflow.
  2. From each bomb, run a DFS/BFS counting reachable nodes (mark on push so none is double-counted).
  3. Return the best count over all start nodes.

⏱️ Time O(n³) — O(n²) edges to build, plus an O(n²) traversal from each of n bombs 💾 Space O(n²) — dense adjacency lists in the worst case

⚠️ Gotchas. Do not build an undirected graph — the relation isn't mutual, and treating it as symmetric over-counts. Coordinates and radius can be up to 1e5, so dx*dx + dy*dy overflows int; cast to long and compare squared distance to squared radius (never use floating-point sqrt). Bombs exactly on the boundary count (≤, not <).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The trap is assuming the trigger relation is symmetric — it isn't, a larger bomb reaching a smaller one doesn't mean the reverse. So I model it as a directed graph, edge a→b when b is within a's radius, and the answer for starting at a is just the count of nodes reachable from a. With only ~100 bombs I can afford O(n²) edges and an O(n²) traversal from each start, so O(n³) is fine. I compare squared distances in long to avoid both overflow and floating-point error."
📄 DetonateTheMaximumBombs.java

3 Minimum Time to Visit a Cell in a Grid LC 2577 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Start at (0,0) at time 0; each move to an adjacent cell costs 1 second, but you may only enter cell (r,c) once the clock is ≥ grid[r][c]. Return the minimum time to reach the bottom-right, or -1.

💡 Key insight. Minimizing arrival time over unit-cost moves with entry thresholds is Dijkstra keyed on time. The twist is waiting: you burn time by stepping to an adjacent cell and back (+2 per bounce). Because each step flips the parity of both (r+c) and the clock, the earliest legal entry into a future cell is grid[nr][nc], bumped by 1 when its parity disagrees with (nr+nc) — that +1 is one extra bounce.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If both cells adjacent to the start have value > 1, you can never take a first step → return -1 up front.
  2. Dijkstra by time from (0,0); first settle of the target is the optimum.
  3. For each neighbor: arrive = t + 1; if that's still earlier than grid[nr][nc], jump to grid[nr][nc] and add 1 if (arrive − (nr+nc)) is odd.
  4. Relax and push if it improves dist[nr][nc].

⏱️ Time O(mn log(mn)) — each cell settles once; heap ops are logarithmic 💾 Space O(mn) — the distance grid and heap

⚠️ Gotchas. The only unreachable case is the blocked start (both first moves gated above time 1) — once you've moved, the wait-by-bouncing trick guarantees you can always reach any cell, so the loop's trailing -1 is effectively just the start guard. The parity correction is the subtle part: forgetting it lets you "arrive" at a time you physically can't stand there. (x & 1) == 1 tests oddness.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's shortest-time on a grid with non-negative effective costs, so Dijkstra by arrival time. The wrinkle is that I can wait by pacing back and forth, two seconds a round trip. Each step changes the parity of row+col and of the clock together, so to enter a cell whose value is in the future I arrive at exactly its value, plus one if the parity is wrong — that one represents a single extra bounce. The only way to be stuck is if both cells next to the start are gated past time 1, which I check first; otherwise the bottom-right is always reachable."
📄 MinimumTimeToVisitCell.java

4 Number of Good Paths LC 2421 ↗ Hard 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. In a tree with node values vals, a good path has equal endpoint values and no intermediate node exceeding that value. Count all good paths (a single node counts).

💡 Key insight. A good path's endpoints share the maximum value on the path. Process nodes in increasing value and grow components with union-find, joining each new node only to already-added neighbors (value ≤ it). Then the component formed at value v contains exactly the nodes reachable through values ≤ v — and any two value-v nodes in it form a good path. A component holding c nodes of value v contributes c·(c−1)/2.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed good = N (every single node is trivially a good path).
  2. Bucket nodes by value; iterate values ascending.
  3. For each value-v node, union it with neighbors whose value ≤ v.
  4. Group those value-v nodes by DSU root; for each root holding c of them, add c·(c−1)/2.

⏱️ Time O(N log N + E·α) — sorting values dominates; unions are near-constant amortized 💾 Space O(N + E) — adjacency, DSU arrays, value buckets

⚠️ Gotchas. You must add nodes strictly in ascending value order so that when you tally at value v, the component already contains every relevant smaller-valued connector. Only union toward neighbors with value ≤ v (a strictly larger neighbor isn't processed yet). The c == 1 case correctly adds 0, which is why seeding good = N up front cleanly handles single-node paths. Path-halving in find keeps the DSU near-flat.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The defining fact is that the endpoints of a good path are the maximum on that path, so I sweep values from low to high and use union-find to maintain 'components reachable through values seen so far.' When I activate all nodes of value v and union them with their already-active neighbors, every pair of value-v nodes that lands in the same component is a good path — that's c-choose-2 per component. I seed the count with N for the trivial single-node paths. Sorting is the bottleneck at N log N; the unions are effectively linear."
📄 NumberOfGoodPaths.java

5 Critical Connections in a Network LC 1192 ↗ Hard 🟪 L6 differentiator

Problem. Given an undirected connected network of n servers and bidirectional connections, return every connection whose removal would disconnect some pair of servers (the bridges).

💡 Key insight. A critical connection is a bridge. Tarjan's DFS gives each node a discovery time disc[u] and a low-link low[u] — the smallest discovery time reachable from u's subtree using tree edges plus at most one back edge. Edge (u, v) with child v is a bridge iff low[v] > disc[u]: v's subtree has no back edge climbing to u or above, so that edge is the subtree's only link out.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build undirected adjacency lists; set disc[] = -1 as the unvisited marker.
  2. DFS from each unvisited node. On entry: disc[u] = low[u] = timer++.
  3. For a tree edge to unvisited v: recurse, then low[u] = min(low[u], low[v]); if low[v] > disc[u], record (u, v) as a bridge.
  4. For a back edge to a visited non-parent v: low[u] = min(low[u], disc[v]).

⏱️ Time O(V + E) — one DFS touching every node and edge once 💾 Space O(V + E) — adjacency lists, disc/low arrays, recursion stack

⚠️ Gotchas. Skip the edge back to the parent — but only once: a second u–parent edge is a genuine parallel/back edge and means the connection isn't a bridge, so the code uses a skippedParent flag rather than a blanket v == parent skip. Use disc[v] (not low[v]) when relaxing a back edge. The strict inequality low[v] > disc[u] is what distinguishes a bridge from a cycle edge (low[v] == disc[u] means v can climb back to u).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Removing an edge disconnects the graph exactly when that edge is a bridge — it lies on no cycle. I find all bridges in a single DFS using Tarjan's low-link: disc is when I first see a node, and low is the earliest node its subtree can reach via one back edge. A tree edge u→v is a bridge precisely when low[v] is strictly greater than disc[u], meaning v's whole subtree has no alternate route back up to u. One subtlety: I only ignore the parent edge once, so parallel edges are correctly treated as back edges. It's linear, O(V+E)."
📄 CriticalConnections.java

6 Cat and Mouse LC 913 ↗ Hard 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Mouse (starts at node 1) and Cat (starts at node 2) alternate moves on an undirected graph; mouse aims for the hole (node 0), cat aims to catch the mouse, cat may never enter the hole. With optimal play, return 1 (mouse wins), 2 (cat wins), or 0 (draw).

💡 Key insight. Forward minimax stalls on cyclic draws, so solve by retrograde analysis: seed the known terminal states (mouse at hole → mouse wins; mouse meets cat → cat wins) and propagate verdicts backward. A state resolves the instant we can prove it — if the mover has any move into a state they win, they win; if every move leads to an opponent win, they lose. The "all moves lose" case is caught with a degree counter decremented as each successor resolves against the predecessor; reaching 0 means forced loss. States never reached stay draws.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. State = (mouse, cat, turn); result and degree tables sized n × n × 2.
  2. Precompute each state's branching factor, subtracting the illegal cat-into-hole move.
  3. Seed terminals: result[0][c][*] = MOUSE_WIN, result[x][x][*] = CAT_WIN; enqueue them.
  4. BFS backward: for each resolved state, visit predecessors. If the successor is a win for the predecessor's mover, mark and enqueue; else decrement degree, and on hitting 0 mark a forced loss and enqueue.
  5. Answer is result[1][2][MOUSE_TURN] (0 if it never resolved → draw).

⏱️ Time O(V³) — O(V²) turn-states, each examining O(V) predecessor moves once 💾 Space O(V²) — result and degree tables over (mouse, cat, turn)

⚠️ Gotchas. The hole (node 0) is special: exclude it from the cat's legal moves both when computing degrees and when enumerating predecessors. Predecessors are reverse moves — if it's the mouse's turn now, the cat just moved, so vary the cat's previous position (and vice versa). The degree counter is what makes "all children lose ⇒ I lose" efficient; you can't just check children on demand because they resolve incrementally. Unreached states defaulting to 0 (DRAW) is intentional, not a bug.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A naive minimax loops forever because the game can cycle into a draw, so I work backward from the outcomes I already know — mouse on the hole is a mouse win, mouse and cat colliding is a cat win — and propagate. A state is a win for whoever moves if they have any edge into a winning state; it's a loss only if every one of their moves leads to an opponent win, which I detect by counting down that state's unresolved moves. Whatever the backward BFS never colors is a draw. There are V² position-pairs times 2 turns, each touched by O(V) predecessors, so O(V³). The one trick to remember is the cat can never step onto the hole, so I strip that move everywhere."
📄 CatAndMouse.java

🔁 Practice plan

Re-derive in this order — each builds the muscle for the next. (1) Network Delay Time from a blank file: get the heap comparator, the stale-entry guard, and the "max over all dist" finish without peeking. (2) Detonate: reach for a directed graph reflexively and remember the long squared-distance compare. (3) Minimum Time to Visit a Cell: reproduce the parity bump argument out loud. (4) Critical Connections: write Tarjan's DFS cold — disc/low init, the bridge test low[v] > disc[u], and the single-parent-skip — this is the highest-value L6 muscle here. (5) Number of Good Paths: the "process by ascending value + union-find" pattern. (6) Cat and Mouse: rebuild the retrograde BFS with the degree counter. Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse: Cheapest Flights Within K Stops (787) as a Dijkstra/Bellman-Ford variant, Path With Minimum Effort (1631) and Swim in Rising Water (778) as Dijkstra-on-grid cousins of 2577, and Number of Provinces (547) / Redundant Connection (684) for union-find warmups.

  • ✅ I can explain why Dijkstra (not BFS or Bellman-Ford) is correct here, and what the non-negative-weight precondition buys me.
  • ✅ I can state the stale-entry guard and why it preserves correctness while bounding heap work.
  • ✅ I can derive the bridge condition low[v] > disc[u] from the meaning of disc and low — and handle parallel edges.
  • ✅ I can articulate the "endpoints are the path max ⇒ sweep by value + union-find" idea and the c·(c−1)/2 tally.
  • ✅ I can explain why forward minimax fails on Cat and Mouse and how retrograde analysis with a degree counter fixes it.
  • ✅ I can justify the parity +1 in the grid wait, and why the only unreachable case is the blocked start.

🤑 Greedy

Greedy algorithms commit to the locally-best choice at each step and never look back — the hard part is proving that local optimality rolls up to a global optimum (usually via an exchange argument or a conservation/invariant argument). Google L6 loves these because the code is tiny but the justification separates engineers who memorize from those who can reason; "why is greedy correct here?" is the real question.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 3 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Find the minimum number of …" or "is it feasible to …" where a single forward sweep with a running total seems to suffice.
  • A conservation law is lurking (total gas vs total cost) so feasibility reduces to a global sum check.
  • The most frequent / most constrained element dictates the structure (scheduling, intervals).
  • "Repeatedly take the two smallest / cheapest and combine" → min-heap driven greedy (Huffman-style).
  • You can argue: if an optimal solution disagrees with the greedy choice, you can swap to match greedy without making it worse (exchange argument).

🧠 Core idea & template

Greedy has two recurring shapes. (1) Single-sweep with an invariant: maintain a running quantity, reset or commit at the moment it violates a constraint, and rely on a conservation argument to prove the answer is unique/correct. (2) Heap-driven combination: repeatedly pop the extremal element(s), merge, push the result, and let an exchange argument prove the rarest/cheapest items belong deepest. Always be ready to state the proof — the skeletons are trivial.

// Shape 1 — single sweep with a resettable running total (feasibility + answer)
int total = 0, running = 0, start = 0;
for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
    int diff = gain(i) - cost(i);
    total += diff;
    running += diff;
    if (running < 0) { start = i + 1; running = 0; } // commit: restart candidate here
}
return total >= 0 ? start : -1;                       // conservation decides feasibility

// Shape 2 — repeatedly combine the two cheapest via a min-heap (Huffman)
PriorityQueue<Node> pq = new PriorityQueue<>((a, b) -> a.weight - b.weight);
// ... seed pq with leaves ...
while (pq.size() > 1) {
    Node a = pq.poll(), b = pq.poll();
    pq.add(new Node(a.weight + b.weight, a, b));      // merge the two minima
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Gas Station LC 134 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Conservation + one-pass restartO(n)O(1)
2Task Scheduler LC 621 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Frame the busiest task + backfillO(t)O(1)
3Huffman Coding — Medium 📌 Reported L6 Min-heap merge (prefix code)O(k log k)O(k)

📘 Deep dive

1 Gas Station LC 134 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Around a circular route, station i gives gas[i] and the drive to i+1 costs cost[i]; return a starting index from which you can complete the loop, or -1.

💡 Key insight. A solution exists iff total gas ≥ total cost (conservation of fuel). And if the running tank dips below zero at station i, no start in [start..i] can work — each such start reaches i with even less fuel — so the only viable next candidate jumps to i+1. One pass yields both the feasibility check and the unique answer.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Maintain total (global surplus) and tank (fuel since the current candidate start), plus start = 0.
  2. For each i: let diff = gas[i] - cost[i]; add it to both total and tank.
  3. If tank < 0: you can't reach i+1 from the current start, so set start = i + 1 and reset tank = 0.
  4. After the sweep, return start if total >= 0, else -1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single sweep over the stations 💾 Space O(1) — two running totals

⚠️ Gotchas. Don't restart the candidate at i — it must be i+1, because i itself is part of the failing stretch. The answer is unique when one exists (a known property), so you never need to try multiple candidates. The total >= 0 guard is what proves the final start actually completes the loop.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force tries every start in O(n²). But fuel is conserved: if the grand total of gas minus cost is non-negative, some start works, and it's unique. As I sweep, the moment my tank goes negative I know nothing from my current start through here can succeed, so I leapfrog the start to the next station. One O(n) pass, O(1) space."
📄 GasStation.java

2 Task Scheduler LC 621 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Given task labels and a cooldown n (identical tasks must be ≥ n apart), return the minimum number of time units (including idles) to run all tasks.

💡 Key insight. The busiest task dictates the skeleton. Place its maxFreq copies n+1 apart, forming (maxFreq-1) full frames of width (n+1) plus a final partial frame holding the countOfMax tasks tied for the maximum: (maxFreq-1)*(n+1) + countOfMax slots. When there are enough distinct tasks to backfill every gap, no idling is needed and the answer is just the task count — hence the max with the array length.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Tally frequencies into a 26-bucket array.
  2. Find maxFreq, then countOfMax = how many tasks share that peak.
  3. Compute framed = (maxFreq - 1) * (n + 1) + countOfMax.
  4. Return max(framed, tasks.length) — a dense input may leave no idle gaps.

⏱️ Time O(t) — one pass to tally (fixed 26-letter alphabet) 💾 Space O(1) — a 26-bucket frequency array

⚠️ Gotchas. The max(framed, tasks.length) is essential: with many distinct tasks the frames overflow with real work and the formula would undercount idle-free schedules. Multiple tasks tied at the max all sit in the last frame (that's countOfMax, not 1). This closed form sidesteps a heap simulation, which also works but is O(t log 26).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The most frequent task forces the worst-case spacing, so I lay it out first: maxFreq-1 frames of width n+1, then a tail with every task tied for the max. Other tasks slot into the gaps. If there are so many tasks that the gaps overflow, there's no idling and the answer is simply the number of tasks — that's the max() guard."
📄 TaskScheduler.java

3 Huffman Coding Medium 📌 Reported L6

Problem. Build an optimal prefix-free binary code for a set of symbols with given frequencies, minimizing total weighted code length (and support encode/decode round-trips).

💡 Key insight. An optimal prefix code is built bottom-up by greedily merging the two least-frequent nodes into a parent until one tree remains. The exchange argument guarantees the two rarest symbols sit deepest as siblings, so repeatedly combining the minima (via a min-heap keyed on frequency) minimizes total weighted length. Labeling left edges 0 and right edges 1 yields a prefix-free code — no codeword is an ancestor of another — so the bitstream decodes unambiguously.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Count symbol frequencies; push one leaf node per symbol into a min-heap ordered by frequency.
  2. While the heap has > 1 node: poll the two smallest a, b, push a parent with weight a.freq + b.freq and children a, b.
  3. Walk the final tree, appending 0 on left edges and 1 on right edges, recording each leaf's path as its code.
  4. Edge case: a single distinct symbol is a lone leaf → assign it code "0".
  5. Encode by concatenating codes; decode by scanning bits until the accumulated prefix matches a symbol (safe because the code is prefix-free).

⏱️ Time O(k log k) — k distinct symbols, logarithmic heap ops 💾 Space O(k) — heap, tree, and code table

⚠️ Gotchas. The single-symbol case has tree height 0, so naive traversal yields the empty string — special-case it to "0". Decoding relies on the prefix property: the first matching prefix is always the intended symbol, so greedily cut there. Tie-breaking among equal frequencies doesn't affect optimality (total length is the same), only the specific codes. Off-LeetCode, but a reported L6 systems-flavored question.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I want to give rare symbols long codes and common ones short codes. Greedy: always merge the two least-frequent nodes, which pushes the rarest symbols deepest — an exchange argument shows any optimal tree can be rearranged to match this without increasing cost. A min-heap makes each merge O(log k). Left=0, right=1 gives a prefix-free code, so decoding is an unambiguous walk down the tree."
📄 HuffmanCoding.java

🔁 Practice plan

Do Gas Station (134) first and practice saying the conservation proof — that's the whole question. Then Task Scheduler (621): derive the framing formula on paper and explain the max(framed, length) guard with a dense example. Finish with Huffman (—), which is the canonical "prove greedy is optimal via exchange argument" exercise; rebuild the tree from a blank file and walk the encode/decode round-trip. Google follow-ups to rehearse: Jump Game II (45) and Jump Game (55) as single-sweep greedy; Reorganize String (767) as the heap cousin of Task Scheduler; and "what if the alphabet were arbitrary / Unicode?" for the scheduler.

  • ✅ I can state the conservation argument for why Gas Station's greedy is correct and unique.
  • ✅ I can derive the Task Scheduler framing formula and explain when idling drops to zero.
  • ✅ I can give the exchange-argument proof that Huffman's merge is optimal.
  • ✅ I can handle the single-symbol Huffman edge case and explain prefix-free decoding.
  • ✅ I can recognize when a problem is greedy vs. when it secretly needs DP.

📐 Dynamic Programming

Dynamic programming is the art of recognising that a hard problem is just a small recurrence over overlapping subproblems, then storing each subproblem's answer exactly once. It is the single highest-ROI family for a Google L6 loop: interviewers use it to test whether you can name the state, write the transition, justify the base case, and then casually drop the table to O(1) rolling variables — all while talking. Master the half-dozen idioms here (linear scan, unbounded knapsack, grid walk, two-string edit, and state-machine counting) and most "medium-hard" DP questions collapse into a known shape.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 17 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The prompt asks for a count of ways, a min/max value, or a yes/no feasibility — and a greedy choice clearly does not generalise.
  • The answer at index i depends on a small fixed set of earlier answers (i-1, i-2, or a diagonal neighbour) — the hallmark of optimal substructure.
  • You can phrase the decision as "take it or skip it", "which item is last", or "where is the split point".
  • Two sequences / a 2-D grid where progress is measured by a pair of indices (i, j) → a 2-D table.
  • Naive recursion would re-solve the same subproblem exponentially often (overlapping subproblems).
  • Counting strings/arrangements under a constraint that depends only on a bounded suffix ("at most 2 trailing L's") → a finite state machine you roll n times.

🧠 Core idea & template

Every DP solution is four decisions you should say out loud: (1) State — what does dp[i] mean in one sentence? (2) Transition — how does dp[i] combine smaller answers? (3) Base case — the smallest subproblem's value. (4) Answer — which cell to read. Write the table first; only then optimise space by noticing the transition reaches back a fixed distance, so you can carry a couple of rolling variables instead of the whole array. A 1-D "bottom-up over the amount/index" loop and a 2-D "fill row by row" loop cover almost everything below.

// 1-D bottom-up: dp[i] from a fixed window of earlier answers.
int[] dp = new int[n + 1];
dp[0] = base;                         // smallest subproblem
for (int i = 1; i <= n; i++) {
    for (each choice c at i)          // e.g. last coin, split point, step size
        dp[i] = combine(dp[i], dp[i - c]);   // min / max / += depending on the goal
}
return dp[n];

// Space cut: if dp[i] only reads dp[i-1], dp[i-2], carry two scalars.
int prev2 = base0, prev1 = base1;
for (int i = 2; i <= n; i++) {
    int cur = combine(prev1, prev2);
    prev2 = prev1; prev1 = cur;
}
return prev1;

// 2-D table (two strings / grid): dp[i][j] from up, left, and diagonal.
int[][] dp = new int[m + 1][n + 1];
// seed dp[i][0] and dp[0][j], then:
for (int i = 1; i <= m; i++)
    for (int j = 1; j <= n; j++)
        dp[i][j] = match ? dp[i-1][j-1] + 1
                         : pick(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1], dp[i-1][j-1]);

📘 Deep dive

1 Climbing Stairs LC 70 ↗ Easy ⚪ Core

Problem. You can climb 1 or 2 steps at a time; count the distinct ways to reach the top of an n-step staircase.

💡 Key insight. The last move into step i is either a 1-step (from i-1) or a 2-step (from i-2), so ways(i) = ways(i-1) + ways(i-2) — it is literally Fibonacci. Only the previous two counts matter, so no array is needed.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed two rolling variables: prev2 = ways(0) = 1 and prev1 = ways(1) = 1.
  2. For i from 2 to n, compute cur = prev1 + prev2.
  3. Slide the window: prev2 = prev1; prev1 = cur.
  4. Return prev1, which now holds ways(n).

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass advancing the pair. 💾 Space O(1) — two scalars regardless of n.

⚠️ Gotchas. Both base cases are 1 (one way to stand at the bottom, one way to take a single step). For large n the count grows like Fibonacci — climbStairs(45) is already 1,836,311,903, near the int ceiling; mention you would switch to long if n grew.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I'd brute-force every sequence of 1s and 2s, but that's exponential. The recurrence is that the ways to reach step i split cleanly by my final move, giving Fibonacci, so I carry two running totals and walk up once in O(n) time and O(1) space."
📄 ClimbingStairs.java

2 House Robber LC 198 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given loot values along a street, maximise the total taken without robbing two adjacent houses (adjacent houses trip the alarm).

💡 Key insight. At house i you make a binary choice: skip it and keep best[i-1], or rob it and add best[i-2] + nums[i]. So best[i] = max(best[i-1], best[i-2] + nums[i]) — the canonical "take or skip" recurrence.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Track prev2 (best ending two houses back) and prev1 (best ending one house back), both starting at 0.
  2. For each value v, compute cur = max(prev1, prev2 + v).
  3. Slide: prev2 = prev1; prev1 = cur.
  4. Return prev1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — single pass. 💾 Space O(1) — two scalars.

⚠️ Gotchas. Initialising both rolling vars to 0 elegantly handles empty and single-element inputs (a lone house just returns its own value). Do not greedily grab every other house — alternating is not always optimal (e.g. [2,1,1,2] wants the two 2's, not the middle).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force tries every subset of non-adjacent houses. The DP insight is that my best loot through house i only depends on the best through i-1 and i-2, because robbing i forbids i-1. That's one max per house, O(n) time, and since I reach back only two slots, O(1) space."
📄 HouseRobber.java

3 House Robber II LC 213 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Same as House Robber, but the houses are arranged in a circle, so the first and last are also adjacent.

💡 Key insight. The only new wrinkle is the wrap-around: house 0 and house n-1 cannot both be robbed. Condition on that single constraint — either forbid the last house or forbid the first — and each branch becomes an ordinary linear House Robber. Answer is the max of the two runs.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If there is a single house, return it directly (no wrap-around to break).
  2. Run linear House Robber on the inclusive range [0 .. n-2] (first allowed, last excluded).
  3. Run it again on [1 .. n-1] (first excluded, last allowed).
  4. Return the larger of the two results.

⏱️ Time O(n) — two linear scans. 💾 Space O(1) — rolling variables in the helper.

⚠️ Gotchas. The n == 1 guard is essential — otherwise the ranges [0..-1] and [1..0] are empty and you would lose the only house. You do not need a third "rob neither endpoint" case: forbidding the last already permits skipping the first, so the two ranges cover every legal configuration.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The circle just couples the two endpoints. I split on that coupling into two linear subproblems — exclude the last, or exclude the first — solve each with the standard recurrence, and take the better. Two O(n) passes, still O(1) space."
📄 HouseRobberII.java

4 Jump Game LC 55 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Each nums[i] is a maximum forward jump length from index i; decide whether you can reach the last index starting from index 0.

💡 Key insight. You never need to track which exact jumps you take — only the farthest index reachable so far. The DP table reach[i] = max(reach, i + nums[i]) collapses to one scalar; you fail the instant a position i exceeds that farthest reach (an unbridgeable gap).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep farthest = 0, the best index reachable so far.
  2. Scan left to right; if i > farthest, return false — you can never stand here.
  3. Otherwise relax farthest = max(farthest, i + nums[i]).
  4. The moment farthest >= n-1, return true.

⏱️ Time O(n) — each index relaxes the reach once. 💾 Space O(1) — only the running farthest index.

⚠️ Gotchas. A value of 0 is not automatically fatal — it only traps you if no earlier index's reach already clears it ([2,0,0] succeeds because index 0 jumps past the zeros). The check i > farthest must come before relaxing, or you would read a stale reach. This greedy is provably equivalent to the DP, which is why it beats an O(n²) "can I reach j from any i" table.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Reachability is monotone: if I can reach index i, I can reach everything up to i + nums[i]. So I just sweep once, maintaining the farthest reachable index, and the answer is whether the last index falls within it. O(n) time, O(1) space — no need for the full reachability DP."
📄 JumpGame.java

5 Jump Game II LC 45 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Same jump rules, but now return the minimum number of jumps needed to reach the last index (a solution is guaranteed to exist).

💡 Key insight. This is breadth-first search over "levels" of reach, done greedily in O(1) space. Every index reachable within j jumps forms a contiguous prefix [0, curEnd]; while scanning that level you track farthest, the boundary of the next level. Each time the scan index hits curEnd you have exhausted the level, so you spend one jump and jump the boundary out to farthest.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Initialise jumps = 0, curEnd = 0 (end of current level), farthest = 0.
  2. Scan i from 0 to n-2, relaxing farthest = max(farthest, i + nums[i]).
  3. When i == curEnd, increment jumps and set curEnd = farthest — commit to the next level.
  4. Return jumps.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass; each index updates farthest once. 💾 Space O(1) — three scalar pointers, no queue.

⚠️ Gotchas. Loop must stop at n-2, not n-1: reaching the last index inside the current level costs no extra jump, and iterating to n-1 would over-count by one. The single-element array correctly returns 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I think of it as BFS by reach-levels: all indices reachable in k jumps are a prefix. I scan that prefix tracking the farthest I can extend, and when I exhaust the current prefix I pay one jump and extend the frontier. That's the BFS layer count without an explicit queue — O(n) and O(1)."
📄 JumpGameII.java

6 Coin Change LC 322 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Given coin denominations (unlimited supply) and a target amount, return the fewest coins that sum to exactly the amount, or -1 if impossible.

💡 Key insight. This is an unbounded knapsack on the amount axis. Define dp[a] = fewest coins summing to exactly a, and consider which coin is the last one used: dp[a] = min over c of dp[a-c] + 1. Sweeping amounts ascending and reading dp[a-c] of the same row lets each coin be reused any number of times.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Fill dp with a sentinel "infinity" of amount + 1 (no real answer can exceed amount coins); set dp[0] = 0.
  2. For each amount a from 1 to amount, try every coin c <= a and relax dp[a] = min(dp[a], dp[a-c] + 1).
  3. If dp[amount] is still the sentinel (> amount), return -1; otherwise return dp[amount].

⏱️ Time O(amount · coins) — each amount tries every coin once. 💾 Space O(amount) — one dp row.

⚠️ Gotchas. Use the amount + 1 sentinel rather than Integer.MAX_VALUE — the latter overflows when you add 1. This is a min problem so loop order does not affect correctness (unlike the counting variant). Greedy "largest coin first" is wrong for arbitrary denominations (e.g. coins [1,3,4], amount 6 → DP gives 2 (3+3), greedy gives 3 (4+1+1)).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I decide which coin I place last; that leaves a smaller amount to solve optimally, so dp[a] = 1 + min over coins of dp[a - coin]. I build the table from 0 up to the target, reusing coins by reading the same row. O(amount × coins) time, O(amount) space, and I guard impossibility with an infinity sentinel."
📄 CoinChange.java

7 Coin Change II LC 518 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Given coin denominations (unlimited supply) and an amount, count the number of distinct combinations that sum to it (order does not matter).

💡 Key insight. dp[a] = number of combinations summing to a using the coins processed so far; folding in a coin c does dp[a] += dp[a-c]. The make-or-break detail is loop order: putting coins on the outer loop introduces each coin once, so {1,2} and {2,1} collapse to one combination. Swapping the loops would count ordered permutations instead.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Set dp[0] = 1 — exactly one way to make 0 (take nothing).
  2. For each coin c (outer loop), sweep amounts a from c to amount and add dp[a] += dp[a-c].
  3. Return dp[amount].

⏱️ Time O(amount · coins) — each (coin, amount) pair relaxed once. 💾 Space O(amount) — single rolling row reused across coins.

⚠️ Gotchas. The loop order is the entire trick — be ready to state precisely why coins-outer counts combinations: each coin's contribution is added on top of all combinations that ignore later coins, so no ordering is double-counted. The dp[0] = 1 empty-combination base is what makes a coin equal to the amount count as a valid single-coin solution.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I count combinations, so I must avoid treating reorderings as distinct. I iterate coins in the outer loop and amounts inner; that way I commit to using a coin type before moving on, which counts each unordered multiset exactly once. dp[0] = 1 seeds the empty set, and it runs in O(amount × coins)."
📄 CoinChangeII.java

8 Decode Ways LC 91 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. A digit string encodes letters via A=1 … Z=26; count how many distinct letter strings it could decode to.

💡 Key insight. Two transitions feed dp[i] (decodings of the prefix of length i): the last digit standing alone as a letter (valid for '1'–'9') contributes dp[i-1], and the last two digits forming "10"–"26" contributes dp[i-2]. A leading '0' decodes nothing alone — it survives only as the second digit of 10 or 20.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. If the string starts with '0', return 0 — no valid first letter.
  2. Seed rolling counters prev2 = 1 (empty prefix) and prev1 = 1 (first char known non-zero).
  3. For each position i: add prev1 if the current digit is non-zero; add prev2 if the two-digit value is in 10–26.
  4. Slide the pair and return prev1.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one pass over the string. 💾 Space O(1) — two rolling counters.

⚠️ Gotchas. Zeros are the whole difficulty: "06" is invalid (leading zero on the pair), "30" is invalid (no letter 30 and 0 can't stand alone), but "10" and "20" decode uniquely. Bound the two-digit value strictly to 10–26, and remember a standalone '0' contributes nothing, which can drive a count to 0 mid-string (e.g. "2101").
🗣️ Explain out loud. "It's Fibonacci-flavoured: each prefix's decodings come from taking the last digit as one letter or the last two as a teens/twenties letter. I add dp[i-1] when the single digit is 1–9 and dp[i-2] when the pair is 10–26, carefully killing invalid zeros. Two rolling counters give O(n) time, O(1) space."
📄 DecodeWays.java

9 Longest Increasing Subsequence LC 300 ↗ Medium 🟥 Staff-tagged

Problem. Return the length of the longest strictly increasing subsequence of an integer array.

💡 Key insight. Patience sorting beats the quadratic DP. Maintain tails, where tails[k] is the smallest possible tail of any increasing subsequence of length k+1. For each x, binary-search the first tail >= x and overwrite it (a smaller tail makes future extension easier); if none is >= x, append. The length of tails is the LIS length.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a tails array and a running size (the current LIS length).
  2. For each x, lower-bound search for the leftmost tail >= x.
  3. Overwrite tails[lo] = x; if lo == size, increment size (x started a strictly longer run).
  4. Return size.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — one binary search per element. 💾 Space O(n) — the tails array.

⚠️ Gotchas. tails is not an actual subsequence — only its length is meaningful; do not try to read the sequence out of it. Lower-bound (first tail >= x) gives strictly increasing; for a non-strict (>=) variant you would switch to an upper-bound search. The simpler O(n²) DP (dp[i] = 1 + max dp[j] for j<i, nums[j]<nums[i]) is correct but too slow for large inputs.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "The O(n²) DP scans all earlier smaller elements per position. To hit O(n log n), I keep the smallest tail achievable for each subsequence length; that array stays sorted, so I binary-search where each new value fits, replacing a tail or extending the longest run. Its size is the answer."
🔁 Google follow-up. A very common Google extension: "what is the longest increasing subsequence you can get if you are allowed to replace exactly one element with any value?" Be ready to discuss prefix/suffix LIS arrays (best increasing run ending at i and starting at j) and stitching them across a replaced gap, rather than restarting the whole algorithm.
📄 LongestIncreasingSubsequence.java

10 Unique Paths LC 62 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Count the paths from the top-left to the bottom-right of an m × n grid moving only right or down.

💡 Key insight. Each cell is entered only from above or from the left, so dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j] + dp[i][j-1], with the entire top row and left column equal to 1 (one straight-line path). Processing row by row, the old dp[j] still holds the value from the row above, so adding the just-updated dp[j-1] collapses the grid to one rolling row.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Initialise a width-n row to all 1s (the top row).
  2. For each subsequent row i, for j from 1, update dp[j] += dp[j-1].
  3. Return dp[n-1].

⏱️ Time O(m · n) — visit every cell once. 💾 Space O(n) — one row of width n.

⚠️ Gotchas. Mention the closed form as a bonus: the answer is the binomial C(m+n-2, m-1) — choosing which of the m+n-2 moves go down — which is O(min(m,n)) to compute but risks intermediate overflow if implemented naively. The rolling-row DP is the safe interview default.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Paths into a cell are the sum of paths into the cell above and to the left, edges being all 1. I fill row by row, and because a fresh row reuses the previous row's values in place, I only need a single array — O(m·n) time, O(n) space. I'd also note it's just C(m+n-2, m-1)."
📄 UniquePaths.java

11 Unique Paths II LC 63 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Same right/down grid walk, but some cells contain obstacles (marked 1) that no path may pass through; count the valid paths.

💡 Key insight. It is the Unique Paths recurrence with one extra rule: an obstacle cell has zero paths terminating on it, so set dp[j] = 0 there instead of summing. The rolling row again carries the previous row's values; a free cell does dp[j] += dp[j-1].
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed dp[0] = 1 unless the start cell is blocked (then 0 — no paths at all).
  2. For each cell: if it is an obstacle set dp[j] = 0; else if j > 0 add dp[j] += dp[j-1].
  3. A free cell in column 0 simply keeps the from-above value untouched.
  4. Return dp[n-1].

⏱️ Time O(m · n) — every cell relaxed once. 💾 Space O(n) — a single rolling row.

⚠️ Gotchas. A blocked start cell zeroes the whole answer — handle it in the seed. In column 0, an obstacle propagates downward automatically: once dp[0] becomes 0 it stays 0 for the rest of the column (a wall cuts off everything below it). Do not unconditionally reset the first column to 1 like the obstacle-free version does.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Same additive grid DP, but obstacles are sinks with zero paths, so I zero those cells instead of summing. The rolling row handles propagation for free — a blocked cell in a column kills everything beneath it. O(m·n) time, O(n) space, with care on the start cell and first column."
📄 UniquePathsII.java

12 Maximal Square LC 221 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. In a binary matrix of '0'/'1', find the area of the largest square containing only 1s.

💡 Key insight. Let dp[i][j] be the side length of the largest all-ones square whose bottom-right corner is (i,j). A square of side k ending here needs three squares of side k-1 ending just above, just left, and up-left to all coexist, so for a '1' cell dp[i][j] = 1 + min(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1], dp[i-1][j-1]); a '0' forces 0. Track the max side and square it.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Pad the table with a zero border row and column so edge cells need no special case.
  2. For each cell that is '1', set dp[i][j] = 1 + min of the three neighbours (up, left, up-left).
  3. Update maxSide as you go.
  4. Return maxSide * maxSide — the problem wants area, not side.

⏱️ Time O(m · n) — each cell from three neighbours in O(1). 💾 Space O(m · n) — the dp table (reducible to one row).

⚠️ Gotchas. The min of three is the crux — it is what guarantees a full square rather than a rectangle; using just two neighbours is a classic wrong answer. Remember to return the area (side²), not the side. The zero-padding border is what lets the first real row/column be handled by the same formula. Mention you can drop to O(n) space with one rolling row plus a saved diagonal.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I anchor each square at its bottom-right corner; that cell can extend a square only as far as the weakest of its top, left, and diagonal neighbours allow, hence 1 + min of those three. I sweep once tracking the largest side, then return its square. O(m·n) time, and O(n) space if I roll the rows."
📄 MaximalSquare.java

13 Word Break LC 139 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Given a string s and a dictionary, decide whether s can be segmented into a sequence of one or more dictionary words.

💡 Key insight. dp[i] = "can the prefix s[0..i) be fully segmented." It is true iff there is a split point j < i where dp[j] is already true and the trailing chunk s[j..i) is a dictionary word. The empty prefix dp[0] is true; the answer is dp[n].
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Put the dictionary in a hash set for O(L) membership tests.
  2. Set dp[0] = true.
  3. For each end i, scan starts j < i; if dp[j] and s[j..i) is in the set, mark dp[i] = true and break.
  4. Return dp[n].

⏱️ Time O(n²·L) — for each end, scan all starts; substring + lookup cost L. 💾 Space O(n) — the dp array plus the word set.

⚠️ Gotchas. You can break on the first valid split since you only need feasibility, not a count. Converting the list to a set is what keeps lookups O(L); scanning the list each time would add a factor. If the interviewer asks to return all segmentations, that is a different (exponential) backtracking problem — do not conflate it with this boolean DP.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I ask, for each prefix length, whether it splits into a good prefix plus a dictionary word ending here. That gives dp[i] = OR over split points of dp[j] AND s[j..i) in dict, seeded with the empty prefix true. Hash-set lookups make it O(n²·L) time and O(n) space."
📄 WordBreak.java

14 Longest Common Subsequence LC 1143 ↗ Medium ⚪ Core

Problem. Return the length of the longest subsequence common to two strings (characters in order, not necessarily contiguous).

💡 Key insight. dp[i][j] = LCS length of the first i chars of a and first j of b. If the current chars match, they pair up and extend the diagonal subproblem: dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1] + 1; otherwise drop one end and take max(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1]). Row and column 0 are empty-prefix zeros.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate an (m+1) × (n+1) table; the zero borders are the base cases.
  2. For each i, j: if a[i-1] == b[j-1], set dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1] + 1.
  3. Else set dp[i][j] = max(dp[i-1][j], dp[i][j-1]).
  4. Return dp[m][n].

⏱️ Time O(m · n) — fill every cell once. 💾 Space O(m · n) — the full grid (reducible to two rows).

⚠️ Gotchas. Subsequence, not substring — a mismatch does not reset to 0 (that would be Longest Common Substring); you carry forward the best of dropping either character. The full 2-D table is kept here precisely so you can backtrack the actual subsequence if asked; if you only need the length, two rolling rows cut space to O(min(m,n)).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I compare prefixes: equal end characters extend the diagonal by one, otherwise I drop one side and keep the better. That fills an (m+1)×(n+1) table in O(m·n). I'd keep the full table to reconstruct the subsequence, or roll two rows if only the length matters."
📄 LongestCommonSubsequence.java

15 Edit Distance LC 72 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Compute the minimum number of single-character insertions, deletions, or replacements to turn word1 into word2 (Levenshtein distance).

💡 Key insight. dp[i][j] = min edits to turn the first i chars of word1 into the first j of word2. If the current characters match, inherit dp[i-1][j-1] for free; otherwise pay 1 plus the cheapest of three moves — delete (dp[i-1][j]), insert (dp[i][j-1]), or replace (dp[i-1][j-1]).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Seed the base row/column: dp[i][0] = i (delete i chars) and dp[0][j] = j (insert j chars).
  2. For each i, j: on a match set dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1].
  3. On a mismatch set dp[i][j] = 1 + min(replace, delete, insert).
  4. Return dp[m][n].

⏱️ Time O(m · n) — each cell from three neighbours in O(1). 💾 Space O(m · n) — full table (reducible to two rows).

⚠️ Gotchas. Get the three neighbour-to-operation mapping right and say it out loud — interviewers probe it: delete moves up a row, insert moves left a column, replace moves diagonally. The base cases are not zeros — they encode building a string from empty by pure inserts/deletes. Be ready to map each of the three predecessor cells to its operation when reconstructing the edit script.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I align prefixes of the two words. Matching characters cost nothing and move diagonally; otherwise I take one plus the cheapest of insert, delete, or replace, each a specific neighbour. Base rows are pure-insert / pure-delete costs. It's O(m·n) time and space, two rows if I only need the number."
📄 EditDistance.java

16 Student Attendance Record II LC 552 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Count length-n attendance records over {A, L, P} that are "rewardable": at most one 'A' total and no run of 3 or more consecutive 'L's. Return the count mod 1e9+7.

💡 Key insight. The validity constraint depends only on a bounded summary of the suffix, so it is a finite state machine over (a, l): a = total absences so far (0 or 1) and l = number of trailing 'L's (0, 1, or 2) — six states. dp[a][l] counts records of the current length ending in that state; roll it n times and sum all six buckets.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Start with dp[0][0] = 1 (the empty record: 0 absences, 0 trailing lates).
  2. For each of n steps, build next from dp: append 'P' → (a, 0); append 'A' only if a == 0 → (1, 0); append 'L' only if l < 2 → (a, l+1).
  3. Take every addition mod 1e9+7.
  4. Sum all six buckets of the final dp for the answer.

⏱️ Time O(n) — constant work (six states) per appended character. 💾 Space O(1) — a fixed 2×3 state table.

⚠️ Gotchas. Both 'A' and 'P' reset the trailing-late count to 0 — an 'A' breaks any 'L' run just like a 'P' does; forgetting this is the most common bug. Apply the modulus on every accumulation, not just at the end, to avoid long overflow. n = 0 must return 1 (the empty record is rewardable). Be ready to mention this can be pushed to O(log n) with matrix exponentiation of the 6×6 transition if n is astronomically large.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Rewardability only cares about how many A's I've used and how many L's trail the end, so I model six states and count records ending in each. Appending P, A, or L is a fixed transition with two guards, so each step is O(1) over six buckets — O(n) time, O(1) space, summing all states at the end mod 1e9+7."
📄 StudentAttendanceRecordII.java

17 Poor Pigs LC 458 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. One of buckets is poisoned. With minutesToTest total and a pig dying minutesToDie after drinking, find the minimum pigs needed to identify the poisoned bucket.

💡 Key insight. This is information theory, not search. With t = minutesToTest / minutesToDie rounds available, a single pig's observable outcome is "died in round 1, 2, …, t, or never died" = t + 1 distinguishable states. Pigs are independent, so p pigs encode (t+1)^p joint states, and you need at least one state per bucket: the minimum p satisfies (t+1)^p >= buckets.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Compute statesPerPig = minutesToTest / minutesToDie + 1 (the t + 1 outcomes).
  2. Multiply a running product by statesPerPig, incrementing a pig counter, until it reaches or exceeds buckets.
  3. Return the pig count — effectively ceil(log_{t+1}(buckets)) computed with integers only.

⏱️ Time O(log_{t+1} buckets) — one multiply per pig added. 💾 Space O(1) — a running product and counter.

⚠️ Gotchas. This is the odd one out — labelled DP by convention but really a counting/encoding argument; do not look for a table. Compute the answer by repeated multiplication, not Math.ceil(Math.log(...)), to dodge floating-point rounding errors. Edge case: 1 bucket needs 0 pigs (nothing to test). The "+1" for the never-died outcome is the most-missed part of the count.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Each pig is a base-(t+1) digit: it can die in any of t rounds or survive, so t+1 states, where t is how many die-cycles fit in the test window. With p independent pigs I distinguish (t+1)^p buckets, so I need the smallest p with (t+1)^p ≥ buckets — I find it by integer multiplication to avoid log rounding."
📄 PoorPigs.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the groups in order — they build on each other. 1-D first: Climbing Stairs → House Robber → House Robber II → Decode Ways; from a blank file, re-derive the "take or skip" recurrence and then prove to yourself you can drop the array to two rolling variables. Greedy-DP pair: Jump Game then Jump Game II — articulate why the farthest-reach scan is equivalent to the reachability DP, and frame Jump Game II as BFS-by-levels without a queue. Knapsack pair: Coin Change vs Coin Change II — the single most testable idea here is loop order (combinations need coins-outer); be able to explain why on a whiteboard. Grid trio: Unique Paths → Unique Paths II → Maximal Square — internalise that Maximal Square's min-of-three is what forces a square. Two-string trio: LCS → Edit Distance, then re-derive the neighbour→operation mapping cold. Finale: Student Attendance Record II (define the six states from scratch) and Poor Pigs (the base-(t+1) encoding argument).

Google-favourite follow-ups to rehearse out loud: (1) LIS with "replace exactly one element" — prefix/suffix LIS arrays stitched across a gap. (2) Coin Change "count combinations vs permutations" — flip the loops and explain the difference. (3) Student Attendance Record II via matrix exponentiation for O(log n). (4) Maximal Square / Edit Distance reduced from O(m·n) to O(n) space, and reconstructing the actual square / edit script from the table.

  • ✅ I can name the state, transition, base case, and answer cell for any of these before writing code.
  • ✅ I can explain why House Robber and Climbing Stairs collapse to two rolling variables.
  • ✅ I can explain why coins-outer counts combinations while amount-outer counts permutations.
  • ✅ I can justify the greedy farthest-reach in Jump Game as equivalent to the reachability DP.
  • ✅ I can explain why Maximal Square uses min of three neighbours (square, not rectangle) and returns area.
  • ✅ I can map each of Edit Distance's three predecessor cells to delete / insert / replace.
  • ✅ I can derive the six (absences, trailing-lates) states for Student Attendance Record II and why 'A' resets the L-run.
  • ✅ I can explain Poor Pigs as a base-(t+1) encoding and avoid the floating-point log trap.
  • ✅ I can sketch the LIS "replace one element" follow-up with prefix/suffix arrays.

🔢 Bit Manipulation & Math / Sampling

This family covers the low-level toolkit Google loves as warm-ups and twists: reasoning about individual bits, reconstructing arithmetic from XOR/AND/shift, fast binary exponentiation, and — the real differentiator — randomized sampling (reservoir sampling and prefix-sum + binary search) where the interview is really about proving uniformity, not writing code. High ROI because each one is short to code but easy to get subtly wrong, so they make excellent signal-per-minute screening questions.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 5 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Do it without the + / * / / operator" — rebuild arithmetic from XOR, AND, and shifts.
  • Asked for popcount of every number 0..n, or anything per-index that relates value i to i>>1 or i & (i-1).
  • An exponent in the bounds (n up to 2^31) and a naive O(n) loop would TLE → binary exponentiation.
  • "Pick uniformly at random" from a stream / linked list of unknown or unbounded length → reservoir sampling.
  • "Pick an index with probability proportional to a weight" → prefix sums + lower-bound binary search.
  • The follow-up is "prove every element is equally likely" — the math, not the loop, is the point.

🧠 Core idea & template

Two reusable engines live here. For bit/math problems, lean on the identities: a + b = (a^b) + ((a&b)<<1) (sum without carry, plus carry), popcount(i) = popcount(i>>1) + (i&1), and x^n = ∏ x^(2^k) over set bits of n. For sampling, weighted pick is just "lay weights on a number line, binary-search a uniform dart," and unknown-length uniform pick is reservoir sampling: keep the k-th item with probability 1/k and induction gives 1/n for all.

// Reservoir sampling, size 1 — uniform over an unknown-length stream
int chosen = first;
int k = 1;
for (T item : streamAfterFirst) {
    k++;
    if (rng.nextInt(k) == 0) chosen = item;   // keep with prob 1/k
}
// Weighted random index — prefix sums + lower-bound binary search
int target = rng.nextInt(total) + 1;          // uniform in [1, total]
int lo = 0, hi = prefix.length - 1;
while (lo < hi) {
    int mid = (lo + hi) >>> 1;
    if (prefix[mid] < target) lo = mid + 1; else hi = mid;
}
return lo;                                     // first prefix sum >= target

📘 Deep dive

1 Counting Bits LC 338 ↗ Easy 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Return an array where entry i is the number of set bits (popcount) of i, for all i in 0..n.

💡 Key insight. i shares every bit except its lowest with i>>1, so popcount(i) = popcount(i>>1) + (i&1). That turns each answer into an O(1) lookup of an already-computed smaller index — pure bottom-up DP, no per-number bit loop.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Allocate dp[0..n] with dp[0] = 0.
  2. For i from 1 to n: dp[i] = dp[i >> 1] + (i & 1) — reuse the count for i with its last bit removed, then add 1 if i is odd.
  3. Return dp.

⏱️ Time O(n) — one constant-work transition per index 💾 Space O(n) — the answer array (O(1) auxiliary beyond output)

⚠️ Gotchas. The naive Integer.bitCount-per-number is O(n log n) and misses the point — interviewers want the DP recurrence. An alternative recurrence dp[i] = dp[i & (i-1)] + 1 (drop the lowest set bit) is equally valid; be ready to derive either. Don't forget index 0.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force is popcount each number independently. But i in binary is i>>1 with one extra low bit appended, so its count is the count of i>>1 plus whether i is odd. That's an O(1) transition over already-solved subproblems, giving a single O(n) pass."
📄 CountingBits.java

2 Sum of Two Integers LC 371 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Compute a + b without using the + or - operators.

💡 Key insight. Addition splits into two independent bitwise parts: a ^ b is the sum ignoring carries, and (a & b) << 1 is exactly the carry bits shifted into their next column. Feed the carry back as the new b and repeat until no carry remains — that is a ripple-carry adder.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. While b != 0: compute carry = (a & b) << 1.
  2. Set a = a ^ b (columns summed without carrying).
  3. Set b = carry (re-add the carry next pass).
  4. Return a once the carry is gone.

⏱️ Time O(1) — at most 32 iterations, one per bit width 💾 Space O(1) — only scalar registers

⚠️ Gotchas. It "just works" for negatives because Java ints are two's-complement and XOR/AND/shift operate on the raw bit pattern. The loop always terminates because each pass pushes carries strictly leftward until they fall off bit 31. In languages without fixed-width ints (Python) you must mask to 32 bits — call that out.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I model a hardware adder: XOR gives the bitwise sum with no carry, AND-then-shift gives where carries go. I loop, folding the carry back in, until there's nothing left to carry. Two's complement makes negatives fall out for free, and it's bounded by the 32-bit width so it's effectively constant time."
📄 SumOfTwoIntegers.java

3 Pow(x, n) LC 50 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Compute x raised to the power n (a possibly negative 32-bit integer) in better than O(n).

💡 Key insight. x^n is the product of x^(2^k) over the set bits of n (binary exponentiation). Square the base each step and multiply it into the result only when the current low bit is 1 — O(log n) multiplications instead of n.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Widen n to a long exp first, so negating Integer.MIN_VALUE is safe.
  2. If exp < 0: replace x with 1/x and set exp = -exp.
  3. Loop while exp > 0: if (exp & 1) == 1 multiply result *= x; then x *= x and exp >>= 1.
  4. Return result.

⏱️ Time O(log n) — one squaring per bit of the exponent 💾 Space O(1) — a handful of scalars (iterative, no recursion stack)

⚠️ Gotchas. The classic trap: n = Integer.MIN_VALUE means -n overflows a 32-bit int. Widening to long before negating fixes it. Watch floating-point underflow (e.g. 2^-2^31 → 0.0) — that's expected, not a crash. A recursive version is fine but uses O(log n) stack.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Multiplying x by itself n times is O(n). Instead I read n in binary: every set bit k contributes a factor of x^(2^k), and I get those by repeated squaring. Negative exponents flip x to 1/x. The one landmine is MIN_VALUE not being negatable as an int, so I widen to long first."
📄 PowXN.java

4 Linked List Random Node LC 382 ↗ Medium ⭐ Google fav

Problem. Return a uniformly random node's value from a singly linked list whose length is unknown and possibly huge, using O(1) extra space.

💡 Key insight. Reservoir sampling of size 1: walk the list keeping one chosen value, and at the k-th node (1-indexed) replace the choice with probability 1/k. By induction every node ends with probability exactly 1/n, so you never need to know n in advance or buffer the list.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Initialize chosen = head.val, counter k = 1.
  2. For each subsequent node, increment k; with probability 1/k (i.e. rng.nextInt(k) == 0) set chosen to that node's value.
  3. Return chosen after the pass.

⏱️ Time O(n) per getRandom — one pass; constructor O(1) 💾 Space O(1) — no buffering regardless of list length

⚠️ Gotchas. The proof is the interview: node i survives if it's picked (1/i) and then never overwritten ((i/(i+1))·…·((n-1)/n)), which telescopes to 1/n. If the list fit in memory you could store it and index in O(1) — but the constraint is unknown/streaming length, which is exactly why reservoir wins. Single-node list must return that node.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "If I knew the length I'd just pick a random index. I don't, and it might be a stream, so I use reservoir sampling: hold one candidate and at node k swap it in with probability 1/k. The telescoping product shows every node lands at 1/n. It's one O(n) pass and O(1) memory."
📄 LinkedListRandomNode.java

5 Random Pick with Weight LC 528 ↗ Medium 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Given weights w, return index i with probability proportional to w[i] on each pickIndex() call.

💡 Key insight. Lay the weights end-to-end on a number line: index i owns the interval (prefix[i-1], prefix[i]]. A uniform integer dart in [1, total] lands in index i with probability proportional to w[i]; the first prefix sum >= target — found by lower-bound binary search — is that index.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Constructor: build prefix as the running sum of w; remember total = prefix[n-1].
  2. pickIndex: draw target = rng.nextInt(total) + 1 (uniform in [1, total]).
  3. Lower-bound binary search for the smallest index whose prefix sum is >= target; return it.

⏱️ Time O(n) constructor, O(log n) per pick 💾 Space O(n) — the prefix-sum array

⚠️ Gotchas. Off-by-one is the killer: drawing [1, total] (not [0, total)) pairs with the half-open interval (prefix[i-1], prefix[i]] so the lower-bound search lands correctly. Use the lo < hi / hi = mid lower-bound shape, not a "found exact" search, since target rarely equals a prefix value. Use >>> for the midpoint to avoid overflow.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I turn weights into a CDF via prefix sums. Each index owns a slice of the line whose length is its weight, so a uniform dart in [1, total] hits it with the right probability. Finding which slice the dart fell into is a lower-bound binary search — O(log n) per query after an O(n) build."
📄 RandomPickWithWeight.java

🔁 Practice plan

Warm up with Counting Bits (338) to lock the i>>1 recurrence, then Sum of Two Integers (371) and Pow(x,n) (50) to cement the bit identities and the MIN_VALUE/overflow guards. Spend the real time on sampling: re-derive Linked List Random Node (382) and Random Pick with Weight (528) from a blank file and, crucially, write the uniformity proof out loud — that's the L6 signal. Google follow-ups to rehearse: extend 382 to reservoir sampling of size k; extend 528 to "remove/update a weight" (switch to a Fenwick tree for O(log n) updates); and "shuffle an array" / Random Pick Index (398) as sibling drills.

  • ✅ I can write the popcount DP and state both recurrences (i>>1 and i & (i-1)).
  • ✅ I can explain why XOR+carry reproduces addition and why it terminates in ≤32 steps.
  • ✅ I can derive binary exponentiation and name the Integer.MIN_VALUE overflow fix.
  • ✅ I can prove reservoir sampling gives 1/n via the telescoping product.
  • ✅ I can justify the [1, total] draw + lower-bound search for weighted pick, off-by-ones included.

🌴 Segment Tree / Fenwick (BIT)

When an array must answer range queries and absorb point updates, neither a plain array (O(n) query) nor a prefix-sum array (O(n) update) is enough — you need a balanced tree of partial aggregates. Fenwick (Binary Indexed) trees give O(log n) prefix sums and updates in a few lines using the low-bit trick; the divide-and-conquer mindset also powers "count pairs with a range constraint" problems via merge sort. These show up at Google L6 as the upgrade path the moment an interviewer says "now support updates" or "count how many subarray sums fall in a range."

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 2 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • "Range sum/min/max query" interleaved with point or range updates → Fenwick tree or segment tree.
  • A prefix-sum solution is the obvious first idea but updates would be O(n) each → upgrade to a BIT.
  • "Count subarrays whose sum lies in [lower, upper]" or "count smaller/greater elements to the right" → prefix sums + (BIT or merge-sort counting).
  • You need ordered-statistic counting ("how many earlier prefixes fall in a window") while keeping data sorted → merge sort with a counting step.
  • Constraints up to ~10^5 with many mixed operations, so each op must be O(log n).

🧠 Core idea & template

A Fenwick tree stores partial sums so that node i covers the i & -i elements ending at i — the lowest set bit isolates the block it owns. Walking down by i -= i & -i accumulates a prefix sum; walking up by i += i & -i propagates a point update; each touches O(log n) nodes, and a range sum is a difference of two prefixes. The merge-sort counting idiom is the cousin: while merging two already-sorted halves, slide a window to count cross-pairs satisfying a constraint in linear time per level.

// Fenwick (Binary Indexed Tree), 1-indexed
int[] tree = new int[n + 1];
void update(int i, int delta) {                 // i is 1-indexed
    for (; i <= n; i += i & -i) tree[i] += delta;   // climb to each owning node
}
int prefix(int i) {                             // sum of first i elements
    int s = 0;
    for (; i > 0; i -= i & -i) s += tree[i];        // drop lowest set bit each step
    return s;
}
int rangeSum(int l, int r) { return prefix(r) - prefix(l - 1); }

// Merge-sort counting: count cross-pairs in [lower, upper] over sorted halves
int start = mid + 1, end = mid + 1;
for (int i = lo; i <= mid; i++) {
    while (start <= hi && p[start] - p[i] <  lower) start++;
    while (end   <= hi && p[end]   - p[i] <= upper) end++;
    count += end - start;                       // both bounds advance monotonically -> O(n)
}

🗺️ Problems at a glance

#ProblemLCDiffPriorityTechniqueTimeSpace
1Range Sum Query - Mutable LC 307 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill Fenwick (BIT) prefix sumsO(log n)/opO(n)
2Count of Range Sum LC 327 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature Prefix sums + merge-sort countingO(n log n)O(n)

📘 Deep dive

1 Range Sum Query - Mutable LC 307 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Support update(index, val) and sumRange(left, right) on an integer array, both efficiently and interleaved.

💡 Key insight. A Fenwick (Binary Indexed) tree stores partial sums indexed so that node i covers the i & -i elements ending at i — that low bit isolates the block this node owns. Walking down by i -= i & -i accumulates a prefix sum; walking up by i += i & -i propagates a point update; each touches only O(log n) nodes. A range sum is the difference of two prefix sums.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep a 1-indexed tree[n+1] and a copy of the current nums (to compute deltas).
  2. Build by calling update(i, nums[i]) for each element.
  3. update(index, val): compute delta = val - nums[index], store the new value, then for i = index+1; i <= n; i += i & -i add delta to tree[i].
  4. sumRange(left, right) = prefix(right+1) - prefix(left), where prefix(i) loops i -= i & -i summing tree[i].

⏱️ Time O(log n) per update and per sumRange; O(n log n) build 💾 Space O(n) — the BIT plus the values copy for deltas

⚠️ Gotchas. The tree is 1-indexed; converting a 0-indexed array position means index+1, and sumRange uses prefix(right+1) - prefix(left) (not right). You must store the old value to compute the delta — a Fenwick tree adds deltas, it can't overwrite a position directly. i & -i relies on two's-complement; it isolates the lowest set bit. A build via n updates is O(n log n) — fine here, but mention the O(n) linear build if pressed.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A prefix-sum array gives O(1) queries but O(n) updates; a plain array is the reverse. A Fenwick tree balances both at O(log n) by storing sums of binary-aligned blocks. The low-bit i & -i tells me the size of the block a node owns, so I climb by adding it on updates and descend by subtracting it on prefix queries. Range sum is just prefix(r) minus prefix(l-1)."
📄 RangeSumQueryMutable.java

2 Count of Range Sum LC 327 ↗ Hard 🟦 Google signature

Problem. Count the number of contiguous subarrays whose sum lies in [lower, upper] inclusive.

💡 Key insight. The sum of subarray (i, j) equals prefix[j+1] - prefix[i], so a range sum in [lower, upper] becomes: for each right prefix P, count left prefixes L with P - upper <= L <= P - lower. Embedding this count inside merge sort exploits that each half is already sorted — a two-pointer window over the right half tallies the valid left prefixes in linear time per merge, while the merge itself restores sorted order.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Build prefix[0..n] as a long[] (sums can overflow int).
  2. Run merge sort on the prefix array. At each node, recurse left and right and accumulate their counts.
  3. With both halves sorted, slide start/end over the right half: for each left index i, advance start past prefixes where p[start] - p[i] < lower and end past where p[end] - p[i] <= upper; add end - start. Both pointers only move forward → O(n) per level.
  4. Merge the two halves back into sorted order and return the total count.

⏱️ Time O(n log n) — merge sort with O(n) counting per level 💾 Space O(n) — prefix array plus the merge scratch buffer

⚠️ Gotchas. Use long for prefixes — adversarial inputs like [Integer.MIN_VALUE, Integer.MAX_VALUE] overflow int. The two windows are asymmetric: < lower (strict) for start vs <= upper (inclusive) for end, so the count is the half-open [start, end) width. The counting must happen before the merge, while both halves are still independently sorted. An alternative is a BIT/segment tree over coordinate-compressed prefix values — same O(n log n), but the merge-sort version is cleaner here.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force is O(n²) over all subarrays. I convert to prefix sums so a subarray sum is a difference of two prefixes; then counting subarrays in [lower, upper] becomes, for each right prefix, counting left prefixes in a value window. I piggyback that count on merge sort: when both halves are sorted I can sweep the window with two monotonic pointers in O(n), and the merge keeps everything sorted for the parent. Overall O(n log n), with long to avoid overflow."
📄 CountOfRangeSum.java

🔁 Practice plan

Memorize the four-line Fenwick tree cold by re-deriving Range Sum Query - Mutable (307) from a blank file until the i & -i climb/descend is automatic and you can explain the 1-indexing. Then tackle Count of Range Sum (327), which fuses prefix sums with merge-sort counting — write it twice, once getting the strict-vs-inclusive window bounds right, and rehearse the overflow argument. Google follow-ups to rehearse: Count of Smaller Numbers After Self (315) and Reverse Pairs (493) as the same merge-sort/BIT counting family; Range Sum Query 2D - Mutable (308) for a 2D Fenwick; and "support range updates" (lazy segment tree / difference-BIT) as the next escalation.

  • ✅ I can write a Fenwick tree from scratch and explain why i & -i isolates a node's block.
  • ✅ I can justify the 1-indexing and the delta-based update.
  • ✅ I can reduce subarray-sum-in-range to counting prefix differences in a value window.
  • ✅ I can embed that count in merge sort and explain the O(n) two-pointer step and the long guard.
  • ✅ I can name when to reach for a BIT vs. a segment tree vs. merge-sort counting.

🏗️ Design

"Design a data structure" problems test whether you can pick the right backing containers and keep every operation cheap as state mutates. The Google L6 signal here is API discipline: name your invariants, justify O(1)/O(log n) per method, and reason about correctness across pushes, evictions, and out-of-order updates — exactly the muscles you use when designing a service's in-memory layer.

Source: Prep Tracker & Deep Research Adds tabs · 5 problems

🎯 Recognition signals

  • The prompt literally says "implement a class" / "design X" with a fixed set of methods and tight per-call complexity targets.
  • You need an auxiliary view of the same data — a running min, a sorted multiset, recency order — kept in lockstep with the primary store.
  • Queries ask for an extreme (min/max/median) or a "top-k recent" slice, hinting at a heap, a TreeMap, or twin heaps.
  • Updates can correct or remove past entries, so a naive running aggregate breaks — you must be able to retract a stale contribution.
  • Eviction policy (LRU/LFU) or capacity bound shows up — that's a doubly-linked-list + hashmap tell.

🧠 Core idea & template

Almost every design problem is "a primary map of truth + a secondary structure that makes one query fast." The art is keeping them consistent: whenever you mutate the primary store, mirror the change in the secondary one (increment/decrement a count, splice a node, push a heap entry). When you can't update an aggregate in place, store a multiset so you can decrement the old value and add the new one. Lazy deletion (skip stale entries on read) and sentinel-bounded linked lists (O(1) splice) are the two recurring tricks.

class Design {
    // 1) primary source of truth
    Map<Key, Value> store = new HashMap<>();
    // 2) secondary structure tuned for the hot query (extreme / order / recency)
    TreeMap<Value, Integer> multiset = new TreeMap<>(); // value -> count

    void update(Key k, Value v) {
        if (store.containsKey(k)) decrement(multiset, store.get(k)); // retract stale
        store.put(k, v);
        multiset.merge(v, 1, Integer::sum);                          // mirror new
    }
    Value queryExtreme() { return multiset.lastKey(); }              // O(log n)

    static <V> void decrement(TreeMap<V, Integer> ms, V v) {
        int c = ms.get(v) - 1;
        if (c == 0) ms.remove(v); else ms.put(v, c);
    }
}
