📘 Deep dive

1 Min Stack LC 155 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Build a stack supporting push, pop, top, and getMin — all in O(1).

💡 Key insight. The minimum can only change at a push or a pop boundary. So alongside each value, store the minimum of the whole stack at the moment that value was pushed. The top of this auxiliary stack is then always the current minimum, and it pops away in lockstep with the main stack — no rescanning.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep two stacks: values and mins (a running minimum per level).
  2. On push(val): push val onto values; push min(val, mins.peek()) onto mins (or just val if empty).
  3. On pop(): pop both stacks together so the running min stays aligned.
  4. top() reads values.peek(); getMin() reads mins.peek().

⏱️ Time O(1) — push/pop/top/getMin are each a single stack op 💾 Space O(n) — the min stack mirrors the value stack's depth

⚠️ Gotchas. Duplicates of the minimum must each get their own mins entry — otherwise popping one removes the min prematurely. Carrying the min per-level handles this for free. Watch for integer overflow if you instead store deltas against the min (the "single stack" optimization); the two-stack version sidesteps that entirely.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "A naive getMin would scan the stack in O(n). The trick is that the min only changes when the stack's contents change, so I pair every pushed value with the min-so-far. Reading or restoring the min becomes a peek/pop — O(1) time, O(n) extra space, and duplicates fall out correctly because each level remembers its own min."
📄 MinStack.java

2 Stock Price Fluctuation LC 2034 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Stream of (timestamp, price) records that arrive out of order and may correct earlier timestamps; support current() (price at the latest timestamp), maximum(), and minimum().

💡 Key insight. Because any past timestamp can be overwritten, you can't keep a simple running min/max. Hold a source of truth timeToPrice, and mirror its values as a TreeMap multiset price -> count. A correction decrements the old price's count before adding the new one, so the multiset always reflects only live prices — and its first/last key give min/max in O(log n).
🛠️ Approach.
  1. update(t, p): if t already exists, decrement the count of its old price in priceCounts (remove the key if it hits 0).
  2. Write timeToPrice[t] = p and priceCounts.merge(p, 1, sum).
  3. Track latestTime = max(latestTime, t) so current() is an O(1) lookup.
  4. maximum() = priceCounts.lastKey(); minimum() = priceCounts.firstKey().

⏱️ Time O(log n) — update/min/max do TreeMap work; current O(1) 💾 Space O(n) — one entry per distinct timestamp

⚠️ Gotchas. The classic bug is forgetting to retract the stale price on a correction, leaving a phantom max that no live timestamp supports. Also remember to remove a price key when its count drops to 0, or firstKey/lastKey will return a value with zero occurrences. current() uses the latest timestamp, not the latest insertion — they differ when an old timestamp is corrected.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Records can rewrite the past, so I keep timestamp→price as truth and maintain a sorted multiset of prices in parallel. On a correction I subtract the old price and add the new one, which keeps min/max honest in O(log n). current just reads the max timestamp seen, so it's O(1)."
📄 StockPriceFluctuation.java

3 Detect Squares LC 2013 ↗ Medium 🔹 Google-tagged

Problem. Support add(point) and count(point) — the number of axis-aligned squares (with positive area) the query point can form with three previously added points, counting duplicate points.

💡 Key insight. An axis-aligned square is pinned down once you fix the query point and a second point on the same vertical line (same x): the y-gap is the side length, which forces the other two corners. So only iterate candidates that share the query's x. Storing points as a multiset lets a query multiply the three other corners' counts — that product is exactly how many distinct squares those repeated points form.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. Keep pointCount (encoded (x,y) → count) and byColumn (x → multiset of y's) so you can scan a column fast.
  2. For count(qx, qy), walk every y in column qx (skip y == qy). Let side = y - qy (signed, so it selects one square per candidate).
  3. The two far corners sit one side-length to the left and to the right; multiply their counts: left = cnt(qx-side, qy) * cnt(qx-side, y), right symmetrically.
  4. Accumulate cntVertical * (left + right) over all candidate y.

⏱️ Time add O(1); count O(K) — K points share the query's x 💾 Space O(N) — one multiset entry per distinct point

⚠️ Gotchas. Skip y == qy or you'd count a degenerate zero-area "square." Multiplying corner counts (not just checking presence) is what makes duplicate points contribute correctly — a doubled corner doubles the squares. Encoding (x,y) into a single long key avoids nested-map lookups; just make sure the bit shift covers the coordinate range (here values up to 1000 fit in 20 bits).
🗣️ Explain out loud. "Brute force over triples is O(n³). Instead I anchor on the query point and any point directly above or below it — that fixes the side length and the remaining two corners. I keep a point multiset so a query just multiplies the counts of the three partner corners, summing over the column. Add is O(1); count is O(K) over the points in that column."
📄 DetectSquares.java

4 Design Twitter LC 355 ↗ Medium ◻️ Gap-fill

Problem. Implement postTweet, getNewsFeed (10 most-recent tweets from a user and everyone they follow), follow, and unfollow.

💡 Key insight. A single global, ever-increasing timestamp stamped on every tweet makes "most recent" a total order across all users. Each user keeps their own tweet list (newest last) plus a followee set. The feed is then a k-way merge: seed a max-heap with the newest tweet from each relevant user and pop 10 times, pushing the predecessor after each pop. You never scan full histories — only as deep as the 10 freshest tweets require.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. postTweet: append Tweet(id, clock++) to the user's list — O(1).
  2. getNewsFeed: collect sources (followees ∪ self). For each non-empty list, push its newest tweet [time, owner, index] into a max-heap keyed on time.
  3. Pop up to 10 times; after each pop, if the owner has an older tweet (index > 0), push [prevTime, owner, index-1].
  4. follow/unfollow: add/remove from the follower's set (ignore self-follow).

⏱️ Time post/follow/unfollow O(1); feed O(F + 10 log F) for F sources 💾 Space O(T + F) — all tweets plus follow edges

⚠️ Gotchas. A user must see their own tweets, so always add userId to the source set. Guard against self-follow corrupting the feed. Each heap entry must remember its position in the owner's list so you can walk backwards — pushing only the newest tweet per user up front keeps the heap at size F, not size T. Feed is capped at 10, so don't drain the whole heap.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "I give every tweet a monotonic timestamp, which orders all tweets globally. The feed is a 10-element k-way merge: I seed a max-heap with each followed user's latest tweet, then pop ten times, each time pulling in that user's next-older tweet. That's O(F + 10 log F) instead of merging entire histories, and posting/following stay O(1)."
📄 DesignTwitter.java

5 LFU Cache LC 460 ↗ Hard ⭐ Google fav

Problem. A fixed-capacity cache with O(1) get/put that evicts the least-frequently-used key, breaking ties by least-recently-used.

💡 Key insight. Bucket keys by frequency. Each frequency owns a doubly-linked list of exactly the keys at that count, ordered by recency (MRU at head, LRU at tail). Touching a key splices its node out of bucket f and into bucket f+1 in O(1); the LRU tie-break is free because each list is already recency-ordered. A minFreq pointer names the bucket to evict from — its tail is globally the least-frequent, least-recent key.
🛠️ Approach.
  1. nodes: key → Node{key, value, freq, prev, next}. freqBuckets: freq → sentinel-bounded DLList.
  2. get(key): miss → −1; hit → touch(node) (promote frequency) and return its value.
  3. put(key, value): existing key → update value and touch. New key when full → evict freqBuckets[minFreq].removeLast(), then insert fresh node into bucket 1 and set minFreq = 1.
  4. touch: remove node from bucket f; if that emptied the minFreq bucket, bump minFreq; set freq = f+1 and addFirst into bucket f+1.

⏱️ Time O(1) — get and put are constant splices and lookups 💾 Space O(capacity) — node map plus frequency buckets

⚠️ Gotchas. Handle capacity == 0: put must store nothing. minFreq only resets to 1 on a fresh insert, or increments when promotion empties the current min bucket — never scan to recompute it. Insertion order within a bucket is the LRU order, so adding at the head and evicting from the tail gives correct tie-breaking. Use sentinel head/tail nodes so splices need no null checks.
🗣️ Explain out loud. "LFU is two-dimensional: frequency first, recency as the tie-break. I keep one doubly-linked list per frequency, recency-ordered, plus a hashmap to the nodes. Get and put promote a key one bucket up in O(1), and a minFreq pointer tells me which bucket's tail to evict — no scanning. minFreq resets to 1 on insert and climbs only when its bucket empties, so eviction is always the least-frequent, least-recent key."
📄 LFUCache.java

🔁 Practice plan

Work the list in order — each problem layers one new container skill onto the last. From a blank file, re-derive: (1) Min Stack's paired-min invariant, (2) the HashMap+TreeMap "retract-then-add" pattern that powers Stock Price, (3) the corner-counting product in Detect Squares, (4) the k-way heap merge in Twitter, and (5) the freq-bucket + minFreq machinery in LFU. LFU is the centrepiece — be able to type the doubly-linked list and touch from memory.

  • ✅ I can explain why Min Stack stores a min per level and how that handles duplicate minimums.
  • ✅ I can explain why a running max fails for Stock Price and how the multiset's decrement keeps min/max honest.
  • ✅ I can derive Detect Squares' O(K) count by anchoring on a vertical side and multiplying corner counts.
  • ✅ I can implement Design Twitter's feed as a 10-pop k-way merge and state its O(F + 10 log F) cost.
  • ✅ I can write LFU's touch and eviction from scratch and prove both get and put are O(1).
  • ✅ Follow-ups to rehearse: LRU Cache (LC 146), Design Twitter with "most recent K" tuning, and Time-Based Key-Value Store (LC 981) as a binary-search-on-timestamp variant.
